Nuances: Complete Cause, Correlatives, and Essential Priority
Discusses nuances: complete vs efficient cause, priority by nature, correlativity, and essential priority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will grasp the finer distinctions within real priority: the role of the complete cause, the mutual dependence of priority and posteriority, and the concept of essential priority.

Core Idea
Priority and posteriority are correlative: each implies the other, but only within the same type.

Having defined the two primary types, the author now addresses subtle questions. Is the cause the complete cause or merely the efficient cause? How do priority and posteriority relate as correlatives? This lesson unpacks these nuances and clarifies the terminology of 'essential priority'.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Read from 'On the first (view)...' to the end. Notice the shift from definition to analysis.
2
Analyze the two views on causation
The author presents two possibilities: the cause is the complete cause (mutual concomitance) or the efficient cause (prior can exist without posterior). Understand the implications.
3
Understand priority by nature
The prior by nature does not necessitate the posterior's existence; rather, the posterior necessitates the prior's existence. Note the asymmetry.
4
Grasp the correlativity of priority and posteriority
Priority and posteriority are correlatives (muḍāyifān). They are mutually dependent in definition and exist together, but only within the same division (e.g., by nature).
This leads to the distinction between essential priority and other types.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Pause and ask: are you living as if your essence subsists independently of Him?

Logic and Technical Terms

Is Priority Predicated by Homonymy or Analogy?
Examines whether 'priority' is predicated of its divisions by homonymy or by analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate over whether the term 'priority' is applied to its divisions by homonymy or by analogy, and the argument against a univocal definition.

Core Idea
The term 'priority' is either homonymous or analogical, not univocal.

In Islamic logic and theology, terms like 'priority' can be applied in different contexts: causation, time, rank, etc. This lesson examines a key methodological question: when we say one thing is 'prior' to another in these different senses, are we using the same word with the same meaning, different meanings, or a graded meaning? The passage presents two views and critically evaluates them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then the translation. Notice the two views introduced: homonymy (ishtirak lafzi) and analogy (tashkik).
2
Understand the analogical view
The analogical view says that 'priority' has a common meaning (being needed by another) but is applied in degrees. For example, priority by causation is 'more prior' than others.
The reduction to three divisions (causation, nature, time) or two is mentioned as a basis for this view.
3
Analyze the refutation
The author refutes the idea that 'priority' could have a single univocal meaning like 'having an extra attribute'. He argues that such a definition would be too broad, and if the extra attribute is specific, it collapses into homonymy.
The example of the word 'eye' illustrates the problem with claiming a common meaning.
4
Synthesize the conclusion
After the refutation, the passage implies that homonymy or analogy remains plausible, but univocity is not viable. Reflect on why this matters for theological and philosophical arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
معقولية السبق بالاشتراك أو التشكيك قال: ومن ههنا ترددوا في أن مقولية السبق ومقابلته بالاشتراك أو بالتشكيك. قد اختلفت العبارات في أن مقولية التقدم والتأخر والمعية على الأقسام الخمسة أو الستة بحسب الاشتراك اللفظي، بأن يكون موضوعاً لكل على حدة، أو بحسب التشكيك، بأن يكون موضوعاً بمعنى مشترك بين الكل لا على السواء لكونه في التقدم بالعلية أقدم وفي التقدم بالطبع أولى، حيث يكون بالنظر إلى الذات وفي التقدم بالرتبة الحسية أشد منه في العقلية، وكان هذا مبنى على أن الكل عائد إلى التقدم بالعلة، وبالطبع، وبالزمان أو إلى الأولين فقط، واللفظ موضوع بإزاء معنى مشترك، هو كون الشيء محتاجاً إليه وإلا فليس للفظ مفهوم مشترك بين الكل، لا يقال الكل مشترك في معنى واحد ، وهو أن للمتقدم أمراً زائداً لا يوجد للمتأخر كالتأثير في الذي بالعلية، وكونه مقوماً أو شرطاً في الذي بالطبع، وكونه مضى له زمان أكثر في الذي بالزمان، وزيادة الكمال في الذي بالشرف، وقرب الوصول إليه من مبدأ معين في الذي بالرتبة، لأن نقول ليس هذا هو مفهوم لفظ التقدم، وإلا لصدق على كل شيء ينسب إلى آخر، ضرورة أنه يشتمل على أمر لا يوجد في الآخر، وإن أريد اشتماله على أمر زائد هو أحد الأمور المذكورة، فمثله يتأتى في كل مشترك لفظي، بأن يقال لفظ العين موضوع بإزاء معنى مشترك بين الكل هو مفهوم أحد المعاني.
On the intelligibility of priority by homonymy or by analogy. He said: And from here they hesitated concerning whether the predication of priority and its opposite is by homonymy or by analogy. The statements have differed concerning whether the predication of priority, posteriority, and togetherness with respect to the five or six divisions is according to homonymy, in that the term is placed for each separately, or according to analogy, in that the term is placed for a meaning common to all but not equally (i.e., in a graded manner) because in priority by causation it is more prior and in priority by nature it is more rightful, where it is with respect to essence, and in priority by sense rank it is stronger than in intellectual rank. And this was based on the idea that all (divisions) reduce to priority by cause, by nature, and by time, or to the first two only, and the term is placed for a common meaning, which is that a thing is needed by another; otherwise there is no common concept for the term among all (divisions). One cannot say that all share in one meaning, which is that the prior has an extra attribute not found in the posterior, like effect in that by causation, being a constituent or condition in that by nature, having passed more time in that by time, increase of perfection in that by honor, and closeness of reaching it from a specific starting point in that by rank; because we say this is not the concept of the term 'priority', otherwise it would be true of everything attributed to another, since it necessarily includes something not found in the other. And if what is intended is that it includes an extra attribute that is one of the mentioned matters, then its like occurs in every homonymous term, as when it is said that the term 'eye' is placed for a common meaning among all (meanings) which is the concept of one of the meanings.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage presents a debate among scholars (of kalam) regarding the predication of the terms 'priority' (al-sabq), 'posteriority', and 'togetherness' across their various divisions (causation, nature, time, rank, honor, and possibly order). The central question is whether these terms are applied homonymously (bi al-ishtirāk al-lafẓī), meaning the word is used for each division with a completely different sense, or analogically (bi al-tashkīk), meaning the word has a single core meaning that applies to all divisions but in varying degrees of strength or priority. The author first notes the disagreement. He then explains the analogy view: the term is placed for a common meaning (that the prior is needed by the posterior) but is not applied equally, as priority by causation is 'more prior' (aqdam) than others, and priority by nature is more appropriate (awlā) in terms of essence. This view is based on the idea that all divisions ultimately reduce to causation, nature, and time (or perhaps only the first two). The author then refutes the objection that one might propose a single common meaning such as 'the prior has an extra attribute not found in the posterior' (e.g., causation, being a constituent, etc.). He argues that this is not the concept of priority, because if it were, then any two things where one has an attribute the other lacks would count as priority, which is too broad. Moreover, if one insists that the extra attribute is one of the specific attributes mentioned (like causation), then this is just like any homonymous term (e.g., 'eye' has multiple meanings, but one could claim a common meaning like 'an organ' which is true of both the eye and the eye of a needle? Actually the example given is that 'eye' is placed for a common meaning which is the concept of one of its meanings—this is a problematic claim, indicating the weakness of that objection). Thus, the passage defends the homonymy view, showing that attempting to posit a single common meaning fails.

Key Terms
الاشتراك اللفظي
التشكيك
مقولية السبق
التقدم بالعلية
التقدم بالطبع
التقدم بالزمان
التقدم بالشرف
التقدم بالرتبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Priority has a single univocal meaning, such as 'having an extra attribute not found in the posterior', that applies equally to all its divisions.
Correction: The passage shows that this definition is too broad (applying to any two things with differing attributes) and if the extra attribute is specified, it becomes like homonymy, thus failing as a univocal meaning.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why cannot the concept of 'priority' be defined simply as 'the prior having an extra attribute not in the posterior'?
A) Because that definition would apply to any two things, not just genuine priority.
B) Because the prior does not always have an extra attribute.
C) Because the posterior might also have an extra attribute.
D) Because the definition is too narrow.

Key Takeaway
The passage demonstrates that attempts to find a single univocal meaning for 'priority' across its divisions fail, leaving homonymy or analogy as the plausible alternatives.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is knower' and 'I am knower', do you assume the word 'knowledge' means the same? How does this lesson's critique of univocity challenge your default assumption? Will you now pause every time you describe Allah with a word also used for humans?
?
The passage shows that even in philosophy, words like 'priority' cannot be taken univocally across categories. What does that imply for your understanding of terms like 'will', 'power', and 'life' when applied to Allah? Are you comfortable with the possibility that you do not fully grasp their meanings?
?
If 'priority by causation' and 'priority by rank' are only homonymously or analogically related, then your own ranking of priorities in life (e.g., worship vs. work) may be a different kind of 'priority' than the divine order. How does that realization call you to examine the priorities you set, knowing they are not the same as Allah's?

The Classification of Priority as Equivocation or Analogy
The author classifies the term 'priority' as either equivocal or analogous.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why classical scholars debated whether 'priority' is used equivocally or analogically, and why the correct view is that it is equivocal.

Core Idea
Priority is a word with distinct meanings, not a single concept applied differently.

This passage addresses a subtle logical question: Is the term 'priority' (sabq) used in the same way across its various types (causal, natural, temporal, honor, rank) or does it have multiple distinct meanings? The author presents the two competing views, an objection, and a rebuttal, ultimately favoring the view of equivocation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Focus on the overall argument.
2
Identify the two views
Find where the author states the two possibilities: equivocation (ishtirak lafzi) and analogy (tashkik). Note the difference.
Equivocation means the word has separate meanings; analogy means a shared meaning with degrees.
3
Examine the objection and rebuttal
Notice the objection that all types share an additional attribute. The author shows why this would be too broad and actually reduces to equivocation.
4
Understand the conclusion
The passage concludes that priority is equivocal. Confirm this by rereading the rebuttal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
معقولية السبق بالاشتراك أو التشكيك (قال): ومن ههنا ترددوا (۱) في أن مقولية السبق ومقابلته بالاشتراك أو بالتشكيك (۲). قد اختلفت العبارات في أن مقولية التقدم والتأخر والمعية على الأقسام الخمسة أو الستة بحسب الاشتراك اللفظي، بأن يكون موضوعاً لكل على حدة، أو بحسب التشكيك، بأن يكون موضوعاً بمعنى مشترك بين الكل لا على السواء لكونه في التقدم بالعلية أقدم وفي التقدم بالطبع أولى، حيث يكون بالنظر إلى الذات وفي التقدم بالرتبة الحسية أشد منه في العقلية، وكان هذا مبنى على أن الكل عائد إلى التقدم بالعلة، وبالطبع، وبالزمان أو إلى الأولين فقط، واللفظ موضوع بإزاء معنى مشترك، هو كون الشيء محتاجاً إليه وإلا فليس للفظ مفهوم مشترك بين الكل، لا يقال الكل مشترك في معنى واحد، وهو أن للمتقدم أمراً زائداً لا يوجد للمتأخر كالتأثير في الذي بالعلية، وكونه مقوماً أو شرطاً في الذي بالطبع، وكونه مضى له زمان أكثر في الذي بالزمان، وزيادة الكمال في الذي بالشرف، وقرب الوصول إليه من مبدأ معين في الذي بالرتبة، لأن نقول ليس هذا هو مفهوم لفظ التقدم، وإلا لصدق على كل شيء ينسب إلى آخر، ضرورة أنه يشتمل على أمر لا يوجد في الآخر، وإن أريد اشتماله على أمر زائد هو أحد الأمور المذكورة، فمثله يتأتى في كل مشترك لفظي، بأن يقال لفظ العين موضوع بإزاء معنى مشترك بين الكل هو مفهوم أحد المعاني.
The intelligibility of priority (al-sabq) by equivocation or by analogy. (He said): Hence they differed (1) about whether the predication of priority and its opposite is by equivocation or by analogy (2). The expressions have differed about whether the predication of precedence (al-taqaddum), posteriority (al-ta'akhkhur), and simultaneity (al-ma'iyya) are according to five or six divisions in terms of verbal equivocation (al-ishtirak al-lafzi), such that the word is coined for each separately, or in terms of analogy (al-tashkik), such that the word is coined for a meaning shared among all but not equally, because in priority by causality it is more prior, and in priority by nature it is more worthy, where it is relative to essence, and in priority by sensory rank it is stronger than in intellectual rank. And this was based on the fact that all [types] reduce to priority by cause, by nature, and by time, or to the first two only, and the word is coined for a shared meaning, which is that something is needed for it; otherwise the word has no common concept among all. It cannot be said that all share in one meaning, which is that the prior has an additional thing not found in the posterior, such as influence in that which is by causality, being constitutive or conditional in that which is by nature, passing more time in that which is by time, increase of perfection in that which is by honor, and closeness of attainment from a specific starting point in that which is by rank; because we say: this is not the concept of the word 'precedence', otherwise it would be true of everything attributed to another, necessarily because it includes something not found in the other. And if it is meant that it includes an additional thing which is one of the aforementioned things, then the like of that occurs in every verbal equivocation, such that it is said: the word 'eye' is coined for a common meaning among all, which is the concept of one of the meanings.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of a technical issue in the classification of terms. The author begins by stating the problem: whether the term “priority” (سبق) and its opposite are predicated on their various kinds (by cause, nature, time, honor, rank) by way of verbal equivocation (اشتراك لفظي) — i.e., the word is coined separately for each meaning — or by way of analogy (تشكيك) — i.e., the word is coined for a meaning common to all but not equally. He notes that there are either five or six divisions of precedence, posteriority, and simultaneity. The basis for the analogical view is that all types reduce to priority by cause, nature, and time (or just the first two), and the word is coined for a common meaning: that something is needed for the prior. If not, there is no common concept. Then he considers an objection: that all types share one meaning, namely that the prior has an additional attribute not found in the posterior, such as influence (causality), being constitutive or conditional (nature), longer time elapsed (time), greater perfection (honor), or closer attainment from a starting point (rank). The author refutes this by saying that if this were the concept of “priority,” it would apply to any two things, since for any two relative things one always has something not in the other. Rather, if one insists on a particular additional attribute, that just reduces to equivocation, like the word “eye” which has different meanings. Thus the correct view is that the term is equivocal.

اشتراك لفظي (ishtirak lafzi)
تشكيك (tashkik)
تقدم (taqaddum)
تأخر (ta'akhkhur)
معية (ma'iyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All types of priority share a single common meaning, namely that the prior has an additional attribute not found in the posterior.
Correction: The author shows that this would apply to any two things and if specified further it actually supports equivocation, not a unified concept.

Checkpoint

A) Because that meaning would apply to any two things, not just priority.
B) Because the additional attributes are not truly additional.
C) Because the word 'priority' is only used for temporal priority.
D) Because the author accepts the view of equivocation only for causality.

Key Takeaway
Priority (sabq) is an equivocal term with multiple distinct meanings, not a single analogous concept.

Four Discussions on Unity and Multiplicity
Lists four discussions on unity and multiplicity as rational considerations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four key discussions that the author presents regarding the concepts of unity and multiplicity as rational considerations.

Core Idea
Unity and multiplicity are rational categories with distinct logical properties.

The author now introduces four specific discussion points under the fourth method concerning unity and multiplicity. These points clarify that unity and multiplicity are mental considerations, that their subject matters vary, that duality cannot share a single subject, and that otherness is inherent to multiplicity. This passage sets the stage for deeper theological analysis of how we speak about oneness and plurality.

Guided Path
1
Read the enumeration
Read the Arabic passage once to see the four discussion points listed. Then read the translation to understand their basic meaning.
Each point starts with an ordinal number (first, second, etc.).
2
Identify the first point
The first discussion states that unity and multiplicity are rational considerations. Reflect on what it means for something to be a rational consideration rather than a physical property.
This establishes the conceptual nature of these categories.
3
Understand the second and third points
The second point says the subject of unity and multiplicity differs. The third says duality is not unified in its subject. Compare these two: the subject of unity/multiplicity can vary, but duality (two-ness) cannot be one subject.
This shows logical constraints on how unity and multiplicity apply.
4
Consider the fourth point
The fourth discussion says otherness (difference) is a property of multiplicity. Think about why multiplicity necessarily involves distinction between parts.
This connects multiplicity to differentiation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفيه مباحث : في المنهج الرابع الوحدة والكثرة الأول : الوحدة والكثرة من الاعتبارات العقلية الثاني : معروض الوحدة والكثرة يختلف وما يتعلق به الثالث : الاثنينية لا تتحد في معروضها الرابع : التغاير الذي هو من خواص الكثرة
And in it are discussions: On the fourth method: unity and multiplicity. First: Unity and multiplicity are among rational considerations. Second: The subject of unity and multiplicity differs and what pertains to it. Third: Duality is not unified in its subject. Fourth: Otherness [or difference] which is among the properties of multiplicity.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of four discussion points (mabāḥith) under the fourth method (al-manhaj al-rābiʿ) concerning unity (al-waḥda) and multiplicity (al-kathra). The author introduces it by saying 'And in it are discussions' referring to a broader section. The first discussion states that unity and multiplicity are rational considerations (iʿtibārāt ʿaqliyya), meaning they are conceptual categories applied by the mind. The second discussion says that the subject (maʿrūḍ) of unity and multiplicity differs and what pertains to it, indicating that the thing being described as one or many can vary. The third discussion states that duality (al-ithnayniyya) is not unified in its subject, meaning two things cannot be one in the same subject. The fourth discussion states that otherness or difference (al-taghāyur) is among the properties (khawāṣṣ) of multiplicity, so multiplicity inherently involves distinction between parts.

Unity and Multiplicity as Rational Considerations
Sanusi defines unity and multiplicity as rational considerations and critiques their definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that unity (al-waḥdah) and multiplicity (al-kathrah) are rational considerations (iʿtibārāt ʿaqliyyah) with no external existence, and that their definitions are verbal and subject to critique.

Core Idea
Unity and multiplicity are in the mind, not in the world.

This passage from al-Sanusi's Sharh al-Maqasid introduces a foundational distinction: unity and multiplicity are not real attributes of objects but rational considerations. The author explains why these concepts are self-evident yet defined verbally, and why each is used to define the other.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get the author's own words before interpretation.
Notice the concise style.
2
Understand the nature of these concepts
Note that unity and multiplicity are rational considerations with no external existence, are self-evident, and are defined only verbally.
Compare to necessity and contingency mentioned earlier.
3
Examine the definitions and their critique
Look at the two verbal definitions given and the author's point that they fail with composites of disparate things.
Also note the alternative definition based on epistemological priority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الأول في الوحدة والكثرة من الاعتبارات العقلية. قال: المنهج الرابع في الوحدة والكثرة، وفيه مباحث: المبحث الأول أنهما من الاعتبارات العقلية الغنية عن التعريف، إلا أن الوحدة أعرف عند العقل والكثرة عند الخيال، ولذا يقع كل في تفسير الآخر. والحق أنهما من الاعتبارات العقلية التي لا وجود لها في الأعيان بمثل ما سبق في الوجوب والإمكان، وإن تصورهما بديهي لحصوله لمن لم يمارس طريق الاكتساب، فلا يعرفان إلا لفظاً كما يقال: الوحدة عدم الانقسام، وقد يقال الوحدة عدم الانقسام إلى أمور متشابهة، والكثرة هو الانقسام إليها. ولا خفاء في انتقاضهما طرداً وعكساً بالمجتمع من الأمور المتخالفة. وأما ما يقال أن الوحدة عدم الكثرة والكثرة هي المجتمع من الوحدات فمبناه على أن الوحدة أعرف عند العقل والكثرة عند الخيال.
The first investigation: on unity and multiplicity being rational considerations. He said: The fourth method on unity and multiplicity, and it contains investigations. The first investigation: that they are rational considerations independent of definition, except that unity is better known to the intellect and multiplicity to the imagination, and therefore each is used in the definition of the other. The truth is that they are rational considerations that have no existence in external entities, similar to what preceded concerning necessity and contingency, and their conception is self-evident, being attained by one who has not practiced the way of acquisition; thus they are not known except verbally, as it is said: unity is the absence of divisibility, and it has been said: unity is the absence of divisibility into similar things, and multiplicity is the divisibility into them. There is no concealment of their failure both in extension and intension with what is composed of disparate things. As for what is said that unity is the absence of multiplicity and multiplicity is the aggregate of unities, it is based on that unity is better known to the intellect and multiplicity to the imagination.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage defines and explains the nature of unity (al-waḥdah) and multiplicity (al-kathrah) as rational considerations (iʿtibārāt ʿaqliyyah). The author begins by stating that they are rational concepts that are independent of definition, being intuitively understood. However, unity is clearer to the intellect, while multiplicity is clearer to the imagination, which is why each is used in defining the other (e.g., unity as absence of multiplicity, multiplicity as aggregate of unities). The author then affirms that they have no external existence in concrete objects, analogous to the concepts of necessity (wujūb) and contingency (imkān) discussed earlier. Their conception is self-evident (badīhī), even for someone untrained in reasoning; they are only definable in words. The author gives two common verbal definitions: unity as absence of divisibility (nafy al-inqisām), or more precisely absence of divisibility into similar parts; multiplicity as divisibility into those parts. He notes that these definitions fail both in extension and intension when applied to a composite of disparate elements (e.g., a body composed of different substances). Finally, he addresses an alternative definition—unity as absence of multiplicity, multiplicity as aggregate of unities—and explains that this is based on the relative familiarity of unity to the intellect and multiplicity to the imagination. The passage thus clarifies the epistemological and ontological status of these fundamental concepts.

الكثرة
الاعتبارات العقلية
بديهي
الانقسام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Believing that unity and multiplicity are real attributes existing in external objects.
Correction: The author explicitly states they have no existence in external entities (لا وجود لها في الأعيان).

Checkpoint

C) Because the two concepts are identical in meaning.
D) Because these definitions are derived from sensory experience.

Key Takeaway
Unity and multiplicity are self-evident rational concepts with no external reality, defined verbally and critiqued for their limitations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Logic and Technical Terms

5 min
Unity is not synonymous with existence or quiddity
Taftazani proves that unity is distinct from existence and quiddity with two arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why unity and multiplicity are distinct from existence and quiddity, based on two rational proofs from Taftazani.

Core Idea
Unity and multiplicity are not existence or quiddity—they can be separated in conception and in physical examples.

Because unity always accompanies existence, some scholars mistakenly thought they are identical. Taftazani corrects this by showing that we can conceive existence without unity, and that changing unity/multiplicity does not change existence or quiddity—as demonstrated by combining or dividing water.

Guided Path
1
Read the thesis and its context
Taftazani states that unity is not synonymous with existence or quiddity, and that some falsely imagine they are the same. He then promises two proofs.
Notice the key terms: الوحدة, الوجود, الماهية.
2
Analyze the first proof: conceptual separation
We can conceive a thing's existence and quiddity without conceiving its unity or multiplicity—for example, we know the Creator exists before proving He is one.
This shows that unity is not essential to existence.
3
Analyze the second proof: the water experiment
When we combine or separate water, unity/multiplicity changes while the water's existence and quiddity remain unchanged, proving they are distinct.
The example is empirical and clear.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوحدة غير مرادفة للوجود ولا الماهية

قال : والقطع بجواز الانفكاك في التعقل وبأن الجمع والتفريق ليس بإعدام يدل على مغايرتهما للماهية والوجود (۱) وإن كانت الوحدة تساوقه (۲)) . لما كانت الوحدة مساوقة للوجود بمعنى أن كل ما له وحدة فله وجود ما (۳)، وكل ما له وجود فله فله وحدة بوجه ما توهم بعضهم أن الوحدة هي الوجود وهو باطل، لأن الكثير من حيث هو كثير موجود وليس بواحد، فحاول التنبية على أن كلا من الوحدة والكثرة مغاير لكل من الوجود والماهية وذلك بوجهين .

أحدهما : لنا (٤) أن نتعقل ماهية الشيء ووجوده من غير أن نتعقل وحدته أو كثرته بل مع التردد فيه كما نقطع بوجود الصانع، ثم نثبت بالبرهان وحدته ونقطع بوجود الفلك وماهيته، ثم نثبت كثرته .

وثانيهما : أنا إذا جمعنا مياه أوان كثيرة في إناء واحد، حتى صار ماء واحداً، أو فرقنا ماء إناء واحد في أوان كثيرة، حتى صارت مياها كثيرة، فقد زالت الوحدة والكثرة مع أن الوجود والماهية بحالهما (٥) من غير زوال وتبدل .

قطعا ، فلو كانت الوحدة أو الكثرة نفس الوجود أو الماهية لما كان كذلك .
Unity is not synonymous with existence nor quiddity. He said: And the certainty of the permissibility of separation in conception and that combination and separation are not annihilation indicates their difference from quiddity and existence (1), even though unity is concomitant with it (2). Since unity is concomitant with existence in the sense that everything that has unity has some existence (3), and everything that has existence has unity in some respect, some imagined that unity is existence, which is false, because the many qua many is existent and is not one. So he attempted to point out that each of unity and multiplicity is different from each of existence and quiddity, and that by two aspects. The first: we (4) can conceive the quiddity of a thing and its existence without conceiving its unity or multiplicity, but rather with hesitation about it, just as we are certain of the existence of the Maker, then we prove by proof His unity, and we are certain of the existence of the celestial sphere and its quiddity, then we prove its multiplicity. The second: if we collect the waters of many vessels into one vessel until it becomes one water, or we divide the water of one vessel into many vessels until it becomes many waters, then unity and multiplicity have ceased while existence and quiddity remain in their state (5) without cessation or change. Certainly, if unity or multiplicity were existence or quiddity itself, it would not be so.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof. Taftazani begins by stating the thesis: unity is not synonymous with existence or quiddity (al-mahiyya). He then offers a supporting statement: we are certain that it is possible to separate in conception (al-ta'aqqul) between a thing's existence/quiddity and its unity/multiplicity; indeed, we actually do so. For example, we know that the Creator (al-Sani') exists without initially knowing whether He is one or multiple—we prove His unity later by demonstration. Likewise, we know the existence and quiddity of the celestial sphere (al-falak) and then prove its multiplicity (kathra). This shows that unity and multiplicity are not identical to existence or quiddity, because we can conceive the latter without the former. The second proof is empirical: if we combine water from many vessels into one, unity and multiplicity change (from many to one, or vice versa), but the existence and quiddity of the water remain unchanged. Therefore, unity/multiplicity are not the same as existence/quiddity.

Key Terms
الوحدة (al-wahda)
الكثرة (al-kathra)
الوجود (al-wujud)
الماهية (al-mahiyya)
التعقل (al-ta'aqqul)
الانفكاك (al-infikak)
المساوقة (al-musawaqa)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some imagine that unity is identical to existence because they always accompany each other.
Correction: The passage proves they are distinct by showing that unity can be absent from existence (the many is existent but not one) and that unity/multiplicity can change while existence remains constant.

Checkpoint
What is the first proof that unity is not identical to existence?
A) We can conceive a thing's existence without conceiving its unity.
B) Unity is a part of existence.
C) Existence is always one.
D) Unity is the absence of multiplicity.

Key Takeaway
Unity and multiplicity are not identical to existence or quiddity, as demonstrated by conceptual separation and the water example.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You can conceive of God's existence without conceiving His unity, as the passage says. Have you ever truly separated these in your mind? When you say 'Allah exists,' do you automatically assume His unity, or have you personally proven it? What would it mean for your faith if you only believed in existence but not unity?
?
The water example shows that unity can change while essence stays the same. Consider your own identity – if you lost your sense of personal unity (e.g., through confusion or dissociation), would your existence cease? How does this lesson help you understand that your unity is not your essence?
?
Are you guilty of confusing God's existence with His unity in your worship? Do you sometimes act as if acknowledging God's existence is enough, without actively affirming and proving His oneness? What step will you take today to ensure your belief in tawhid is not just emotional but rationally grounded?

Proof That Unity and Multiplicity Are Positive
Taftazani presents a proof for the positivity of unity and multiplicity and notes the counterargument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument for why unity and multiplicity must be positive (wujudi) rather than negative ('adami), and the weakness of the opposing view.

Core Idea
Unity and multiplicity cannot be merely absences; the proof shows they are real positive attributes.

In this passage, Taftazani presents a reductio ad absurdum argument to show that both unity and multiplicity are positive existential concepts. He then mentions the counterargument that they are merely negative (absence of division or composition), but dismisses it as weak. This lesson walks through the logical steps and clarifies the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the compressed style of kalam arguments.
2
Understand the starting assumption
The proof begins by assuming unity is negative. Follow the logical consequences of this assumption.
This is a reductio ad absurdum: if assumption leads to contradiction, assumption is false.
3
Explore the two branches
The proof splits into two cases: if multiplicity is also negative, or if multiplicity is positive. See how both lead to contradictions.
Each branch forces the conclusion that unity is positive.
4
Examine the counterargument
The opposing view says unity is just absence of division and multiplicity is composition of units. Consider why Taftazani calls both weak.
5
Reflect on the overall lesson
Take a moment to see how the proof establishes that unity and multiplicity are positive, real attributes, not mere negations.
This is foundational for later discussions on divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدليل على وجودية الوحدة والكثرة

قال : وقد يستدل على وجوديتهما (۱) ، بأن الوحدة لو كانت عدمية (۲) لكانت عدم الكثرة (۳) ، وهي إما عدمية فتكون الوحدة وجودية لكونها عدم العدم(4) هذا خلف (٥) . إما وجودية فيلزم كون الجميع من العـدمـات وجودية وهو محال . وكون الوحدة وجودية لكونها جزاها هذا خلف . إذ اليست الكثرة إلا التألف من الوحدات (٦) ، يلزم كونها أيضاً وجودية، وعلى حدميتهما بأنه لا يعقل من الوحدة إلا عدم الانقسام (۷)، ومن الكثرة إلا التألف من الوحدات وكلاهما (ضعيف).
The proof for the positivity of unity and multiplicity. He said: And it may be argued for their positivity (1): that if unity were negative (2), it would be the absence of multiplicity (3). And multiplicity: either it is negative, in which case unity would be positive because it is the absence of non-existence (4); this is a contradiction (5). Or it is positive, then it would necessitate that all of the non-existences are positive, which is impossible. And that unity is positive because it is a part of it (i.e., multiplicity) is a contradiction. Since multiplicity is nothing but the composition of units (6), it necessarily follows that it too is positive. And on their negativity, [it is argued] that nothing is conceived from unity except the absence of division (7), and from multiplicity except the composition of units; and both (are weak).
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage is a proof and a counterargument. Taftazani presents an argument for the positivity (wujudiyya) of unity and multiplicity. The proof assumes unity is negative (i.e., non-existential). If so, it would be the absence of multiplicity. Then multiplicity itself is considered: if it is also negative, then unity is the absence of non-existence, which is positive—a contradiction to the initial assumption. If multiplicity is positive, then since unity is a part of multiplicity, it too must be positive. This is also contradictory to the assumption. Therefore, both unity and multiplicity must be positive. He then mentions the opposing view: on the negativity side, it is said that unity is merely the absence of division, and multiplicity is the composition of units, both of which are negative concepts. Taftazani remarks that both positions are weak.

Key Terms
وجودي (wujudi)
عدمي ('adami)
العدم (al-'adam)
الانقسام (al-inqisam)
التألف (al-ta'alluf)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that unity is simply the absence of division (a negative concept) and that the proof is circular.
Correction: The proof shows that assuming unity is negative leads to logical contradictions; thus, unity must be positive. It is not circular but a reductio ad absurdum.

Checkpoint
According to the proof, what happens if unity is assumed to be negative and multiplicity is also negative?
A) Unity becomes positive as the absence of non-existence.
B) Both remain negative.
C) Multiplicity becomes positive.
D) The assumption is confirmed.

Key Takeaway
Unity and multiplicity are positive attributes because assuming otherwise leads to logical contradictions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In this lesson, you saw that reducing unity to a mere absence leads to contradictions. When you affirm 'La ilaha illa Allah', do you tend to think of it as merely negating other gods, or do you actively affirm the positive, unique existence of Allah? What would change if you consciously shifted to a positive affirmation?
?
Taftazani’s proof relies on logical reasoning to defend a theological concept. Are you willing to engage your intellect to deepen your faith, or do you sometimes dismiss rational arguments as unnecessary? Commit to studying one logical argument in ‘aqeedah this week.
?
The proof shows that components of a positive whole must themselves be positive. Consider your own actions: are the 'units' of your daily deeds aligned with a positive intention towards Allah, or are they merely the absence of sin? How can you make each action a positive affirmation of your faith?

The Disagreement on Unity and Multiplicity
Taftazani records the disagreement and the philosophers' arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theological dispute over whether unity and multiplicity are positive or negative, including the philosophers' arguments and Taftazani's assessment.

Core Idea
Unity and multiplicity: are they real or just mental constructs?

Taftazani presents a debate between philosophers and theologians on the ontological status of unity and multiplicity. Before assessing the arguments, we need to clarify the terms 'positive' and 'negative', as they are used differently by each side. This passage records the philosophers' case and Taftazani's immediate judgment.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
This is a 'naql khilaf' (record of disagreement) between two schools: philosophers and theologians.
Taftazani is not taking sides yet; he is documenting the debate.
2
Clarify the terms
Notice that 'positive' (wujudi) and 'negative' ('adami) have two possible meanings: one set takes them as existent/non-existent, the other as concepts that include or exclude non-existence.
This ambiguity is part of the debate.
3
Examine the philosophers' arguments
Read the three arguments: unity as a part of the existent one, unity as opposite of negative non-unity, and the logical consequence if unity were not existent.
Taftazani says all these are 'evidently false' – keep that in mind.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثاني : نقل خلاف بين الفلاسفة والمتكلمين في أن الوحدة والكثرة وجوديتان أو عدمیتان وتمسكات من الطرفين يشعر بعضها، بأن المراد بالوجودي الموجود، وبالعدمي المعدوم، وبعضها بأن المراد بالعدمي ما يدخل في مفهومه العدم وبالوجودي ما لا يدخل فمن تمسكات الفلاسفة، أن الوحدة جزء هذا الواحد الموجود وأنها نقيض اللا وحدة العدمية لصدقها على الممتنع ، وأنها لو لم تكن موجودة لما كان شيء ما واحداً لعدم الفرق بين قولنا وحدته لا وقولنا لا وحدة ،له والكل ظاهر الفساد، ومنها ما أورده الإمام
Second investigation: transmission of disagreement between the philosophers and the theologians concerning whether unity and multiplicity are positive or negative; and points of evidence from both sides, some of which suggest that by 'positive' is meant existent, and by 'negative' is meant non-existent; and some of which suggest that what is meant by 'negative' is that into whose concept non-existence enters, and by 'positive' that into which it does not enter. Among the points of evidence of the philosophers: that unity is a part of this existent one; and that it is the opposite of non-unity which is negative, because it applies to the impossible; and that if it were not existent, nothing would be one, because there would be no difference between our saying 'its unity is not' and our saying 'there is no unity for it'; and all of these are evidently false; and among them is what the Imam (al-Razi) mentioned.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage is a record of a disagreement (naql khilaf). Taftazani introduces a second investigation: the dispute between philosophers (falasifa) and theologians (mutakallimun) about whether unity and multiplicity are positive (wujudiyya) or negative ('adamiyya). He notes that different meanings of 'positive' and 'negative' are used: some take 'positive' to mean existent and 'negative' to mean non-existent; others take 'negative' as a concept that includes non-existence, and 'positive' as a concept that does not. He then lists arguments from the philosophers: (1) unity is a part of the existent one; (2) unity is the opposite of non-unity, which is negative, so unity must be positive; (3) if unity were not existent, no thing would be one, because there would be no difference between saying 'its unity does not exist' and 'there is no unity for it'. Taftazani remarks that all these are clearly false (zahir al-fasad), and mentions that Imam al-Razi has other arguments, but the passage cuts off.

Key Terms
الفلاسفة (al-falasifa)
المتكلمون (al-mutakallimun)
وجودي (wujudi)
عدمي ('adami)
نقيض (naqid)
الممتنع (al-mumtani')

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Taftazani supports the philosophers' view on unity.
Correction: Taftazani explicitly states the philosophers' arguments are 'apparently false' (zahir al-fasad).

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers' arguments, what would be the consequence if unity were not existent?
A) Nothing would be one.
B) Multiplicity would be positive.
C) Existence would be impossible.
D) The theologians would be correct.

Key Takeaway
This passage provides a snapshot of a key debate in Islamic theology: whether unity and multiplicity are real positive attributes or merely negative concepts, with the philosophers' arguments dismissed by Taftazani as clearly false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'La ilaha illa Allah', do you feel the oneness of Allah as a positive reality in your heart, or merely as a negation of other gods? How does this lesson's distinction challenge your daily awareness of tawhid?
?
If the philosophers were right that unity is just a mental construct, what would that do to your certainty that Allah is truly One? How does Taftazani's dismissal of their arguments strengthen your conviction?
?
Is there any area of your life where you treat unity (of purpose, of community, of worship) as a mere concept rather than a living reality? What would change if you took it as a positive, existent truth?

When Unity is Not Multiplicity: Absolute Unity and Simple Units
Classifies the first branch of unity: absolute unity, point, separate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first branch of unity classification: when the subject of unity is not also subject of multiplicity, and its subcases of absolute unity, point, and separate substance.

Core Idea
Unity can be absolute or limited; simple units are points or separate substances.

The passage begins by distinguishing situations where the subject of unity is exclusive — it cannot be described by multiplicity. This leads to a cascade of possibilities: either the thing is so simple that its only concept is indivisibility (absolute unity), or it has a concept and is indivisible, leading to two distinct types: a point (with position) or a separate substance (without position). This lesson walks through these foundational categories.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without focusing on details. Then read the translation to get the overall meaning.
Notice the logical structure: condition, then subcases.
2
Identify the two main conditions
Find where the text says 'if it has no concept other than absence of division' vs 'if it does have a concept'. These lead to different outcomes.
3
Distinguish the three types: absolute, point, separate
Compare absolute unity (no additional concept), point (has position and no division), and separate (no position and no division). Understand the criteria.
Absolute unity is pure indivisibility; point and separate have additional conceptual content.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثاني
في معرض الوحدة والكثرة
(قال : معروض (۱) الوحدة قد لا يكون معروض الكثرة (٢)، وحينئذ إن لم يكن له مفهوم سوى عدم الانقسام فوحدة على الإطلاق (۳) ، وإن كان فإن لم يقبل الانقسام، فإما ذو وضع (٤) فنقطة ، أو لا فمفارق، وإن قبله فإما بالذات (٥) فكم أو بالعرض ( ٦ ) إلى أجزاء متشابهة (٧) ، أو متحالفة [ فواحدة باتصال أجزائه (۸) ، فواحد بالاجتماع طبيعة أو صناعة أو وضعاً كالشجر والبيت، والدرهم. وقد يكون معروض الكثرة ولا بد من اختلاف الجهة فإن قومت جهة الوحدة جهة الكثرة فوحدة جنسية، أو نوعية أو فصلية وإلا فإن عرضت لها، فوحدة بالعرض، وإلا فبالنسبة .
والوحدة في الجنس تسمى مجانسة، وفي النوع مماثلة، وفي الكم مساواة وفي الكيف مشابهة وفي النسبة مناسبة وفي الخاصة مشاكلة وفي الأطراف مطابقة ، وفي وضع الأجزاء موازاة .
(۱) معروض الوحدة : موصوفها .
،

(٤) أي مكان يشار إليه .
(٥) كالمقدار من طول وعرض وعمق فإن قبوله هذا الأبعاد للانقسام لذواتها وقبل الأجسام لها
بعروضها له عند الفلاسفة .
(٦) كالجسم فإن قبوله للانقسام عند الفلاسفة بعروض المقدار له.
(۷) في الحقيقة والصورة.

Second investigation on the subject of unity and multiplicity. (He said: The subject (1) of unity may not be the subject of multiplicity (2), and in that case if it has no concept other than absence of division, then it is unity absolutely (3); and if it does [have a concept], then if it does not admit division, then either it has position (4) so it is a point, or not so it is separate; and if it admits [division], then either essentially (5) so quantity, or accidentally (6) into similar parts (7), or dissimilar [so one by continuity of its parts (8), so one by aggregation—by nature, craft, or position, like trees, a house, and a dirham. And [the subject] may be subject to multiplicity, and there must be difference of aspect: if the aspect of unity determines the aspect of multiplicity, then it is generic unity, or specific unity, or differential unity; and if not, then if [unity] occurs to it, then it is accidental unity, and if not, then relative unity. And unity in genus is called homogeneity, in species similarity, in quantity equality, in quality resemblance, in relation proportionality, in property analogy, in extremities correspondence, and in arrangement of parts parallelism. (1) Subject of unity: its descriptor. (2) That is, it is particular, not universal that can be predicated of many. (3) That is, real unity not dependent on a condition. (4) That is, a place that can be pointed to. (5) Like magnitude of length, width, and depth, for their acceptance of these dimensions for division is essential, and bodies accept it by their occurrence to it according to the philosophers. (6) Like body, for its acceptance of division according to the philosophers is by the occurrence of magnitude to it. (7) In reality and form. (8) What is between the brackets is missing from (A) and (B).
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage defines the classification of unity in terms of its subject and introduces key terminology. It begins by stating that the subject of unity may or may not also be a subject of multiplicity. If it is not, and it has no concept other than absence of division, it is absolute unity. If it has a concept but does not admit division: if it has position, it is a point; if not, it is something separate (a simple substance). If it admits division: either essentially (quantity) or accidentally (body) into similar or dissimilar parts—similar parts yield unity by continuity, dissimilar parts yield unity by aggregation (e.g., a tree, house, dirham). Then it discusses cases where the subject does admit multiplicity, requiring a difference of aspect: if the aspect of unity determines the aspect of multiplicity, it is generic, specific, or differential unity; if unity merely occurs to it, it is accidental unity; otherwise relative unity. Finally, it lists the technical terms for unity across categories: homogeneity (genus), similarity (species), equality (quantity), resemblance (quality), proportionality (relation), analogy (property), correspondence (extremities), parallelism (arrangement). The footnotes clarify the terms.

Key Terms
معروض الوحدة
وحدة على الإطلاق
نقطة
مفارق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many think that 'absolute unity' means any indivisible thing.
Correction: Absolute unity specifically refers to a subject that has no concept other than absence of division; other indivisible things like points have additional concepts (like position).

Checkpoint
What type of unity does a point have according to this passage?
A) Absolute unity
B) Separate substance
C) It is a point and falls under the second subcase
D) Unity by continuity

Key Takeaway
When the subject of unity is exclusive to unity, it leads either to absolute unity (no additional concept) or to point (with position) / separate (without position).

When Unity Admits Division: Quantity, Body, Continuity, and Aggregation
Classifies unity when subject admits division: quantity, body, continuity, aggregation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second branch of unity classification: when the subject of unity admits division, and its subcases of quantity (essential division), body (accidental division), and what results: unity by continuity (similar parts) or unity by aggregation (dissimilar parts).

Core Idea
Division can be essential (quantity) or accidental (body), leading to continuity or aggregation.

The passage continues by considering subjects that do admit division. This splits into two main types based on whether the division is essential to the thing itself (quantity) or accidental (body). Furthermore, the parts can be similar (yielding unity by continuity) or dissimilar (yielding unity by aggregation). This lesson unpacks these distinctions with examples like a tree, house, and dirham.

Guided Path
1
Read the source and translation
Read the passage in Arabic and its English translation to get an overview of the classification.
Notice the key contrast between 'essentially' and 'accidentally'.
2
Understand essential vs accidental division
Identify that quantity divides essentially, while body divides accidentally. Review the examples: magnitude vs. body.
The footnote explains that bodies accept division because magnitude occurs to them.
3
Distinguish between continuity and aggregation
See that similar parts yield unity by continuity (one continuous whole), while dissimilar parts yield unity by aggregation (composite whole).
Examples: a continuous line vs. a tree with different parts.
4
Apply to the given examples
Consider the examples of a tree (aggregation by nature), house (aggregation by craft), and dirham (aggregation by position). Understand how each fits the category.

المبحث الثاني
في معرض الوحدة والكثرة
(قال : معروض (۱) الوحدة قد لا يكون معروض الكثرة (٢)، وحينئذ إن لم يكن له مفهوم سوى عدم الانقسام فوحدة على الإطلاق (۳) ، وإن كان فإن لم يقبل الانقسام، فإما ذو وضع (٤) فنقطة ، أو لا فمفارق، وإن قبله فإما بالذات (٥) فكم أو بالعرض ( ٦ ) إلى أجزاء متشابهة (٧) ، أو متحالفة [ فواحدة باتصال أجزائه (۸) ، فواحد بالاجتماع طبيعة أو صناعة أو وضعاً كالشجر والبيت، والدرهم. وقد يكون معروض الكثرة ولا بد من اختلاف الجهة فإن قومت جهة الوحدة جهة الكثرة فوحدة جنسية، أو نوعية أو فصلية وإلا فإن عرضت لها، فوحدة بالعرض، وإلا فبالنسبة .
والوحدة في الجنس تسمى مجانسة، وفي النوع مماثلة، وفي الكم مساواة وفي الكيف مشابهة وفي النسبة مناسبة وفي الخاصة مشاكلة وفي الأطراف مطابقة ، وفي وضع الأجزاء موازاة .
(۱) معروض الوحدة : موصوفها .
،
(۲) وذلك بأن يكون جزئياً لا كلياً يصح صدقه على الكثرة. (۳) أي وحدة حقيقية لا تتوقف وحدتها على قيد .
(٤) أي مكان يشار إليه .
(٥) كالمقدار من طول وعرض وعمق فإن قبوله هذا الأبعاد للانقسام لذواتها وقبل الأجسام لها
بعروضها له عند الفلاسفة .
(٦) كالجسم فإن قبوله للانقسام عند الفلاسفة بعروض المقدار له.
(۷) في الحقيقة والصورة.
(۸) ما بين القوسين سقط من (أ) و (ب).
Second investigation on the subject of unity and multiplicity. (He said: The subject (1) of unity may not be the subject of multiplicity (2), and in that case if it has no concept other than absence of division, then it is unity absolutely (3); and if it does [have a concept], then if it does not admit division, then either it has position (4) so it is a point, or not so it is separate; and if it admits [division], then either essentially (5) so quantity, or accidentally (6) into similar parts (7), or dissimilar [so one by continuity of its parts (8), so one by aggregation—by nature, craft, or position, like trees, a house, and a dirham. And [the subject] may be subject to multiplicity, and there must be difference of aspect: if the aspect of unity determines the aspect of multiplicity, then it is generic unity, or specific unity, or differential unity; and if not, then if [unity] occurs to it, then it is accidental unity, and if not, then relative unity. And unity in genus is called homogeneity, in species similarity, in quantity equality, in quality resemblance, in relation proportionality, in property analogy, in extremities correspondence, and in arrangement of parts parallelism. (1) Subject of unity: its descriptor. (2) That is, it is particular, not universal that can be predicated of many. (3) That is, real unity not dependent on a condition. (4) That is, a place that can be pointed to. (5) Like magnitude of length, width, and depth, for their acceptance of these dimensions for division is essential, and bodies accept it by their occurrence to it according to the philosophers. (6) Like body, for its acceptance of division according to the philosophers is by the occurrence of magnitude to it. (7) In reality and form. (8) What is between the brackets is missing from (A) and (B).
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage defines the classification of unity in terms of its subject and introduces key terminology. It begins by stating that the subject of unity may or may not also be a subject of multiplicity. If it is not, and it has no concept other than absence of division, it is absolute unity. If it has a concept but does not admit division: if it has position, it is a point; if not, it is something separate (a simple substance). If it admits division: either essentially (quantity) or accidentally (body) into similar or dissimilar parts—similar parts yield unity by continuity, dissimilar parts yield unity by aggregation (e.g., a tree, house, dirham). Then it discusses cases where the subject does admit multiplicity, requiring a difference of aspect: if the aspect of unity determines the aspect of multiplicity, it is generic, specific, or differential unity; if unity merely occurs to it, it is accidental unity; otherwise relative unity. Finally, it lists the technical terms for unity across categories: homogeneity (genus), similarity (species), equality (quantity), resemblance (quality), proportionality (relation), analogy (property), correspondence (extremities), parallelism (arrangement). The footnotes clarify the terms.

Key Terms

جسم
واحد بالاتصال
واحد بالاجتماع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that all divisible unities are the same.
Correction: The passage distinguishes essential (quantity) from accidental (body) division, and further between similar parts (continuity) and dissimilar parts (aggregation).

Checkpoint
According to the passage, a body (جسم) admits division in what way?
A) Essentially
B) Accidentally
C) By continuity
D) By aggregation

Key Takeaway
When subject admits division, unity is either essential (quantity) or accidental (body), leading to continuity (similar parts) or aggregation (dissimilar parts).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your own body is made of many different organs and parts. According to this lesson, is it united by continuity or aggregation? How does that classification change the way you think about your identity as a single person?
?
Think of something you consider 'one' but is actually composed of many parts—like a family or a community. Does this lesson's distinction between continuity and aggregation help you understand that unity better? What responsibility does it place on you to maintain that unity?

Logic and Technical Terms

When the Subject is Multiplicity: Unity by Genus, Species, Accident, and Relation
Classifies unity when subject is universal: generic, specific, differential, accidental, relative, with terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third branch of unity: when the subject is also subject to multiplicity, leading to generic, specific, differential, accidental, or relative unity, and the specialized terminology for unity across categories.

Core Idea
Unity can be by genus, species, differential, accident, or relation; each category has its own term.

The final part of the classification deals with subjects that are universal and can be applied to many. Here, the aspect of unity and the aspect of multiplicity are distinguished. If the unity aspect determines the multiplicity aspect, we get generic, specific, or differential unity. If unity merely occurs to the subject, it is accidental unity; otherwise relative unity. The passage then lists the specific terms for unity in different categories: homogeneity, similarity, equality, resemblance, proportionality, analogy, correspondence, parallelism.

Guided Path
1
Read the source and translation
Read the passage and translation to understand the flow from condition to the three cases and then the terminology.
Notice the phrase 'difference of aspect' is crucial.
2
Understand the condition of difference of aspect
The subject is universal, so unity and multiplicity come from different aspects. Without this difference, the classification wouldn't apply.
3
Identify the three types when unity determines multiplicity
When the aspect of unity determines the aspect of multiplicity, we get generic, specific, or differential unity. Understand each: genus unifies across species, species unifies individuals, differentia unifies within a species.
4
Learn the specialized terms
Memorize the terms for unity in each category: homogeneity (genus), similarity (species), equality (quantity), resemblance (quality), proportionality (relation), analogy (property), correspondence (extremities), parallelism (arrangement).
These terms are used in logic and kalam discussions.

Classification of Unity: From Absolute to Continuous and Aggregated
Hierarchical classification of unity from absolute to specific types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the hierarchical classification of unity as presented in Islamic logic, distinguishing between absolute unity, point, separate substance, quantity, body, and ultimately the one by continuity and the one by aggregation.

Core Idea
Unity has a logical hierarchy leading to two key types: continuity and aggregation.

In this passage, the author revisits the concept of unity and presents a detailed classification based on whether the subject of unity can be multiplied and whether it has a concept other than indivisibility. This classification is foundational for understanding how different types of wholes are considered one in Islamic logic and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Trace the classification tree
Read carefully as the author divides unity step by step. Start with the subject of unity: is it multiplied or not? If not, does it have another concept? Follow the branches.
2
Identify the two main categories
Notice that when the subject is divisible, it is either essentially divisible (quantity) or accidentally divisible (body). Then, if the parts are similar, it's one by continuity; if different, one by aggregation.
3
Note the inclusion of quantity
The author explicitly includes quantity under one by continuity. Reflect on why quantity is considered continuous.
4
Reflect on the logical precision
This classification shows how Islamic scholars carefully defined terms to avoid ambiguity. Consider how each branch excludes the others.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

سبق
أن الوحدة قد تعرض لنفس الوحدة كما يقال : وحدة واحدة ووحدات كثيرة، ولغيرها فهذا بيان لأقسامها، باعتبار المعروض تنبيهاً على بعض الاصطلاحات وعلى اختلاف معناها بحسب الأفراد فموضوع الوحدة إما أن يكون معروضاً للكثرة بأن يصدق على كثيرين أو لا فإن لم يكن، فإما أن يكون له مفهوم سوى عدم (۱) الانقسام أو لا فإن لم يمكن له مفهوم سوى عدم الانقسام. كما في قولنا وحدة واحدة، فهو الوحدة على الإطلاق وإن كان له مفهوم سوى ذلك، فإما أن يكون ذلك (۲) المفهوم قابلاً، أو لا فإن لم يكن، فإما أن يكون بحيث يمكن أن يشار إليه إشارة حسية أو لا فالأول النقطة، والثاني المفارق، وإن كان قابلاً للقسمة، فقبوله القسمة للانقسام (۳)، إما بالذات وهو الكم أو بالعرض وهو الجسم، فإن كان بسيطاً متشابه الأقسام فهـو الواحد بالاتصال، وإن كان مركباً مختلف الأقسام، فهو الواحد بالاجتماع . والكم أيضاً من قبيل الواحد بالاتصال، وقد يقال الواحد بالاتصال بمقدارين يلتقيان عند حد مشترك ()()() كضلعي (٥) الزاوية ، ولجسمين يتلازم طرفاهما بحيث يتحرك أحدهما بحركة الأخر، سواء كان الالتئام (٦) طبيعياً كاللحم مع العظم أو لا كأجزاء السلسلة . قال الإمام : الأجسام المتشابهة إن اعتبر حالها قبل حصول الانقسام فهو الواحد بالاتصال لأن صورته وهيولاه واحدة وإن (۷) أمكن أن يعرض فيه أجزاء تتلاقى عند حد مشترك، وإن اعتبر حالها عند حصول القسمة، فإنه لا بد أن تكون تلك الأجزاء من شأنها أن تتحد موضوعاتها بالفعل، لا كأشخاص الناس، فإنه ليس من شأنها الاتحاد فهذا القسم مع أنه واحد بالنوع واحد بالموضوع يعني أن المياه المتكثرة...
It has preceded that unity may occur to unity itself, as it is said: one unity and many unities, and to other than itself; so this is an explanation of its divisions in consideration of the subject, as a reminder of some terminologies and of the variation of its meaning according to individuals. So the subject of unity either is a subject of multiplicity (i.e., predicable of many) or not. If it is not, then either it has a concept other than absence (1) of division or not. If it is not possible for it to have a concept other than absence of division, as in our saying 'one unity', then it is absolute unity. And if it has a concept other than that, then either that (2) concept is divisible or not. If it is not, then either it can be pointed to sensibly or not: the first is the point, the second is the separate. And if it is divisible, then its acceptance of division (3) is either essentially (quantity) or accidentally (body). Then if it is simple with similar parts, it is the one by continuity; and if it is composite with different parts, it is the one by aggregation. Quantity also is of the type of the one by continuity. And the one by continuity is sometimes said of two magnitudes meeting at a common limit ()()() like the two sides (5) of an angle, and of two bodies whose ends are contiguous such that one moves with the motion of the other, whether the union (6) is natural (like flesh with bone) or not (like parts of a chain). Imam said: Similar bodies—if their state is considered before division occurs, then it is the one by continuity because their form and matter are one, even though (7) it is possible that parts in them come together at a common limit; and if their state is considered at the time of division, then necessarily those parts are of such a nature that their subjects actually unite, not like individual humans, for it is not their nature to unite. So this division, while it is one by species, is one by subject, meaning that the many waters...
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage elaborates on the classification of unity, particularly distinguishing between the one by continuity (واحد بالاتصال) and the one by aggregation (واحد بالاجتماع). It begins by revisiting the earlier point that unity can apply to itself or others. Then it re-explains the classification: if the subject is not subject to multiplicity, check if it has another concept; if not, it's absolute unity. If it has a concept but is indivisible: sensible pointing makes it a point, otherwise a separate substance. If divisible, division is either essential (quantity) or accidental (body). If simple with similar parts, it is one by continuity; if composite with different parts, it is one by aggregation. Quantity is included in the one by continuity. It then gives additional examples: two magnitudes meeting at a common limit (like sides of an angle) and two bodies with contiguous ends (natural or artificial). The passage quotes 'Imam' (likely al-Razi) who clarifies that similar bodies considered before division are one by continuity because their form and matter are one, though parts may theoretically meet. After division, such parts can actually unite (unlike individual humans). The last sentence indicates that many waters, while many, are one by subject—though the text cuts off.

الواحد بالاتصال
الواحد بالاجتماع

جسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All indivisible unities are points.
Correction: The passage distinguishes between point (sensible) and separate substance (non-sensible) when the concept is not divisible.

Checkpoint
In the classification, when does a divisible thing qualify as 'one by continuity'?
A) When it is composite with different parts.
B) When it is simple with similar parts.
C) When it is a point.
D) When it is absolute unity.

Key Takeaway
The logical classification of unity proceeds from absolute unity to divisible wholes, culminating in the distinction between continuous and aggregated unity.

Examples of the One by Continuity and the Imam's Clarification
Provides examples of the one by continuity and an analytical clarification from Imam al-Razi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the practical examples of the one by continuity, including two magnitudes meeting at a common limit and two bodies with contiguity, as well as the Imam's distinction between considering similar bodies before and after division.

Core Idea
The one by continuity is illustrated through geometric and physical examples, and its application to similar bodies depends on temporal consideration.

After establishing the classification, the author provides concrete examples to illustrate the one by continuity. These include geometric figures like the sides of an angle and physical bodies with natural or artificial union. He then quotes Imam al-Razi, who offers a nuanced perspective: similar bodies can be considered one by continuity before division because their matter and form are one, but after division they are actually united in subject if their nature allows.

Guided Path
1
Study the geometric example
The passage mentions two magnitudes meeting at a common limit, like the sides of an angle. Recognize that this is a mathematical example of continuity.
2
Consider the physical examples
Notice the two kinds of union: natural (flesh and bone) and artificial (chain links). Both fall under one by continuity.
3
Analyze Imam al-Razi's clarification
Read the Imam's words carefully. He distinguishes between considering similar bodies before division (one by continuity) and after division (if they can actually unite in subject). Contrast this with individual humans who cannot unite.
4
Reflect on the incomplete ending
The text ends mid-sentence about many waters being one by subject. Consider what that might imply about unity by aggregation.

أن الوحدة قد تعرض لنفس الوحدة كما يقال : وحدة واحدة ووحدات كثيرة، ولغيرها فهذا بيان لأقسامها، باعتبار المعروض تنبيهاً على بعض الاصطلاحات وعلى اختلاف معناها بحسب الأفراد فموضوع الوحدة إما أن يكون معروضاً للكثرة بأن يصدق على كثيرين أو لا فإن لم يكن، فإما أن يكون له مفهوم سوى عدم (۱) الانقسام أو لا فإن لم يمكن له مفهوم سوى عدم الانقسام. كما في قولنا وحدة واحدة، فهو الوحدة على الإطلاق وإن كان له مفهوم سوى ذلك، فإما أن يكون ذلك (۲) المفهوم قابلاً، أو لا فإن لم يكن، فإما أن يكون بحيث يمكن أن يشار إليه إشارة حسية أو لا فالأول النقطة، والثاني المفارق، وإن كان قابلاً للقسمة، فقبوله القسمة للانقسام (۳)، إما بالذات وهو الكم أو بالعرض وهو الجسم، فإن كان بسيطاً متشابه الأقسام فهـو الواحد بالاتصال، وإن كان مركباً مختلف الأقسام، فهو الواحد بالاجتماع . والكم أيضاً من قبيل الواحد بالاتصال، وقد يقال الواحد بالاتصال بمقدارين يلتقيان عند حد مشترك ()()() كضلعي (٥) الزاوية ، ولجسمين يتلازم طرفاهما بحيث يتحرك أحدهما بحركة الأخر، سواء كان الالتئام (٦) طبيعياً كاللحم مع العظم أو لا كأجزاء السلسلة . قال الإمام : الأجسام المتشابهة إن اعتبر حالها قبل حصول الانقسام فهو الواحد بالاتصال لأن صورته وهيولاه واحدة وإن (۷) أمكن أن يعرض فيه أجزاء تتلاقى عند حد مشترك، وإن اعتبر حالها عند حصول القسمة، فإنه لا بد أن تكون تلك الأجزاء من شأنها أن تتحد موضوعاتها بالفعل، لا كأشخاص الناس، فإنه ليس من شأنها الاتحاد فهذا القسم مع أنه واحد بالنوع واحد بالموضوع يعني أن المياه المتكثرة...
It has preceded that unity may occur to unity itself, as it is said: one unity and many unities, and to other than itself; so this is an explanation of its divisions in consideration of the subject, as a reminder of some terminologies and of the variation of its meaning according to individuals. So the subject of unity either is a subject of multiplicity (i.e., predicable of many) or not. If it is not, then either it has a concept other than absence (1) of division or not. If it is not possible for it to have a concept other than absence of division, as in our saying 'one unity', then it is absolute unity. And if it has a concept other than that, then either that (2) concept is divisible or not. If it is not, then either it can be pointed to sensibly or not: the first is the point, the second is the separate. And if it is divisible, then its acceptance of division (3) is either essentially (quantity) or accidentally (body). Then if it is simple with similar parts, it is the one by continuity; and if it is composite with different parts, it is the one by aggregation. Quantity also is of the type of the one by continuity. And the one by continuity is sometimes said of two magnitudes meeting at a common limit ()()() like the two sides (5) of an angle, and of two bodies whose ends are contiguous such that one moves with the motion of the other, whether the union (6) is natural (like flesh with bone) or not (like parts of a chain). Imam said: Similar bodies—if their state is considered before division occurs, then it is the one by continuity because their form and matter are one, even though (7) it is possible that parts in them come together at a common limit; and if their state is considered at the time of division, then necessarily those parts are of such a nature that their subjects actually unite, not like individual humans, for it is not their nature to unite. So this division, while it is one by species, is one by subject, meaning that the many waters...
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage elaborates on the classification of unity, particularly distinguishing between the one by continuity (واحد بالاتصال) and the one by aggregation (واحد بالاجتماع). It begins by revisiting the earlier point that unity can apply to itself or others. Then it re-explains the classification: if the subject is not subject to multiplicity, check if it has another concept; if not, it's absolute unity. If it has a concept but is indivisible: sensible pointing makes it a point, otherwise a separate substance. If divisible, division is either essential (quantity) or accidental (body). If simple with similar parts, it is one by continuity; if composite with different parts, it is one by aggregation. Quantity is included in the one by continuity. It then gives additional examples: two magnitudes meeting at a common limit (like sides of an angle) and two bodies with contiguous ends (natural or artificial). The passage quotes 'Imam' (likely al-Razi) who clarifies that similar bodies considered before division are one by continuity because their form and matter are one, though parts may theoretically meet. After division, such parts can actually unite (unlike individual humans). The last sentence indicates that many waters, while many, are one by subject—though the text cuts off.

Misconception: The one by continuity only applies to continuous magnitudes like lines, not to physical bodies.
Correction: Imam al-Razi says similar physical bodies before division are also one by continuity because their form and matter are one.

Key Takeaway
The one by continuity includes not only geometric magnitudes but also similar physical bodies before division, as their form and matter are unified.

?

Your life is made of many moments—are you living them as a continuous whole directed toward Allah, or are you fragmented by distractions? What would it mean for you to be 'one by continuity' in your devotion?
?
Imam al-Razi clarifies that after division, parts can unite in subject. Consider a time when a sin or mistake divided you from your sense of purity. How does the truth that reunification is still possible—just as divided parts can be united—call you to seek forgiveness and rebuild your connection with Allah without despair?
