How does this truth about God's attributes challenge your tendency to think of Him as either abstractly distant or as a mere projection of human qualities? What specific step will you take today to deepen your reverence for His unique attributes?

The Linguistic Argument and Its Refutation
Critique of the linguistic argument about 'other than'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the common linguistic argument 'there is no one in the house other than Zayd' fails as a metaphysical proof and leads to absurd conclusions.

Core Idea
Linguistic usage is not a reliable guide for metaphysical distinctness, as it can lead to absurdities.

The author now critiques the main argument of those who rely on everyday language to deny distinctness between a thing and its attributes. The example 'There is no one in the house other than Zayd' is examined to show its logical flaws. Prepare to see how a seemingly clear statement, if taken literally, implies contradictions and is therefore invalid as a proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the critique of the linguistic example
Read the passage from 'إلا أن عمدتهم الوثقى' to the end. Note the examples: 'ليس في الدار غير زيد' and 'ليس في يدي غير عشرة دراهم'.
The author argues that if taken literally, the phrase would exclude Zayd's limbs and even his clothes from being distinct.
2
Understand the absurd consequence
The argument implies that Zayd's clothes and all goods in the house are not other than Zayd, which is obviously false. This shows the corruption of the reasoning.
3
Examine the footnotes (1)-(4)
These additional points address objections: relata cannot be distinct, agent and object are perceived as non-conjoining, and conceptual separation of essences (father-son) is valid.
The footnotes may be separate but are integrated here due to OCR.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

They argue that since this speech is correct even though Zayd's limbs and attributes are in the house, the phrase 'other than' must exclude them, implying they are not distinct. However, the author points out the absurdity: if that argument were valid, it would also imply that Zayd's clothes and all other items in the house are not other than Zayd, which is obviously false. The intended meaning of the phrase is different. The footnotes further address objections: the case of relata (المضافين) is inconceivable as distinct; the agent and object are perceived as non-conjoining; and the separation of essence in the father-son case is conceptually valid.

Key Terms
لغة
عرف
تفرق
غيره

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Common language like 'there is no one else' proves that a person's attributes are not distinct from them.
Correction: The author shows this leads to absurdity; the intended meaning of such phrases is different and not a metaphysical proof.

Checkpoint
What does the author conclude about the argument based on the phrase 'there is no one in the house other than Zayd'?
A) It proves that Zayd's attributes are not distinct from him.
B) It is a corrupt argument because it would absurdly exclude Zayd's clothes and goods.
C) It is only valid for separable attributes.
D) It is a correct metaphysical proof.

Key Takeaway
The linguistic argument fails because it leads to absurdities; common speech cannot be used as a definitive proof in metaphysics.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'There is no god but Allah,' do you ever stop to examine whether your understanding of 'but' (as in 'other than') is shaped by linguistic habits rather than careful reflection? How might this lesson protect you from a shallow understanding of tawhid?
?
Think of a time you assumed that because a phrase is common in everyday speech, it must be true in a deeper sense. What was the consequence? How can you cultivate the discipline of questioning linguistic conveniences in your faith?
?
This lesson shows that even a seemingly harmless phrase like 'no one other than Zayd' can lead to absurdity if taken metaphysically. Where in your own beliefs about Allah's attributes might you be unwittingly using such a misleading linguistic shortcut? What will you do about it?

Definition of Distinctness and the Condition of Dissociation
Al-Taftazani defines al-ghayraan and states the condition of dissociation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the early Sunni definition of two distinct things (al-ghayraan) as two existents whose dissociation is possible, and the implications of this definition.

Core Idea
Distinctness is not mere multiplicity; it requires possible dissociation.

Al-Taftazani begins by addressing an objection: isn't the distinctness of father and son necessary? He clarifies that while distinctness in essence is necessary, the key question is whether multiplicity always entails distinctness. The early Sunni scholars said no, and they defined two distinct things as two existents whose dissociation is possible. This lesson unpacks that definition and its exclusions.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Start with the objection that distinctness is necessary, and see how al-Taftazani responds by distinguishing between essential distinctness and the entailment of multiplicity.
2
Study the definition of al-ghayraan
Identify the definition: two existents whose dissociation is possible. Notice that this excludes non-existents and pairs where dissociation is impossible.
The definition is existential and based on the possibility of separation.
3
Examine the exclusions
The passage excludes non-existents, part-whole pairs, and subject-attribute pairs (when the attribute is inseparable), because dissociation is not possible.
4
Understand the two types of dissociation
Dissociation can be in terms of location (tahayyuz) or in terms of existence and non-existence. This is general enough to cover bodies and separable attributes.
Dissociation from one side suffices.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل تغاير مثل الأب والابن والعلة والمعلول ضروري. قلنا نعم بحسب الذات. فإنه لا يتصور إلا بين متعدد، وإنما الخلاف في عكسه. وهو أن التعدد هل يستلزم التغاير فعند المتقدمين من أهل السنة لا. ولذا قالوا الغيران موجودان جاز انفكاكهما فخرج المعدومان وكذا المعدوم والموجود ومبناه على أن التغاير عندهم وجودي كالاختلاف والتضاد فلا يتصف به المعدوم وأما التعليل بأنه لا تمايز بين الإعدام فيخص المعدومين وخرج الجزء مع الكل وكذا الموصوف مع الصفة لامتناعه الانفكاك ودخل الجسمان وإن فرضنا كونهما قديمين لأنهما ينفكان بأن يوجد هذا في حيز لا يوجد فيه الآخر وكذا الصفة المفارقة مع موصوفها سواء كان قديماً أو حادثاً لأنهما ينفكان بأن يوجد الموصوف وتنعدم الصفة فجواز الانفكاك أعم من أن يكون بحسب التحيز أو بحسب الوجود والعدم فلا حاجة إلى التقييد بقولنا في حيز أو عدم على ما ذكره الشيخ. وهذا التقرير مشعر بأنه يكفي في التغاير الانفكاك من جانب وأن الصفة التي ليست عين الموصوف ولا غيره هي الصفة اللازمة النفسية وقيل بل الصفة القديمة كعلم الصانع وقدرته بخلاف مثل سواد الجسم وبياضه إلا أن عمدتهم الوثقى في التمسك وهو أن قولنا ليس في الدار غير زيد وليس في يدي غير عشرة دراهم كلام صحيح لغة وعرفاً مع أن لا في الدار أعضاء زيد وصفاته وفي اليد آحاد العشرة وأوصاف الدراهم تفرق بين الصفات المفارقة واللازمة ويقتضي أن لا يكون ثياب زيد بل سائر ما في الدار من الأمتعة غير زيد وفساده بين وكيف يخفى على أحد أن المراد بهذا الكلام نفي إنسان آخر غير زيد وعدد آخر فوق العشرة.
If it is said: The distinctness of father and son, and cause and effect is necessary. We say: Yes, in essence. For it is only conceivable between multiple things, but the disagreement is about its opposite: does multiplicity necessitate distinctness? According to the early scholars from Ahl al-Sunna, no. Hence they said: 'Two distinct things (al-ghayraan) are two existents whose dissociation (infikak) is possible.' So the two nonexistent things are excluded, and likewise the nonexistent and the existent. The basis of this is that distinctness according to them is existential (wujudiyy), like difference and opposition, so the nonexistent is not described by it. As for the explanation that there is no distinction between nonexistences, that pertains specifically to the two nonexistents. And the part with the whole is excluded, and likewise the described with the attribute, due to the impossibility of dissociation. And the two bodies are included, even if we suppose them to be eternal, because they dissociate by one existing in a location where the other does not exist. Likewise, the separable attribute (al-sifa al-mufariqa) with its subject, whether it is eternal or originated, because they dissociate by the subject existing and the attribute ceasing to exist. So the permissibility of dissociation (jawaz al-infikak) is more general, being either in terms of location (tahayyuz) or in terms of existence and non-existence, so there is no need for restriction by saying 'in a location' or 'nonexistence' according to what the Shaykh mentioned. This explanation indicates that dissociation from one side is sufficient for distinctness, and that the attribute which is neither identical to the subject nor other than it is the essential necessary attribute (al-sifa al-lazima al-nafsiyya). And it is said: rather the eternal attribute, like the knowledge and power of the Creator, unlike the blackness of a body and its whiteness. But their strongest proof (umdatuhum al-wuthqa) is that our saying: 'There is no one other than Zayd in the house' and 'I have no more than ten dirhams in my hand' is a correct statement in language and custom, although in the house there are Zayd's limbs and attributes, and in the hand there are the units of ten and the descriptions of the dirhams. They distinguish between separable and inseparable attributes, and require that Zayd's clothes, indeed all the goods in the house, are not other than Zayd, and the corruption of this is obvious. How could it be hidden from anyone that the intent of this speech is to negate another human being other than Zayd, and another number above ten?
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage begins with an objection and answer regarding the necessity of distinctness between things like father and son, and cause and effect. The author (al-Taftazani, likely) states the objection that their distinctness is necessary. His answer: yes, in essence, but the real issue is whether multiplicity entails distinctness. He explains that the early Sunni scholars hold that multiplicity does not necessarily entail distinctness. They define two distinct things (al-ghayraan) as two existents whose dissociation (infikak) is possible. Then he elaborates on what this definition entails and excludes: non-existents, parts and wholes, subjects and attributes, etc. He clarifies that dissociation can be in terms of location or existence/non-existence, and that dissociation from one side suffices. He also discusses the attribute that is neither identical nor entirely other, and then presents a linguistic argument using everyday examples to show that language distinguishes between separable and inseparable attributes. The aim is to support the definition that dissociation is the criterion for distinctness.

Key Terms
mutaghayirayn
ghayraan
infikak
wujudiyy
tahayyuz

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that distinctness requires actual separation or difference in location or time.
Correction: The passage teaches that possible dissociation from one side suffices; the two things need not be actually separate.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the condition for two things to be considered distinct (ghayr)?
A) They must exist in different locations.
B) Dissociation (infikak) must be possible, even if only from one side.
C) They must be separable in external existence.
D) They must be different in essence.

Key Takeaway
Two things are distinct if dissociation between them is conceivable, even if only in one direction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of one aspect of your faith that you have been treating as separate from the rest of your life (e.g., prayer separate from ethics, belief separate from action). According to this lesson, if dissociation is impossible, then that aspect is not truly distinct—it is part of the whole. Will you stop treating it as separate and begin integrating it today?
?
The passage excludes part and whole from being distinct. In your relationship with Allah, are you trying to dissociate your reliance on Him from your daily decisions? If you truly believed that no part of your life can be distinct from His will, what would you change starting this moment?

Attributes and the Linguistic Proof for Distinctness
Al-Taftazani discusses attributes and presents a linguistic proof for the dissociation criterion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the discussion of attributes and a linguistic example support the condition of dissociation as the criterion for distinctness.

Core Idea
Even everyday language shows that dissociation distinguishes things, not mere multiplicity.

After establishing the definition, al-Taftazani addresses the nature of attributes: some are inseparable (essential necessary attributes) while others are separable. He then presents a powerful linguistic argument using common statements to show that dissociation is the key criterion. This lesson examines that argument and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish between types of attributes
Read the discussion about attributes that are neither identical to nor other than the subject, and the distinction between essential necessary attributes and separable ones.
Attributes like knowledge and power are eternal and inseparable; blackness of a body is separable.
2
Study the linguistic proof
Examine the everyday sayings: 'There is no one other than Zayd in the house' and 'I have no more than ten dirhams.' These statements are correct even though they include parts and attributes.
The proof shows that language distinguishes between separable and inseparable attributes.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
The proof demonstrates that dissociation is the operative criterion, not mere difference in essence or number.

Objections to the Definition of al-Mutaghayirayn and Responses
Objection to the definition of al-mutaghayirayn and responses by al-Amidi and others.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate around the definition of al-mutaghayirayn (two distinct things) and the role of intellectual conception (ta'aqqul) in distinguishing entities that are inseparable in reality.

Core Idea
Distinction can be conceptual even if separation is impossible in reality.

This passage dives into a vital debate in Islamic theology: what does it truly mean for two things to be 'distinct'? The definition of al-mutaghayirayn as 'two existents whose dissociation is possible' faces a powerful objection: the world and the Creator are clearly distinct, yet the world cannot exist without the Creator. How can they be 'dissociable'? The answers offered by al-Amidi and others reveal deep insights about the nature of distinction, reality, and intellectual conception. Let's follow the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial objection
Read the first sentence of the passage. It states an objection against the definition of al-mutaghayirayn: the definition is not comprehensive because the world and the Creator are distinct (mutaghayirayn) yet their separation (infikak) is impossible—the world cannot exist without the Creator.
Notice that the objector assumes that 'dissociation possible' must mean actual separation.
2
Examine al-Amidi's first answer
Al-Amidi responds that dissociation from one side is sufficient. It is possible for the world not to exist while the Creator exists. This preserves distinctness even though the reverse is impossible.
The key phrase: 'انفكاك من جانب واحد' (dissociation from one side).
3
Understand the refutation of that answer
The refutation argues that if one-sided dissociation suffices, then the definition is not exclusive (mani'). It would include relationships like part-whole and subject-attribute, where the part can exist without the whole, etc., but they are not considered distinct in the intended sense.
This refutation forces a refinement of the definition.
4
Learn the second answer: intellectual conception
Some scholars answer that what is meant is the permissibility of dissociation in intellectual conception (fi al-ta'aqqul), not in external existence. Even if the world cannot exist without the Creator, we can conceive of the world without conceiving the Creator (though we need proof to know Him).
This aligns with the Mu'tazila view that two things are distinct if one can be known without the other.
5
Resolve the final objection about the world as effect
A final objection says that the world as an effect cannot be conceived without its cause. The answer: distinctness is based on dissociation in essence (dhat) and reality (haqiqa), not in conception. The passage ends with 'wa la 'ibra' (no consideration), implying that conceptual inseparability does not negate real distinctness.
This shows the ultimate criterion is ontological, not merely conceptual.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعترض على تعريف المتغايرين بأنه ليس بجامع لأن العالم والصانع متغايران ولا يجوز انفكاكهما لامتناع وجود العالم بدون الصانع. وأجاب الآمدي بأنه يكفي الانفكاك من جانب واحد وقد أمكن عدم العالم مع وجود الصانع. ورد بأنه حينئذ لا يكون مانعا لأنه حينئذ يدخل فيه الجزء مع الكل والموصوف مع الصفة إذ يمكن وجود الجزء والموصوف مع عدم الكل والصفة وإن امتنع عكسه. وأجاب بعضهم بأن المراد جواز الانفكاك من الجانبين لكن بحسب التعقل دون الخارج وكما يمكن أن يعقل وجود الصانع دون العالم كذلك يمكن أن يعقل وجود العالم ولا يعقل وجود الصانع بل يطلب بالبرهان وهذه العناية توافق ما نقل عن بعض المعتزلة أن الغيرين هما اللذان يصح أن يعلم أحدهما ويجهل الآخر ولفظ أحدهما لإبهامه كثيرا ما يقع موقع كل واحد منهما وما قيل إن الشيء قد يعلم من جهة دون جهة كالسواد يعلم أنه لون ويجهل أنه مستحيل البقاء فلو تغايرت الجهتان لزم كون العرض الواحد الغير المتجزىء شيئين متغايرين ليس بشيء لأن تغاير جهتي الشيء لا يستلزم تغايره في نفسه. فإن قيل العالم من حيث إنه معلول ومصنوع للصانع لا يمكن أن يعقل بدونه فيلزم أن لا يكونا متغايرين. قلنا المعتبر في المغاير هو الانفكاك بحسب الذات والحقيقة ولا عبرة.
And an objection was raised against the definition of the two distinct things (al-mutaghayirayn) that it is not comprehensive, because the world and the Creator are distinct but their dissociation is not permissible, due to the impossibility of the world existing without the Creator. Al-Amidi answered that dissociation from one side is sufficient, and it is possible for the world not to exist while the Creator exists. It was refuted that then it would not be exclusive, because then it would include the part with the whole, and the described with the attribute, since it is possible for the part and the described to exist without the whole and the attribute, although the opposite is impossible. Some of them answered that what is meant is the permissibility of dissociation from both sides, but in terms of intellectual conception (ta'aqqul) not externally. Just as it is possible to conceive the Creator without the world, so also it is possible to conceive the world without conceiving the Creator, rather it is sought by proof. This concern agrees with what is transmitted from some Mu'tazila that the two other things (al-ghayrayn) are those such that it is valid to know one and be ignorant of the other, and the word 'one of them' (ahaduhuma) often stands for each one of them due to its vagueness. And what is said that a thing may be known from one aspect and not from another, like blackness is known to be a color and unknown to be incapable of permanence; if the two aspects were distinct, then it would necessitate that a single indivisible accident be two distinct things. This is nothing, because the distinctness of the two aspects of a thing does not necessitate its distinctness in itself. If it is said: The world, insofar as it is an effect and product of the Creator, cannot be conceived without Him, so it would necessitate that they are not distinct. We say: What is considered in distinctness is dissociation in terms of essence (dhat) and reality (haqiqa), and no consideration is given.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the definition of al-mutaghayirayn (two distinct things) as two existents whose dissociation is possible. The objector says it is not comprehensive because the world and the Creator are distinct yet their dissociation is impossible (the world cannot exist without the Creator). Al-Amidi responds that dissociation from one side is sufficient (the world can be absent while the Creator exists). A refutation follows: this would include part-whole and subject-attribute. A second answer: dissociation is meant in terms of intellectual conception (ta'aqqul), not external existence. This aligns with some Mu'tazila's view that two things are distinct if one can be known without the other. The passage ends with a further objection: the world as an effect cannot be conceived without the cause, so they would not be distinct. The answer: the criterion is dissociation in essence (dhat) and reality (haqiqa), not in conception.

Key Terms
mutaghayirayn
infikak
ta'aqqul
ghayrayn
dhat
haqiqa

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many assume that if two things cannot be separated in reality (like the world and Creator), they cannot be truly distinct.
Correction: The passage shows that distinctness can be based on one-sided dissociation or intellectual conception, and ultimately on essence and reality, not external separation.

Checkpoint
How does the passage respond to the objection that the world and the Creator cannot be dissociated?
A) They are not distinct.
B) Dissociation is possible from one side.
C) Dissociation is only in the intellect, not externally.
D) Both B and C are given as answers.

Key Takeaway
The definition of al-mutaghayirayn requires dissociation in essence (dhat) and reality (haqiqa), not necessarily in external existence or even in conception, though intellectual conception serves as a helpful indicator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever confuse knowing about God from proofs with actually knowing Him? This lesson distinguishes conceptual separation from real distinction. In your own life, do you treat God as a concept you can conceive of without being transformed, or do you recognize that He is distinct in essence and your knowledge of Him must lead to submission? Commit to one action today that moves from intellectual acknowledgment to heartfelt worship.
?
The objection suggests that because the world cannot exist without the Creator, they are not distinct. Are there areas in your life where you act as if you can exist without Him? Examine your habits: what do you rely on as if it were independent of God? What would it look like to live in constant awareness that everything is dependent on Him?
?
The passage warns against confusing distinctness of aspects with distinctness of the thing itself. In your spiritual journey, have you mistaken partial understanding of a truth for full possession of it? For example, knowing about tawhid without it affecting your actions. What is one aspect of your belief that you have only conceptualized but not actualized? How will you bridge that gap today?

Objection and Answers on the Definition of Two Distinct Things
Dialectical exchange on the definition of al-mutaghayirayn.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the definition of 'two distinct things' (al-mutaghayirayn) requires separability in mental conception rather than external reality, and how this resolves objections.

Core Idea
Distinctness in kalam is about mental separability, not physical separation.

This passage dives into a classic objection against the definition of two distinct things: how can the world and its Creator be distinct if they cannot exist separately? Al-Amidi navigates this by shifting the criterion from external reality to mental conception. As you read, trace how each answer refines the previous one, sharpening the definition until it withstands further critiques.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and first answer
Start with the objection: the definition is not comprehensive because the world and Maker are distinct but inseparable. Then read al-Amidi's first answer: one-sided separation suffices.
Notice the quick rejection of this answer in the next sentence.
2
Examine the rebuttal
The rebuttal points out that one-sided separation would include too much, like part-whole pairs. See why the first answer fails to be exclusive.
3
Understand the refined answer
The refined answer introduces mental conception: both-sided separation is required, but only in the mind, not in reality. This aligns with the Mu'tazilite criterion.
Pay attention to how this avoids the rebuttal while preserving distinctness of Creator and world.
4
Work through the nuance on aspects
A further nuance: knowing a thing in one aspect but not another does not make the thing itself two distinct things. This prevents over-application.
The example of blackness helps clarify.
5
Consider the final objection and answer
The final objection: the world as effect cannot be conceived without the Maker. The answer distinguishes essence from relations; distinctness concerns essence, not relational aspects.
This ties back to the core idea of mental separability based on essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعترض على تعريف المتغايرين بأنه ليس بجامع لأن العالم والصانع متغايران ولا يجوز انفكاكهما لامتناع وجود العالم بدون الصانع . وأجاب الآمدي بأنه يكفي الانفكاك من جانب واحد (٣) ، وقد أمكن عدم العالم مع وجود الصانع . ورد بأنه حينئذ لا يكون مانعاً ، لأنه حينئذ (٣) يدخل فيه الجزء مع الكل، والموصوف مع الصفة، إذ يمكن وجود الجزء والموصوف مع عدم الكل والصفة وإن امتنع عكسه. وأجاب بعضهم (٤) . بأن المراد جواز الانفكاك من الجانبين، لكن بحسب التعقل دون الخارج، وكما يمكن أن يعقل وجود الصانع دون العالم، كذلك يمكن أن يعقل وجود العالم، ولا يعقل وجود الصانع، بل يطلب بالبرهان، وهذه العناية توافق ما نقل عن بعض المعتزلة أن الغيرين هما اللذان يصح أن يعلم أحدهما ويجهل الآخر، ولفظ أحدهما لإبهامه كثيراً ما يقع موقع كل واحد منهما، وما قيل إن الشيء قد يعلم من جهة دون جهة، كالسواد يعلم أنه لون، ويجهل أنه مستحيل البقاء ، فلو تغايرت الجهتان لزم كون العرض الواحد الغير المتجزىء شيئين متغايرين ليس بشيء لأن تغاير جهتي الشيء الا يستلزم تغايره في نفسه (ه) . فإن قيل : العالم من حيث إنه معلول ومصنوع للصانع، لا يمكن أن يعقل بدونه فيلزم أن لا يكونا متغايرين . قلنا : المعتبر في المغاير هو (٦) الانفكاك بحسب الذات والحقيقة، ولا عبرة بالإضافات والاعتبارات والعالم باعتبار كـونـه معلولا للصانع من قبيل المضاف
And an objection was raised against the definition of 'the two distinct things' (al-mutaghayirayn) that it is not comprehensive, because the world and the Maker are distinct and their separation is not permissible due to the impossibility of the existence of the world without the Maker. Al-Amidi answered that separation from one side is sufficient, and the non-existence of the world along with the existence of the Maker is possible. It was rebutted that then it would not be exclusive, because then it would include the part with the whole, and the described with the attribute, since the existence of the part and the described can occur along with the non-existence of the whole and the attribute, although the reverse is impossible. And some answered that what is intended is the permissibility of separation from both sides, but according to mental conception (al-ta'aqqul) not external [reality]; just as it is possible to conceive of the existence of the Maker without the world, so too it is possible to conceive of the existence of the world without conceiving of the existence of the Maker, rather it is sought by proof. And this care agrees with what is transmitted from some Mu'tazila that the two distinct things are those about which it is valid to know one and be ignorant of the other, and the term 'one of them' due to its ambiguity often stands in the place of each one of them. And what is said that a thing may be known in one aspect and unknown in another, like blackness: it is known that it is a color, and unknown that it is impossible to subsist. So if the two aspects were distinct, it would entail that a single indivisible accident is two distinct things, which is nothing, because the distinctness of the two aspects of a thing does not necessitate its distinctness in itself. If it is said: The world, insofar as it is an effect and made by the Maker, cannot be conceived without Him, so it would follow that they are not distinct. We say: What is considered in distinctness is separation according to essence and reality, and no heed is paid to relations and considerations; and the world, by virtue of being an effect of the Maker, is of the category of the relative (al-mudaf).
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical exchange on the definition of 'two distinct things' (al-mutaghayirayn). The objection is that the definition is not comprehensive because the world and the Creator are distinct yet cannot be separate in reality. Al-Amidi first answers that separation from one side suffices—the world's non-existence is possible while the Creator exists. This is rebutted: if one-sided separation is enough, then other pairs like part-whole would be included, making the definition non-exclusive. Another answer refines this: separation must be possible from both sides, but only in mental conception (al-ta'aqqul), not externally. This is supported by the Mu'tazilite criterion of knowing one and being ignorant of the other. A further nuance is added: if a thing is known in one aspect and unknown in another (e.g., blackness as color vs. as non-subsisting), distinctness of aspects does not imply distinctness of the thing itself. Then a new objection: the world as effect cannot be conceived without the Maker, so they would not be distinct. The answer: distinctness concerns essence and reality, not relations; the world as effect is a relative concept, and distinctness of essences remains.

Key Terms
المتغايران
الانفكاك
التعقل
المضاف
الآمدي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That two things must be separable in external reality to be considered distinct (mutaghayirayn).
Correction: The passage explains that mental separability (al-ta'aqqul) is sufficient; external separation is not required.

Checkpoint
According to the refined answer in this passage, what kind of separation is sufficient for two things to be considered distinct (mutaghayirayn)?
A) External separation in reality
B) Separation from one side only
C) Possible separation in mental conception from both sides
D) Impossible separation

Key Takeaway
Distinctness in kalam is determined by the possibility of mental separation (conceptual independence) of essences, not by actual external separability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you picture Allah in your mind, do you subconsciously mix Him with images of creation—like the sky, nature, or events? This lesson insists Allah can be conceived entirely without the world. Is that how you think of Him? Or does your heart still tie His existence to the world? What will you do today to purify your mental image of Allah from any association with creation?
?
The Mu'tazilite criterion says two things are distinct if you can truly know one and be ignorant of the other. Can you honestly say you ‘know’ Allah in such a way that you could be completely unaware of everything else? Or is your awareness of Allah always entangled with your awareness of His blessings, signs, or the world around you? How would your prayer change if you focused solely on Allah, setting aside all thoughts of creation?
?
You constantly rely on Allah for your very existence, just as the world cannot exist without its Maker. Yet do you live with a felt sense of that dependence, or do you mostly feel independent and self-sufficient? This lesson reminds us that our essence is distinct from Allah's, but our existence is entirely tied to Him. Before you sleep tonight, what one thought can you fix in your heart to acknowledge that you are nothing without Him?

The Objection from Correlatives and the Answer on Essential Distinctness
Sanusi presents an objection from correlatives and the answer clarifying essential distinctness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection from correlatives is answered by distinguishing between essential distinctness and relational description.

Core Idea
Correlatives are distinct in essence, not in description.

Students of kalam often puzzle over how things that always imply each other, like father and son, can be considered distinct. This passage presents the objection that correlatives cannot be separated mentally, so they cannot be 'two distinct things' according to the definition. The response clarifies that what is self-evident is the distinctness of their essences, not their relational descriptions.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and concession
Begin by reading the opening lines where the objection is raised: correlatives cannot be separated even mentally, so they would not be distinct. Notice that the advocates of the definition concede this point.
The concession is that correlatives as such are not existent.
2
Analyze the counter-objection
The next part presents a counter-objection: the distinctness of father-son, cause-effect, etc. is self-evident and cannot be denied. Pause to consider why someone might object at this point.
3
Grasp the answer on essential distinctness
Read the answer carefully: the self-evident distinctness is between the two essences, not between the relational descriptions. The relational description does not entail existential separation. Distinctness is a property of the existent.
This is the key insight: essences are distinct even if relations are inseparable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد أورد على القائلين بأن الغيرين موجودان بحواز انفکاكهما، إنه لا انفكاك بين المتضايفين الابحسب الخارج ، ولا بحسب التعقل ، فيلزم أن لا يكونا متغايرين ، فالتزموا ذلك، وقالوا إنهما من حيث إنهما متضايفان ، ليسا بموجودين، والغيران لا بد أن يكونا موجودين فإن قيل : تغاير مثل الأب والابن والعلة والمعلول، وسائر المتضايفات، كالأخوين ضروري لا يمكن إنكاره . قلنا : الضروري هو التغاير بين الذاتين، وأما مع وصف الإضافة، فليسا بموجودين، والتغاير عندهم من خواص الموجود ، وبمثل هذا يندفع ما يقال أن تعريف الغيرين لا يشمل الجوهر مع العرض، ولا الاستطاعة مع الفعل لعدم الانفكاك، وذلك لأنهما باعتبار الذات ممكن الانفكاك في التعقل، بل في الخارج أيضاً، بأن يوجد هذا الجوهر بدون هذا العرض وبالعكس سيما عند من يقول بعدم بقاء الأعراض، وأن يحصل هذا الفعل بخلق الله تعالى ضرورة من غير استطاعة العبد وأن يحصل بهذه الاستطاعة غير هذا الفعل، سيما عند من يقول بأن الاستطاعة تصلح للضدين.
And an objection was raised against those who say that two distinct things exist with the possibility of their separation: there is no separation between correlatives (al-mutadhayifayn) except externally, not according to mental conception, so it follows that they are not distinct. So they conceded that, and said that they, insofar as they are correlatives, are not existent; and the two distinct things must be existent. If it is said: The distinctness of such as father and son, cause and effect, and all correlatives, like two brothers, is necessary and cannot be denied. We say: What is necessary is the distinctness between the two essences; but with the description of relation, they are not existent; and distinctness according to them is a property of the existent. And by something like this, what is said is rebutted: that the definition of the two distinct things does not include substance with accident, nor capability with action, due to lack of separation. That is because they, by virtue of essence, are possible of separation in mental conception, even externally, in that this substance can exist without this accident and vice versa, especially according to those who deny the subsistence of accidents; and that this action occurs by the creation of God necessarily without the slave's capability, and that by this capability something other than this action occurs, especially according to those who say that capability is suitable for opposites.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection from the perspective of correlatives (al-mutadhayifayn, e.g., father-son, cause-effect). The objection states that correlatives cannot be separated even mentally—one always implies the other—so they cannot be distinct things according to the definition requiring possible separation. The advocates of the definition concede that correlatives as such (i.e., under the description of relation) are not existent, and distinctness is a property of existents, so they are not distinct in that sense. Then a counter-objection: distinctness of father-son etc. is self-evident. The answer clarifies that what is self-evident is the distinctness of their essences (dhātayn), not of the relational description. The relational description does not entail existential separation. This principle also resolves another objection: that the definition does not include substance and accident or capability and action because they are inseparable in reality (e.g., a substance always has some accident). The answer: by essence they are separable in mental conception, and even externally for those who hold accidents do not persist; similarly, action might occur without the capability (if God creates it) or the capability might be used for a different action.

Key Terms
المتضايفان
التغاير
الذاتين
الإضافة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That correlatives are not distinct at all because they are inseparable.
Correction: Correlatives are distinct in their essences; the inseparability is only in the relational description, which does not negate essential distinctness.

Checkpoint
According to the answer in this passage, what is the self-evident distinctness (al-taghāyur al-ḍarūrī) regarding a father and son?
A) The distinctness of their relational descriptions
B) The distinctness of their essences
C) The distinctness of their names
D) The distinctness of their existence

Key Takeaway
Correlatives are distinct in their essences, not in their relational descriptions; distinctness is a property of existents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a relationship in your life that defines you—like parent, child, spouse, or friend. When you think of that person, do you see their essence, or do you see them through the lens of the relationship? If you stripped away the relation, who are they? Ask yourself: am I treating them as an essence or as a role? What would change if you focused on their essence?
?
In your understanding of Allah’s attributes, have you ever confused the relational descriptions (e.g., ‘Creator’) with His essence? The lesson shows that distinctness applies to essences, not relations. How does this affect the way you think about Allah’s attributes being ‘other than’ His essence? Take a moment to reaffirm that His attributes are not separate existent things, but are one with His essence.
?
When you look at yourself, do you define yourself by your relationships (e.g., ‘I am a son/daughter, student, worker’) or by your essence as a servant of Allah? The objection from correlatives shows that relational descriptions are not existent in themselves. If all relations were removed, what remains of you that truly exists? Pray that Allah helps you know your own essence as His servant.

Extending the Principle to Substance-Accident and Capability-Action
Sanusi applies the principle of essential distinctness to substance-accident and capability-action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will see how the same principle of essential distinctness rebuts objections about substance-accident and capability-action not fitting the definition of two distinct things.

Core Idea
Essences are separable even if descriptions are not.

Having established that correlatives are distinct in essence, the author now applies the same reasoning to other pairs that seem inseparable: substance and accident, and capability and action. Critics argue that these pairs cannot be 'two distinct things' because they are inseparable. The response shows that by essence they are separable in mental conception, and even externally under certain views.

Guided Path
1
Read how the principle is applied to substance-accident
The passage first addresses the objection that substance and accident are not distinct because an accident cannot exist without a substance. Note the response: by essence they are separable in mental conception, and even externally according to those who deny the subsistence of accidents.
The example of 'this substance without this accident' shows conceptual separability.
2
Understand the application to capability-action
Next, the passage tackles capability (istiṭāʿa) and action. Critics say they are inseparable because action depends on capability. The answer points out that action can occur directly from God without the slave's capability, or the capability can be used for a different action.
This is especially relevant for the Ashʿarī view that capability is suitable for opposites.
3
Reflect on the overall rebuttal
Consider how the principle of essential distinctness works in all these cases: what matters is whether the essences can be separated in thought, not whether they are actually separated in reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Nuance in Formulating the Objection and al-Amidi's Answer
Clarifies the proper formulation of the objection and the answer of al-Amidi, with a reference to al-Mawaqif.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the formulation of the objection involving the Creator and the world changes depending on the framework, and the role of mental separation (ta'aqqul) in al-Amidi's answer.

Core Idea
A shift in one premise reshapes the entire kalam objection.

This short note by Sanusi shows that even well-known objections and answers can hinge on subtle terminological frameworks. He points out that al-Amidi's answer works under one formulation, but when we adopt the framework of al-Mawaqif, the objection itself must be restated. This teaches us that precise wording is crucial in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then the English translation. Pay attention to the two formulations mentioned: one 'based on what we mentioned' and the other 'according to what is transmitted in al-Mawaqif'.
Notice that the Arabic uses technical terms like 'الانفكاك تعقلاً' (mental separation).
2
Identify the two formulations
Identify the original formulation and answer (mental separation), and the revised formulation under al-Mawaqif (restricting separation to being in a locus or non-existence). Understand why the revised formulation leads to impossibility.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this nuance matters: it shows that kalam arguments require precise definitions, and that different assumptions lead to different dialectical moves.
This connects to the broader kalam method.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن تقرير الاعتراض بالباري تعالى مع العالم . والجواب : بأن المراد الانفكاك تعقلاً. ورد جواب الآمدي ظاهر على ما ذكرناه ، وأما على ما نقل في المواقف من تقييد جواز الانفكاك بكونه في حيز أو عدم، فينبغي أن يكون تقرير الاعتراض هو أنه يمتنع انفكاك الباري تعالى عن العالم في حيز أو عدم لامتناع تحيزه وعدمه
And know that the formulation of the objection with the Exalted Creator and the world, and the answer: that what is intended is separation mentally. And the response of al-Amidi is apparent based on what we mentioned; but according to what is transmitted in al-Mawaqif—restricting the permissibility of separation to being in a locus or non-existence—the formulation of the objection should be that the separation of the Exalted Creator from the world in a locus or non-existence is impossible, due to the impossibility of His being located or having non-existence.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage is a brief note that summarizes the proper way to formulate the objection involving the Creator and the world, and the answer. It states that the objection is answered by saying the intended separation is mental (ta'aqqul), not external. Al-Amidi's answer is said to be apparent based on what was previously mentioned. However, according to what is transmitted in al-Mawaqif—which restricts the permissibility of separation to being in a locus (ḥayyiz) or non-existence (ʿadam)—the objection must be reformulated: it is impossible for the Creator to separate from the world in a locus (because He is not located) or in non-existence (because His non-existence is impossible). Thus, the objection's force is different under that restriction. This nuanced clarification shows how different frameworks affect the dialectic.

Key Terms
الباري تعالى
الانفكاك تعقلاً
المواقف
الحيز
العدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That al-Amidi's answer is straightforward and applies universally.
Correction: The passage shows that al-Amidi's answer depends on the framework; under al-Mawaqif's restriction, the objection must be reformulated, and the answer may not apply in the same way.

Checkpoint
According to the text, in al-Mawaqif, the permissibility of separation is restricted to what?
A) A locus and non-existence
B) Mental conception only
C) Both sides
D) External reality

Key Takeaway
The validity of a kalam objection depends on how its key terms (like 'separation') are defined within a given framework.

?
This passage shows that the same objection can be valid or invalid depending on the premises. In your own relationship with God, are there any objections or questions you raise that might only seem valid because you are assuming certain definitions (like 'separation' meaning spatial distance)? Will you take the time to reformulate that objection with the proper premises, even if it means the objection dissolves?

Correlates and Otherness: Objection and Response
Objection and response on correlates and definition of al-ghayrayn

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari response to an objection about correlates preserves the definition of al-ghayrayn and applies to substance-accident and capability-action.

Core Idea
Correlates are distinct in essence, not in relation; otherness is a property of existence.

This passage presents a sophisticated objection against the definition of 'two things' as entities that can be separated. The objector argues that correlates like cause and effect are inseparable in the mind, so they cannot be distinct. Read how the Ash'ari response distinguishes essence from relation, and see how this principle resolves potential counterexamples involving substance-accident and capability-action.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Identify the objection against those who define al-ghayrayn as existing with possible separation. What is the problem with correlates?
The objection claims that correlates are inseparable in the mind, so they cannot be distinct.
2
Understand the response
Notice how the text responds: distinction is between essences, not the relational description. As correlates, they are not existent.
3
Apply to substance and accident
See how the principle is used to include substance and accident in the definition, despite their empirical inseparability.
They are separable in essence both mentally and externally.
4
Apply to capability and action
Note the discussion of capability (istita'a) and action, especially the view that capability is suitable for opposites.
This reinforces the definition's applicability.

With additions and considerations, and the world, considering it as an effect of the Maker, is of the type of the relative. And an objection has been raised against those who say that the two things (al-ghayrayn) exist with the possibility of their separation: that there is no separation between correlates (mutadhayifayn) except according to external [existence], and not according to intellection, so it follows that they would not be distinct. So they committed to that, and they said that they, insofar as they are correlates, do not exist, and the two things (al-ghayran) must be existent. For if it is said: The distinction [between] such as father and son, cause and effect, and all correlates like two brothers is necessary and cannot be denied. We say: What is necessary is the distinction between the two essences; but with the description of relation, they are not existent, and distinction according to them is among the properties of the existent. And by something like this is repelled what is said that the definition of the two things does not include substance with accident, nor capability with action, due to lack of separation, and that is because they, in terms of essence, can be separated in intellection, even in external [existence] as well, by this substance existing without this accident and vice versa, especially according to one who says that accidents do not persist, and that this action occurs by the creation of Allah necessarily without the servant's capability, and that by this capability other than this action occurs, especially according to one who says that capability is suitable for opposites.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage begins by noting that the world, as an effect of God, is a relative entity (mudaf). It then presents an objection against those who define two things (al-ghayrayn) as distinct entities with potential separation. The objector argues that correlates like cause and effect are inseparable in the mind, so they cannot be distinct. The response distinguishes between the essence of the two things and their relational aspect: as correlates, they are not existent; only the essences are existent and distinct. The passage then applies this to the definition of al-ghayrayn, showing it includes substance and accident because essence-wise they are separable both mentally and externally. This refutes the claim that the definition fails due to inseparability. The discussion uses examples from atomism (non-persistence of accidents) and the Ash'ari view of capability (istita'a).

Correction: Correlates are distinct in essence, but not in their relational aspect. Otherness applies to existing essences, not to relations.

?

Definition of Otherness as the Opposite of Identity
Defines al-ghayriyya as the opposite of al-huwiyya with logical explanation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical definition of 'otherness' (al-ghayriyya) as the opposite of identity (al-huwiyya) and how the majority of scholars explain that two things are either identical or other, based on a single consideration.

Core Idea
Otherness is simply the negation of identity, not a positive relation.

Before discussing complex theological topics like the attributes of Allah, theologians must first establish clear definitions for basic logical concepts. One such concept is 'otherness' (al-ghayriyya), which is crucial for understanding how two things can be distinct. In this passage, the author defines otherness as the opposite of identity and explains the majority view on how otherness is determined.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully, noting the key terms: al-ghayriyya and al-huwiyya. Try to grasp the logical structure before reading the translation.
2
Understand the definition
The passage begins by stating that otherness is the opposite of identity. This is a simple negation: if two things are not identical, they are other.
Note that this is a definition by opposition.
3
Examine the two conditions
The passage then explains two cases: when one thing is verified to be the same as the other (huwa huwa) and when it is not. In the first case, there is no otherness; in the second, otherness is established.
Notice the phrase 'fahuwa huwa' which indicates that if one is not the other, then it is itself — hence other.
4
Reflect on the single consideration
The passage ends with 'bi-dhat al-i'tibar' (by the same consideration), emphasizing that otherness is determined under a single aspect. This prevents confusion when the same thing is considered under different aspects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الغيرية نقيض الهوية قال الجمهور على أن الغيرية نقيض الهوية هو فلا يعقل كون شيئين في بادىء الرأي فإن تحقق في ذينك المنظور فيهما أن أحدهما هو نفس الآخر وهو معنى قولهم هو هو فلا مغايرة ولا تعدد وإن تحقق أن أحدهما ليس نفس الآخر فهو هو فلا غيرية بينهما بذات الاعتبار
Otherness is the opposite of identity. He said: The majority hold that otherness is the opposite of identity. So it is not conceivable that there be two things in the first impression. For if it is verified in those two considered things that one of them is the same as the other, and that is the meaning of their saying 'it is it', then there is no distinction and no multiplicity; and if it is verified that one of them is not the same as the other, then it is it, so there is no otherness between them in terms of the same consideration.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage defines a fundamental concept: otherness (al-ghayriyya) is the opposite of identity (al-huwiyya). It cites the majority (jumhur) view. The explanation: when two things are considered, if one is exactly the other (huwa huwa), there is no otherness and no multiplicity. If one is not the other, then there is otherness. The phrase 'then it is it' (fahuwa huwa) is tricky: it means if one is not the same, then that one is itself, not the other — thus otherness is established. The passage emphasizes that this judgment is based on a single consideration (bi-dhat al-i'tibar). This logical distinction is foundational for discussions of divine attributes and the Trinity-like debates, but here it serves to clarify the basic meaning of otherness.

Key Terms
الغيرية
الهوية
نقيض
المغايرة
التعدد
الاعتبار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Otherness is a positive relation between two things.
Correction: Otherness is actually the negation of identity, meaning two things are not the same.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does 'otherness' (al-ghayriyya) mean?
A) A positive relation between two existents.
B) The opposite of identity, meaning one thing is not the other.
C) The separability of two things in existence.
D) The multiplicity of entities in the world.

Key Takeaway
Otherness is the opposite of identity: two things are other if, under a single consideration, one is not the same as the other.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Consider your own self: are you ever unsure whether your identity is distinct from your actions? Reflect on how the logical principle of otherness applies to your sense of self. When you identify with a sin, do you become that sin? Or are you other than your actions? How does this lesson force you to separate your identity from your deeds, and what commitment does that require of you today?

Think of a relationship where you have confused another person's identity with your own expectations. Does the principle of otherness require you to see them as truly other, not as an extension of yourself? What one change will you make to respect that otherness?
?
The lesson says otherness is the opposite of identity. In your prayer, do you sometimes fall into the assumption that your perception of Allah is identical to His reality? How does the logical truth that otherness is relative to consideration humble you in worship? What specific step will you take to ensure your worship is based on recognizing His absolute otherness?

5 min
Otherness as the Opposite of Identity
Defines al-ghayriyya as opposite of al-huwiyya and refutes two excuses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of otherness (al-ghayriyya) as the opposite of identity (al-huwiyya) and why it is impossible for two things to be neither identical nor other.

Core Idea
Otherness is the opposite of identity; two things cannot be neither.

This passage begins by establishing the majority view that otherness is the logical opposite of identity. It immediately confronts a potential objection: what about things that seem neither identical nor other? We will see why this cannot be the case.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of otherness
Read the opening lines carefully. The passage states that otherness is the opposite of identity and that it is inconceivable for two things to be neither identical nor other.
2
Understand the two excuses
The passage mentions two excuses offered for why some might think things can be neither: a terminological convention and a conceptual interpretation. Notice how each is framed.
The first excuse claims it's just a convention; the second says 'not it' refers to concept and 'not other' to identity.
3
See how the excuses are refuted
The passage provides a refutation for each excuse. The first is invalidated by the argument that if the one were other than ten, it would be other than itself. The second fails because the discussion includes parts and attributes that are not predicated.
Pay attention to the examples: the one and ten, and power/knowledge with the essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الغيرية نقيض الهوية قال : الجمهور على أن الغيرية نقيض الهوية هو فلا يعقل كون الشيء مع الشيء لا هو ولا غيره بل الغيران هما الاثنان من حيث إن أحدهما ليس هو الآخر، واعتذر بأنه اصطلاح أو المراد لا هو بحسب المفهوم، ولا غيره بحسب الهوية، كما في الحمل، ويبطل الأول استدلالهم الفاسد، بأن الواحد لو كان غير العشرة لكان غير نفسه لأنه من العشرة، والثاني أن الكلام في الأجزاء والصفات الغير المحمولة كالواحد مع العشرة، والقدرة والعلم مع الذات . الهوية هو بمعنى أن الشيء بالنسبة إلى الشيء إن صدق أنه هو هو فعينه ، وإن لم يصدق فغيره إن كان بحسب المفهوم كما في نسبة الإنسان إلى البشر، والناطق فبحسب المفهوم، وإن كان بحسب الذات والهوية، كما في نسبة الإنسان إلى الكاتب والحجر، فبحسب الذات والهوية، وما ذهبوا إليه من أن الجزء بالنسبة إلى الكل، والصفة بالنسبة الى الموصوف ليس عينه ولا غيره، ليس بمعقول لكونه ارتفاعاً للنقيضين، نعم في الغيرية إضافة بها، يصير أخص من النقيض بحسب المفهوم، لأن الغيرين هما الاثنان من حيث أن أحدهما ليس هو الآخر، إلا أنها حيثية لازمة في نفس الأمر، ربما يشعر بها مفهوم النقيض أيضاً، فلذا أطلقوا القول بأن الغيرية نقيض هو هو وبأن الغيرين هما الاثنان والشيئان . واعتذر الإمام الرازي عما ذكره المتكلمون من أن الشيء بالنسبة إلى الشيء قد يكون لا عينه ولا غيره بأنه اصطلاح على تخصيص لفظ الغيرين بما يجوز انفكاكهما كما خص العرف لفظ الدابة بذوات الأربع .
Otherness (al-ghayriyya) is the opposite of identity (al-huwiyya). He said: The majority (al-jumhur) hold that otherness is the opposite of identity, so it is not conceivable that a thing with another thing is neither it nor other than it; rather, the two others (al-ghayran) are the two (al-ithnan) inasmuch as one of them is not the other. And an excuse was given that it is a convention or that what is meant is 'not it' in terms of concept, and 'not other' in terms of identity, as in predication. And the first [excuse] is invalidated by their false argument that if the one were other than the ten, it would be other than itself because it is part of the ten; and the second [excuse] is [invalidated] because the discussion concerns parts and attributes that are not predicated, like the one with the ten, and power and knowledge with the essence. Identity means that a thing in relation to a thing: if it is true that it is it, then it is its very self; and if it is not true, then it is other, whether that is in terms of concept, as in the relation of human to human, and rational—so in terms of concept; or in terms of essence and identity, as in the relation of human to writer and stone—so in terms of essence and identity. And what they have held—that the part in relation to the whole, and the attribute in relation to the subject, is neither its very self nor other—is not intelligible because it is a raising of the two contradictories. Yes, in otherness there is a relation by which it becomes more specific than the opposite in terms of concept, because the two others are the two inasmuch as one of them is not the other, except that it is a necessary respect in reality, which perhaps the concept of the opposite also indicates. Therefore, they made the general statement that otherness is the opposite of 'huwa huwa' and that the two others are the two and the two things. And Imam al-Razi excused what the theologians mentioned—that a thing in relation to a thing may be neither its very self nor other—by saying that it is a convention to restrict the term 'al-ghayran' to that which can separate, just as custom restricted the term 'dabba' to quadrupeds.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of otherness (al-ghayriyya) and establishes its logical relationship with identity (al-huwiyya). It begins by stating the majority position: otherness is the opposite (naqid) of identity, meaning that for any two things, it is impossible that they are neither identical nor different. Rather, two things are other (ghayran) if one is not the other. The text then discusses an excuse offered by some that this is merely a terminological convention, or that it refers to conceptual non-identity (like in predication where the predicate is not the subject in concept). This excuse is rejected. The first interpretation (convention) is invalidated because it leads to a false argument: if the one were other than ten (of which it is a part), it would be other than itself. The second interpretation (conceptual) fails because the discussion involves parts and attributes that are not predicated. Next, the passage gives a precise definition of identity: a thing in relation to another is either its very self (aynuhu) or other (ghayruhu), and this can be in terms of concept (mafhum) or essence (dhat)/identity (huwiyya). Examples: human and human (same in concept), rational and human (same in concept), human and writer (different in essence). The view that part-whole and attribute-subject are neither identical nor other is declared unintelligible because it violates the law of non-contradiction—it would be a raising of the two contradictories (raf' al-naqidayn). The passage then explains that otherness, when considered with a relation, becomes more specific than the mere logical opposite, because it adds the condition that the two things are considered as two inasmuch as one is not the other. This necessary relation is inherent. Finally, Imam al-Razi offers a terminological excuse: the theologians used the word 'al-ghayran' specifically for things that can separate (like separable attributes), analogous to the conventional restriction of the word 'dabba' to quadrupeds. Thus, the apparent paradox of things being neither identical nor other is resolved by recognizing a specialized terminology, not a logical impossibility.

Key Terms
الغيرية
الهوية
نقيض
الغيران
ارتفاع النقيضين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that two things can be neither identical nor other (e.g., part and whole).
Correction: The passage asserts that this is inconceivable because otherness is the opposite of identity; it is a violation of the law of non-contradiction.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the first excuse given for the possibility of things being neither identical nor other?
A) It is a matter of convention.
B) It is a conceptual distinction.
C) It is due to the imperfection of language.
D) It is a special case for parts and attributes.

Key Takeaway
Otherness is the opposite of identity; nothing can be between them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about Allah's attributes like His power and knowledge, do you ever subconsciously treat them as separate from His essence? How does this passage's insistence on either identity or otherness challenge you to reconsider the way you conceive of Allah's oneness?
?
In your daily decisions, do you ever find yourself accepting contradictions—like wanting both obedience and sin, or claiming to trust Allah while holding back? This passage says nothing can be neither identical nor other. Where in your heart are you living in that forbidden middle ground?
?
The first excuse dismissed logical distinctions as mere terminology. Are there areas of your faith where you use convenient labels to avoid facing hard truths? What would it cost you to embrace the uncompromising clarity of tawheed this passage demands?

Identity, Part-Whole, and al-Razi's Excuse
Clarifies identity as either conceptual or essential, refutes part-whole/attribute view, and gives al-Razi's terminological excuse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two types of identity (conceptual and essential) and how al-Razi resolves the apparent paradox of things being neither identical nor other through terminological convention.

Core Idea
Identity can be conceptual or essential; al-Razi's excuse saves the language.

Having established the logical impossibility of neither-nor, the passage now clarifies what identity means. It then addresses the specific case of part-whole and attribute-subject, and finally offers Imam al-Razi's terminological solution to the paradox.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two types of identity
The passage explains that a thing in relation to another can be identical either in concept (mafhum) or in essence (dhat/huwiyya). Examples are given for each.
Human and human are identical in concept; human and writer are different in essence.
2
Refutation of the part-whole view
The passage rejects the claim that a part in relation to a whole, or an attribute to its subject, is neither identical nor other. This is deemed unintelligible because it raises the two contradictories.
The law of non-contradiction is invoked.
3
Al-Razi's terminological excuse
Imam al-Razi excuses the theologians' statements about things being neither identical nor other by saying they used the term 'al-ghayran' in a specialized sense, restricted to things that can separate, analogous to the word 'dabba' for quadrupeds.
This resolves the paradox without contradicting logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الغيرية نقيض الهوية قال : الجمهور على أن الغيرية نقيض الهوية هو فلا يعقل كون الشيء مع الشيء لا هو ولا غيره بل الغيران هما الاثنان من حيث إن أحدهما ليس هو الآخر، واعتذر بأنه اصطلاح أو المراد لا هو بحسب المفهوم، ولا غيره بحسب الهوية، كما في الحمل، ويبطل الأول استدلالهم الفاسد، بأن الواحد لو كان غير العشرة لكان غير نفسه لأنه من العشرة، والثاني أن الكلام في الأجزاء والصفات الغير المحمولة كالواحد مع العشرة، والقدرة والعلم مع الذات . الهوية هو بمعنى أن الشيء بالنسبة إلى الشيء إن صدق أنه هو هو فعينه ، وإن لم يصدق فغيره إن كان بحسب المفهوم كما في نسبة الإنسان إلى البشر، والناطق فبحسب المفهوم، وإن كان بحسب الذات والهوية، كما في نسبة الإنسان إلى الكاتب والحجر، فبحسب الذات والهوية، وما ذهبوا إليه من أن الجزء بالنسبة إلى الكل، والصفة بالنسبة الى الموصوف ليس عينه ولا غيره، ليس بمعقول لكونه ارتفاعاً للنقيضين، نعم في الغيرية إضافة بها، يصير أخص من النقيض بحسب المفهوم، لأن الغيرين هما الاثنان من حيث أن أحدهما ليس هو الآخر، إلا أنها حيثية لازمة في نفس الأمر، ربما يشعر بها مفهوم النقيض أيضاً، فلذا أطلقوا القول بأن الغيرية نقيض هو هو وبأن الغيرين هما الاثنان والشيئان . واعتذر الإمام الرازي عما ذكره المتكلمون من أن الشيء بالنسبة إلى الشيء قد يكون لا عينه ولا غيره بأنه اصطلاح على تخصيص لفظ الغيرين بما يجوز انفكاكهما كما خص العرف لفظ الدابة بذوات الأربع .
Otherness (al-ghayriyya) is the opposite of identity (al-huwiyya). He said: The majority (al-jumhur) hold that otherness is the opposite of identity, so it is not conceivable that a thing with another thing is neither it nor other than it; rather, the two others (al-ghayran) are the two (al-ithnan) inasmuch as one of them is not the other. And an excuse was given that it is a convention or that what is meant is 'not it' in terms of concept, and 'not other' in terms of identity, as in predication. And the first [excuse] is invalidated by their false argument that if the one were other than the ten, it would be other than itself because it is part of the ten; and the second [excuse] is [invalidated] because the discussion concerns parts and attributes that are not predicated, like the one with the ten, and power and knowledge with the essence. Identity means that a thing in relation to a thing: if it is true that it is it, then it is its very self; and if it is not true, then it is other, whether that is in terms of concept, as in the relation of human to human, and rational—so in terms of concept; or in terms of essence and identity, as in the relation of human to writer and stone—so in terms of essence and identity. And what they have held—that the part in relation to the whole, and the attribute in relation to the subject, is neither its very self nor other—is not intelligible because it is a raising of the two contradictories. Yes, in otherness there is a relation by which it becomes more specific than the opposite in terms of concept, because the two others are the two inasmuch as one of them is not the other, except that it is a necessary respect in reality, which perhaps the concept of the opposite also indicates. Therefore, they made the general statement that otherness is the opposite of 'huwa huwa' and that the two others are the two and the two things. And Imam al-Razi excused what the theologians mentioned—that a thing in relation to a thing may be neither its very self nor other—by saying that it is a convention to restrict the term 'al-ghayran' to that which can separate, just as custom restricted the term 'dabba' to quadrupeds.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of otherness (al-ghayriyya) and establishes its logical relationship with identity (al-huwiyya). It begins by stating the majority position: otherness is the opposite (naqid) of identity, meaning that for any two things, it is impossible that they are neither identical nor different. Rather, two things are other (ghayran) if one is not the other. The text then discusses an excuse offered by some that this is merely a terminological convention, or that it refers to conceptual non-identity (like in predication where the predicate is not the subject in concept). This excuse is rejected. The first interpretation (convention) is invalidated because it leads to a false argument: if the one were other than ten (of which it is a part), it would be other than itself. The second interpretation (conceptual) fails because the discussion involves parts and attributes that are not predicated. Next, the passage gives a precise definition of identity: a thing in relation to another is either its very self (aynuhu) or other (ghayruhu), and this can be in terms of concept (mafhum) or essence (dhat)/identity (huwiyya). Examples: human and human (same in concept), rational and human (same in concept), human and writer (different in essence). The view that part-whole and attribute-subject are neither identical nor other is declared unintelligible because it violates the law of non-contradiction—it would be a raising of the two contradictories (raf' al-naqidayn). The passage then explains that otherness, when considered with a relation, becomes more specific than the mere logical opposite, because it adds the condition that the two things are considered as two inasmuch as one is not the other. This necessary relation is inherent. Finally, Imam al-Razi offers a terminological excuse: the theologians used the word 'al-ghayran' specifically for things that can separate (like separable attributes), analogous to the conventional restriction of the word 'dabba' to quadrupeds. Thus, the apparent paradox of things being neither identical nor other is resolved by recognizing a specialized terminology, not a logical impossibility.

Key Terms
الهوية
الجزء
الكل
الصفة
الموصوف
اعتذار الإمام الرازي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that a part and its whole, or an attribute and its subject, are neither identical nor other.
Correction: The passage says this is unintelligible because it is a raising of the two contradictories; everything must be either identical or other.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how does Imam al-Razi excuse the theologians' statements about things being neither identical nor other?
A) He says it is a logical mistake.
B) He says it is a terminological convention to restrict 'otherness' to things that can separate.
C) He says it applies only to conceptual identity.
D) He says it is a mystical insight.

Key Takeaway
Identity is either conceptual or essential; apparent exceptions are resolved by terminological precision.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own identity as a Muslim, are there areas of your life—habits, sins, relationships—that you treat as 'neither truly you nor truly other'? How does this lesson's insistence on clear identity (either it is you or it is not) challenge you to take ownership of every part of your life before Allah?
?
Al-Razi's excuse reveals that sometimes we hide behind vague terms to avoid logical consequences. In your worship and repentance, do you ever use vague language to excuse half-heartedness? Will you commit to being precise with yourself and with Allah about what you truly are and what you are not?
?
The law of non-contradiction exposes that a thing cannot be both identical and not identical. Are there beliefs you hold that contradict the very foundations of your faith? How will you examine your heart and actions to ensure they are consistent with the truth you profess?

Identity and Otherness: Contradictories in Kalam
Defines identity and otherness and refutes a mutakallimun claim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of identity and otherness in kalam and why the mutakallimun's claim that a part is neither identical to nor different from the whole is logically invalid.

Core Idea
Identity and otherness are contradictories; there is no third option.

This passage from a classical kalam text lays the groundwork for understanding relational concepts. Before engaging in deeper theological debates, we must have a clear vocabulary for sameness and difference. The author defines identity and otherness, then critiques a claim by some mutakallimun that a part can be neither identical to nor different from the whole—showing why that position is logically impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation without analyzing. Get a feel for the author's concise style.
Notice that the passage has three clear sections: definition, criticism, and clarification.
2
Identify the two types of identity
Find where the author explains that identity (العين) can be either in concept (بحسب المفهوم) or in essence (بحسب الذات والهوية). Understand examples like insan and bashar (identical in concept) vs. insan and katib (identical in essence).
3
Understand the criticism of the mutakallimun
Read the author's objection: claiming something is neither identical nor other is unintelligible because it removes two contradictories. Ask yourself: Why must identity and otherness be exhaustive?
The author uses the principle of non-contradiction.
4
Grasp the nuance about otherness vs. contradiction
The author says otherness (الغيرية) is more specific than mere contradiction because it requires a relation between two distinct things. Contradiction may also imply otherness, but otherness adds relationality.
This refines the definition to avoid misunderstandings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الهوية هو بمعنى أن الشيء بالنسبة إلى الشيء إن صدق أنه هو هو فعينه، وإن لم يصدق فغيره إن كان بحسب المفهوم كما في نسبة الإنسان إلى البشر، والناطق فبحسب المفهوم، وإن كان بحسب الذات والهوية، كما في نسبة الإنسان إلى الكاتب والحجر، فبحسب الذات والهوية، وما ذهبوا إليه من أن الجزء بالنسبة إلى الكل، والصفة بالنسبة الى الموصوف ليس عينه ولا غيره، ليس بمعقول لكونه ارتفاعاً للنقيضين، نعم في الغيرية إضافة بها، يصير أخص من النقيض بحسب المفهوم، لأن الغيرين هما الاثنان من حيث أن أحدهما ليس هو الآخر، إلا أنها حيثية لازمة في نفس الأمر، ربما يشعر بها مفهوم النقيض أيضاً، فلذا أطلقوا القول بأن الغيرية نقيض هو هو وبأن الغيرين هما الاثنان والشيئان.
Al-huwiyya (identity) means that a thing in relation to another thing: if it is true that it is identical to it, then it is its very self (ayn); if not, then it is other (ghayr) if it is according to concept (bi-hasab al-mafhum), as in the relation of insan (human) to bashar (mankind), and natiq (rational) is according to concept, and if according to essence (dhat) and identity (huwiyya), as in the relation of insan to katib (writer) and hajar (stone), then it is according to essence and identity. And what they held—that the part in relation to the whole, and the attribute in relation to the subject, is neither identical to it nor other—is not intelligible because it is the removal of two contradictories. Indeed, in otherness (ghayriyya) there is a relation that makes it more specific than contradiction according to concept, because the two others (al-ghayran) are the two things from the perspective that one of them is not the other, except that it is a necessary aspect in reality, perhaps the concept of contradiction also suggests it, so they said that otherness is the contradictory of identity and that the two others are the two things and the two entities (al-shay'an).
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage defines the key terms 'ayn (identity) and ghayr (otherness) in relation to two things. The author first explains that a thing relative to another can be identical in concept (like insan and bashar) or in essence (like insan and a writer), or different. Then he criticizes the mutakallimun's claim that a part relative to the whole, or an attribute relative to its subject, is neither identical nor different. The author says this is unintelligible because it would eliminate both identity and difference, which are contradictories. He then clarifies that otherness (ghayriyya) is more specific than simple contradiction, because it requires that two things are distinct such that one is not the other. This nuanced definition shows that the mutakallimun's middle position is logically impossible.

العين
الغير
الغيرية

الجزء

الموصوف
المفهوم
