Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That a part can be neither identical to nor different from the whole (a middle state).
Correction: In kalam logic, identity and otherness are exhaustive contradictories. Claiming 'neither' is logically impossible because it removes both contradictories.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the author's main objection to the mutakallimun's claim that a part relative to the whole is neither identical nor different?
A) It contradicts the Qur'an
B) It is unintelligible because it removes two contradictories
C) It was rejected by most scholars
D) It applies only to accidents

Key Takeaway
Identity and otherness are contradictories; therefore, the mutakallimun's claim of a middle state between them is logically impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own thinking, have you ever held a belief that something was 'neither true nor false' to avoid a difficult decision? How does this lesson's rejection of a middle state between identity and otherness challenge you to confront such intellectual evasion?
?
The mutakallimun's error was refusing to accept that a part must be either identical or different from its whole. Is there a relationship in your life—with a person, a doctrine, or a habit—where you have refused to admit whether it is truly a part of you or something else? What would change if you committed to one side?
?
If the law of excluded middle applies even to subtle relationships like part-whole and attribute-subject, then what areas of your faith—perhaps concerning Allah's attributes or the nature of the Quran—do you need to reexamine with rigorous logic instead of settling for vague ambiguity?

Al-Razi's Apology: A Terminological Convention
Al-Razi's linguistic apology for the mutakallimun's claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's defense that the mutakallimun's phrase 'neither identical nor other' is a terminological convention, not a logical contradiction.

Core Idea
Language can be conventional; the mutakallimun's claim is a special term.

Al-Razi offers a simple defense: the mutakallimun are using a technical term, not making a paradoxical statement. He compares it to how 'dabba' is conventionally restricted to quadrupeds. Let's examine his apology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once and its translation. Notice the structure: al-Razi's apology is a direct response to the mutakallimun's claim.
2
Identify the key claim
The mutakallimun say a thing can be neither identical nor other. Al-Razi says this is a convention. Understand the difference.
3
Understand the analogy
Al-Razi compares their use of 'ghayran' to how 'dabba' is restricted to quadrupeds. Reflect on how conventions work in language.
4
Consider the rejection
The author later declares all apologies false. Think about why a linguistic convention might not resolve the philosophical issue.
This connects to the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعتذر الإمام الرازي عما ذكره المتكلمون من أن الشيء بالنسبة إلى الشيء قد يكون لا عينه ولا غيره بأنه اصطلاح على تخصيص لفظ الغيرين بما يجوز انفكاكهما كما خص العرف لفظ الدابة بذوات الأربع. كما هو وصاحب المواقف بأن معناه أنه لا هو بحسب المفهوم، ولا غيره بحسب الهوية، الواجب في الحمل إذ لو كان المحمول غير الموضوع بحسب الهوية لم يصح الحمل، ولو كان عينه بحسب المفهوم لم يفد، بل لم يصح أيضاً لامتناع النسبة بدون الاثنينية فمن قال بالوجود الذهني صرح بأنهما متحدان في الخارج متغايران في الذهن، ومن لم يقل لم يصرح به، بل قال: لا عين، ولا غير، لأن المعلوم قطعاً اتحاد أنه لا بد بينهما من هو من وجه، واختلاف من وجه وإما أن ذلك في الخارج، وهذا في الذهن، فلا، وكلا الاعتذارين فاسد.
Imam al-Razi apologized for what the mutakallimun mentioned—that a thing in relation to a thing may be neither its very self nor other—by saying that it is a convention to restrict the term 'two others' (al-ghayran) to what can be separated, just as custom restricted the term 'dabba' to quadrupeds. Likewise, the author of al-Mawaqif apologized that its meaning is that it is not identical in concept, nor other in identity (huwiyya), which is necessary in predication (haml); because if the predicate were other than the subject in identity, predication would be invalid, and if it were identical in concept, it would not benefit, rather it would also be invalid due to the impossibility of relation without duality. So whoever affirms mental existence declared that they are united externally and different in the mind, and whoever does not affirm it did not declare that, but said: not identical and not other, because it is certainly known that there must be between them identity in one respect and difference in another; either that is in the external and this in the mind, or not. Both apologies are false.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage presents two apologies for the mutakallimun's claim that a part or attribute is neither identical nor different. Imam al-Razi argues that it is a terminological convention, like how 'dabba' is restricted to quadrupeds. The author of al-Mawaqif offers a metaphysical explanation: in predication, the predicate must be different in concept but identical in identity, otherwise predication fails. He refers to the theory of mental existence where things are united externally but distinct mentally. However, the author concludes that both apologies are false, setting up the refutation.

Key Terms
اعتذار
اصطلاح
الغيرين
الانفكاك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is al-Razi's explanation for the mutakallimun's phrase?
A) It is a logical necessity
B) It is a terminological convention
C) It refers to mental existence
D) It is a mistake in reasoning

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi defends the mutakallimun's claim as a special term, not a logical contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Al-Razi defended a position by clarifying that it was a conventional term. Have you ever used specialized language that others misunderstood? How will you be more precise and charitable in your own speech about matters of creed?
?
The mutakallimun's phrase 'neither identical nor other' is a technical term. Are you willing to learn the technical language of Islamic theology rather than dismissing it as contradictory? What specific term will you look up and understand this week?

Al-Mawaqif's Apology and Its Refutation
Al-Mawaqif's apology and the author's rejection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Mawaqif's metaphysical apology based on concept vs. identity and why the author rejects both apologies.

Core Idea
The predicate differs in concept but is identical in identity—yet this too fails.

Al-Mawaqif offers a deeper explanation rooted in predication theory. He distinguishes between conceptual difference and identity in reality, referencing mental existence. But the author concludes both apologies are false. Let's see why.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully. It contains al-Mawaqif's apology and the author's verdict.
2
Understand the distinction
Al-Mawaqif says the thing is not identical in concept (mafhūm) nor other in identity (huwiyya). Grasp this distinction.
3
Follow the predication argument
He argues that in predication (haml), the predicate must differ in concept but be identical in identity. Consider why this is necessary.
Think about the logical conditions for a valid predication.
4
Note the role of mental existence
Those who affirm mental existence say things are united externally but distinct mentally. See how this relates.
5
Absorb the refutation
The author explicitly says both apologies are false. This is the key takeaway.
The refutation is not elaborated, but the declaration is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Imam al-Razi apologized for what the mutakallimun mentioned—that a thing in relation to a thing may be neither its very self nor other—by saying that it is a convention to restrict the term 'two others' (al-ghayran) to what can be separated, just as custom restricted the term 'dabba' to quadrupeds. Both apologies are false.

Commentary
This passage presents two apologies for the mutakallimun's claim that a part or attribute is neither identical nor different. Imam al-Razi argues that it is a terminological convention, like how 'dabba' is restricted to quadrupeds. The author of al-Mawaqif offers a metaphysical explanation: in predication, the predicate must be different in concept but identical in identity, otherwise predication fails. He refers to the theory of mental existence where things are united externally but distinct mentally. However, the author concludes that both apologies are false, setting up the refutation.

?

Refutation of the First Apology's Proof
Refutes the claim that part-whole otherness leads to self-otherness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the argument that a part cannot be distinct from the whole without self-contradiction is fallacious.

Core Idea
Otherness from the whole does not mean otherness from every part.

The first apology attempts to prove that parts cannot be distinct from wholes using a logical argument. The author shows this argument is based on a misunderstanding of the relationship between part and whole. We will examine the proof and its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the false proof
Read the first part of the passage where the opponent argues that if a part were other than the whole, it would be other than itself. Notice the example of ten and one.
2
Identify the refutation
The author states that otherness from a thing does not entail otherness from every part. This is the key refutation. See how it undermines the proof.
3
Note the historical aside
The author mentions that only Ja'far ibn Hirith held the view that one is other than ten. This illustrates the weakness of the opposing position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الأول. فلأن منهم من حاول إثبات ذلك بالدليل. فقال لو كان الجزء غير الكل لكان غير نفسه لأن العشرة مثلاً اسم لجميع الأفراد متناول كل فرد أغياره فلو كان الواحد غير العشرة لصار غير نفسه لأنه من العشرة وأن تكون العشرة بدونه وكذا اليد من زيد وبطلان هذا الكلام ظاهر، لأن مغايرة الشيء للشيء لا يقتضي مغايرته لكل جزء من أجزائه حتى يلزم مغايرته لنفسه، وزعم هذا القائل أن هذا الدليل قطعي وأن القول بكون الواحد غير العشرة فاسد لم يقل به أحد إلا جعفر بن حرث من المعتزلة وعد هذا من جهالاته. وأما الثاني. فلأن الكلام في الأجزاء والصفات الغير المحمولة، كالواحد من العشرة واليد من زيد والعلم مع الذات والقدرة مع الذات، ونحو ذلك مما لا يتصور اتحادهما بحسب الوجود والهوية. التغاير يكفي فيه الانفكاك قال: وبعضهم على أنه لا بد في عدم مغايرة الشيئين من امتناع كل
As for the first: because some of them tried to prove that by evidence. He said: if the part were other than the whole, it would be other than itself, because 'ten' for example is a name for all the individuals, encompassing each individual of its others; so if the one were other than ten, it would become other than itself because it is from ten, and that ten is without it. Similarly, the hand from Zayd. The falsity of this statement is apparent, because the otherness of a thing from a thing does not necessitate its otherness from every part of its parts such that its otherness from itself would follow. And this claimant claimed that this evidence is certain and that the statement that the one is other than ten is false; no one said it except Ja'far ibn Hirith from the Mu'tazila, and he counted this among his ignorances. As for the second: because the discussion is about parts and attributes that are not predicated (ghayr al-mahmula), such as the one from ten, the hand from Zayd, knowledge with the essence, power with the essence, and similar things where their union in existence and identity is inconceivable. Otherness suffices in [؟] disjunction (al-infikak). He said: and some [claim] that in the non-otherness of two things, it is necessary that every [؟]
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage provides the detailed refutation of both apologies. For the first apology, the author examines the proof that if a part (like the number one) were different from the whole (ten), it would differ from itself because it is part of the whole. The author shows this reasoning is fallacious: otherness from the whole does not imply otherness from every part, so no self-contradiction follows. He also notes that the proponent of this proof claimed it is certain and that only Ja'far ibn Hirith of the Mu'tazila held the contrary view. For the second apology, the author argues that the discussion concerns non-predicated parts and attributes (e.g., one and ten, hand and Zayd, knowledge and the essence), where union in existence is impossible, so the analogy with predication fails. The passage ends with an incomplete remark about otherness requiring disjunction, which likely continues the refutation.

الأجزاء
جعفر بن حرث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof that part-whole otherness leads to self-contradiction is logically sound.
Correction: Otherness from the whole does not necessitate otherness from every part, so the self-contradiction does not follow.

Checkpoint
What is the author's key refutation of the first apology's proof?
A) The proof is based on a grammatical error.
B) Otherness from a thing does not imply otherness from every part.
C) The whole is not a collection of parts.
D) The proof is based on a misunderstanding of numbers.

Key Takeaway
The argument that part-whole otherness implies self-otherness fails because otherness is not transitive across parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The claimant in the passage considered his proof certain and dismissed contrary views as ignorance. Are there areas in your life where you are too quick to label opposing views as baseless? What would it take to examine them with the same careful logic as the author?

Refutation of the Second Apology's Application
Refutes applying part-whole reasoning to non-predicated attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the part-whole argument does not apply to non-predicated attributes and how otherness can be based on disjunction.

Core Idea
Non-predicated attributes cannot be united in existence, so otherness is given.

The second apology attempts to use the part-whole argument on attributes that are not predicated, like knowledge and the essence. The author explains that such attributes cannot be united in existence, so otherness is already established by conceptual disjunction. We will examine this distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify non-predicated attributes
The passage lists examples like the one from ten, the hand from Zayd, and knowledge with the essence. These are not predicated in the way that a part is predicated of a whole.
2
Understand the criterion for otherness
The author states that for such things, otherness suffices through disjunction (al-infikak). This means they are conceptually distinct even if not separate in existence.
3
Note the incomplete argument
The passage ends abruptly, but the context suggests that the author is rejecting the requirement for mutual separation in existence for otherness.

Refuting the Claim that the Part is Identical to the Whole
The author refutes an argument that the part cannot be other than the whole using the example of ten and one.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical fallacy in the argument that if the part were other than the whole, it would be other than itself.

Core Idea
Being other than a whole does not make you other than every part.

This lesson examines a philosophical argument that attempts to prove the part and whole are identical by claiming that otherwise the part would contradict itself. The author exposes the flaw in this reasoning and demonstrates the correct logical relationship between part and whole.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
First, read the Arabic text and its literal translation to grasp the full argument presented in the passage.
2
Identify the initial claim
Notice the argument being refuted: that if the part is other than the whole, then the part would be other than itself. The example of ten and one illustrates this.
The argument uses the idea that 'ten' includes 'one', so if one is other than ten, it would be other than itself.
3
Understand the refutation
Pay attention to the author's rebuttal: being other than the whole does not mean being other than every part. The conclusion that the part would be other than itself does not follow.
The author calls the argument's falsity 'apparent'.
4
Note the sectarian mention
The author notes that only Ja'far ibn Harith of the Mu'tazila held that the part is other than the whole, indicating this was a minority view.
This contextualizes the debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكلا الاعتذارين فاسد. أما الأول. فلأن منهم من حاول إثبات ذلك بالدليل. فقال لو كان الجزء غير الكل لكان غير نفسه لأن العشرة مثلاً اسم لجميع الأفراد متناول كل فرد [بأغياره؟] فلو كان الواحد غير العشرة لصار غير نفسه لأنه من العشرة وأن تكون العشرة بدونه وكذا اليد من زيد وبطلان هذا الكلام ظاهر، لأن مغايرة الشيء للشيء لا يقتضي مغايرته لكل جزء من أجزائه حتى يلزم مغايرته لنفسه، وزعم هذا القائل أن هذا الدليل قطعي وأن القول بكون الواحد غير العشرة فاسد لم يقل به أحد إلا جعفر بن حرث من المعتزلة وعد هذا من جهالاته.
And both excuses are invalid. As for the first, because some of them attempted to prove that by evidence. He said: If the part were other than the whole, it would be other than itself because 'ten', for example, is a name for all individuals, encompassing each individual with its others. So if the one were other than ten, it would become other than itself because it is from the ten and that the ten is without it, and similarly the hand from Zayd. And the falsity of this statement is apparent, because a thing's being other than another thing does not necessitate its being other than every part of its parts such that it would necessitate its being other than itself. And this claimant claimed that this evidence is decisive and that the statement that the one is other than ten is corrupt and that no one has said it except Ja'far ibn Harith from the Mu'tazila, and he counted this among his ignorances.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage refutes a specific argument made by those who claim that the part cannot be other than the whole. The author first identifies the argument: if the part (e.g., the number one) were other than the whole (ten), then the part would be other than itself, because the part is part of the whole. The argument uses the example of ten and one: 'ten' is a name for all individuals including one, so if one were other than ten, then one would be other than itself because it is part of ten. Similarly, if the hand were other than Zayd, then the hand would be other than itself. The author dismisses this as obviously false, explaining that a thing being other than another thing does not require it to be other than every part of that thing. Therefore, the conclusion that the part is other than itself does not follow. The author also notes that the proponent of this argument claimed it was decisive and that only Ja'far ibn Harith of the Mu'tazila held that the part is other than the whole, calling this one of his foolish errors. The key point is that the argument commits a fallacy by conflating being other than the whole with being other than every part.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a part is other than the whole, it must be other than every part of that whole, leading to self-contradiction.
Correction: Being other than a whole only means the part is distinct from the aggregate, not from each individual component.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the argument that if the part is other than the whole, the part would be other than itself?
A) Because being other than the whole implies being other than every part, leading to contradiction.
B) Because being other than another thing does not require being other than every part of that thing, so no self-contradiction occurs.
C) Because the part and whole are actually identical in reality.
D) Because the example of ten and one is not applicable.

Key Takeaway
The logical distinction between being other than a whole and being other than each of its parts is essential to avoid fallacious reasoning about identity and difference.

The Two Competing Criteria for Difference
Author presents two views on the criterion for difference and an initial objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two competing criteria for when two things are other than each other: separation from one side versus mutual impossibility.

Core Idea
Difference requires mutual impossibility, not separation from one side.

In this passage, the author presents a debate about how to determine whether two things are truly distinct. Some scholars argue that for two things not to be different, each must be impossible without the other. Others hold that separation from one side suffices. We examine the initial arguments and objections.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation to get the flow of the argument.
Notice the two views are presented concisely.
2
Identify the two positions
Find where the author states the view that mutual impossibility is required, and where he states the alternative view that separation from one side is sufficient.
3
Understand the initial objection
The author objects that if separation from one side were enough, the part-whole and subject-attribute relations would violate it. That objection is then challenged by the mutual-impossibility proponents.
This sets up the detailed discussion in the next lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال وبعضهم على أنه لا بد في عدم مغايرة الشيئين من امتناع كل بدون الآخر كما في صفات القديم بخلاف مثل البياض مع الجسم وزعموا أن الواحد من العشرة يمتنع بدونها. ولا خفاء في ورود سائر الإضافات حينئذ. تنبيه: غير التغاير بين الذات والصفة إنما يدفع أزلية غير الذات لا تعدد الأزليات. قد سبق أنه لا يكفي في التغاير الانفكاك من جانب، وإلا انتقض بالجزء مع الكل والموصوف مع الصفة، وزعم بعضهم أنه كاف حتى إن عدم تغاير الشيئين إنما يتحقق إذا كان كل منهما بحيث يمتنع بدون الآخر، والنقض غير وارد. أما الموصوف مع الصفة فلأن عدم التغاير إنما هو في الصفات التي تمتنع الذات بدونها، كما تمتنع هي بدون الذات كصفات القديم، لامتناع العدم على القديم، وكذا الصفات بعضها مع بعض، بخلاف مثل البياض مع الجسم، فإنهما غيران. وأما الجزء مع الكل فلأنه، كما تمتنع العشرة بدون الواحد، كذلك الواحد من العشرة يمتنع بدونها، إذ لو وجد بدونها لم يكن واحداً من العشرة، وحاصله أن الجزء بوصف الجزئية يمتنع بدون الكل، وحينئذ ترد سائر الأمور الإضافية، كالأب مع الابن والأخ مع الأخ والصانع مع المصنوع، ويلزم ألا يكونا غيرين ويلزم أن لا يكون الغيران بل الضدان غيرين، لأن التغاير والتضاد من الإضافات، فإن التزموا ذلك بناء على أن الإضافة عدمية، ولا تمايز بين الإعدام.
He said: And some of them held that in the non-difference of two things, it is necessary that each be impossible without the other, as in the attributes of the Eternal, unlike something like whiteness with body. And they claimed that the one from ten is impossible without it. And it is not hidden that all other relations then apply. Notice: The difference between essence and attribute only repels the eternality of other than the essence, not a multiplicity of eternal things. It has already preceded that separation from one side is not sufficient for difference, otherwise it would be refuted by the part with the whole and the described with the attribute. And some claimed that it is sufficient, such that the non-difference of two things is only realized if each of them is such that it is impossible without the other, and the refutation is not applicable. As for the described with the attribute, non-difference is only in attributes which the essence is impossible without, just as they are impossible without the essence, like the attributes of the Eternal, due to the impossibility of non-existence upon the Eternal, and likewise some attributes with others, unlike something like whiteness with body, for they are other. And as for the part with the whole, just as ten is impossible without one, likewise the one from ten is impossible without it, for if it existed without it, it would not be one from ten. The upshot is that the part, under the description of part-hood, is impossible without the whole. Then all relational matters apply, such as father with son, brother with brother, and maker with made, and it would follow that they are not other, and it would follow that the two others, indeed the two opposites, are not other, because difference and opposition are among relations. If they accept that, they build it on the basis that relation is non-existent, and there is no distinction among non-existents.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage discusses the criterion for two things being 'other' (mutaghayir) or not. The author first reports a position: some argue that for two things not to be other than each other, each must be impossible without the other (mutual impossibility), using the example of God's eternal attributes (e.g., knowledge and power) which are inseparable from the essence. In contrast, whiteness is other than body because it can separate. These proponents claim that the number one is impossible without the number ten. The author notes that this leads to problems with all relational concepts (idadaat). A 'tanbih' (notice) clarifies that the issue of difference between essence and attribute only prevents the eternity of something other than the essence, not a plurality of eternal entities. The author then states the earlier teaching: separation from one side (infikak min janib) is insufficient for difference, otherwise it would be refuted by part-whole and subject-attribute relationships. Some claim that separation from one side is enough, so two things are not different only if each is impossible without the other. They argue the refutation does not apply: for subject-attribute, non-difference only occurs for attributes that the essence cannot exist without (like God's attributes), not for contingent attributes like whiteness. For part-whole, the part under the description of 'part' (e.g., one as part of ten) is impossible without the whole, so the whole and part are not other. But then the author points out a vicious consequence: all relational pairs (father-son, brother-brother, maker-made) would become not other, and even opposites would collapse, because difference and opposition are themselves relations. If they accept this, they must hold that relations are non-existent, leading to no distinction among non-existents, which is absurd. Thus, the author implicitly rejects the mutual-impossibility criterion as too broad.

Key Terms
التغاير
الانفكاك
الامتناع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the position reported first, what is required for two things not to be other than each other?
A) They must be separable from one side.
B) Each must be impossible without the other.
C) They must be identical in essence.
D) They must be part of the same whole.

Key Takeaway
The debate centers on whether separation from one side is enough to establish difference, with the author leaning toward the stricter mutual-impossibility criterion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You believe God's attributes are not other than His essence. But have you ever thought of them as separate 'parts' of God? How does the mutual-impossibility criterion force you to reconsider that assumption? What will you do to internalize this unity in your worship?
?
When you say 'Allah knows' and 'Allah is powerful,' do you feel you are referring to two different things? This lesson teaches that difference requires mutual impossibility. How does this affect your understanding of tawhid? Commit to reminding yourself of this unity in your daily prayers.
?
The objection from part-whole shows how easy it is to misunderstand criteria. Have you ever judged a theological issue based on a superficial similarity? This lesson challenges you to dig deeper. What specific step will you take to avoid surface-level reasoning in your study of aqeedah?

Divine Attributes: A Case Study in Mutual Impossibility
Author explains why eternal attributes are not other than the essence using mutual impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the mutual-impossibility criterion applies to the eternal attributes of God but not to contingent attributes like whiteness.

Core Idea
Eternal attributes are inseparable from God's essence; contingent attributes are not.

This lesson examines the application of the mutual-impossibility criterion to divine attributes. The author explains that the attributes of the Eternal (e.g., knowledge, power) are such that the essence cannot exist without them and they cannot exist without the essence. In contrast, whiteness is accidental to body and can be separated.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage focusing on the distinction
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the contrast between attributes of the Eternal and attributes like whiteness.
2
Understand the logic of mutual impossibility
For God's attributes, the essence is impossible without them (because God is essentially knowing, etc.), and they are impossible without the essence. This mutual dependence makes them not other.
This is the criterion of mutual impossibility.
3
Apply the distinction
Whiteness and body can exist separately, so they are other. The author uses this to show the criterion is not universal.
Contingent attributes do not share mutual impossibility with the subject.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال وبعضهم على أنه لا بد في عدم مغايرة الشيئين من امتناع كل بدون الآخر كما في صفات القديم بخلاف مثل البياض مع الجسم وزعموا أن الواحد من العشرة يمتنع بدونها. ولا خفاء في ورود سائر الإضافات حينئذ. تنبيه: غير التغاير بين الذات والصفة إنما يدفع أزلية غير الذات لا تعدد الأزليات. قد سبق أنه لا يكفي في التغاير الانفكاك من جانب، وإلا انتقض بالجزء مع الكل والموصوف مع الصفة، وزعم بعضهم أنه كاف حتى إن عدم تغاير الشيئين إنما يتحقق إذا كان كل منهما بحيث يمتنع بدون الآخر، والنقض غير وارد. أما الموصوف مع الصفة فلأن عدم التغاير إنما هو في الصفات التي تمتنع الذات بدونها، كما تمتنع هي بدون الذات كصفات القديم، لامتناع العدم على القديم، وكذا الصفات بعضها مع بعض، بخلاف مثل البياض مع الجسم، فإنهما غيران. وأما الجزء مع الكل فلأنه، كما تمتنع العشرة بدون الواحد، كذلك الواحد من العشرة يمتنع بدونها، إذ لو وجد بدونها لم يكن واحداً من العشرة، وحاصله أن الجزء بوصف الجزئية يمتنع بدون الكل، وحينئذ ترد سائر الأمور الإضافية، كالأب مع الابن والأخ مع الأخ والصانع مع المصنوع، ويلزم ألا يكونا غيرين ويلزم أن لا يكون الغيران بل الضدان غيرين، لأن التغاير والتضاد من الإضافات، فإن التزموا ذلك بناء على أن الإضافة عدمية، ولا تمايز بين الإعدام.
He said: And some of them held that in the non-difference of two things, it is necessary that each be impossible without the other, as in the attributes of the Eternal, unlike something like whiteness with body. And they claimed that the one from ten is impossible without it. And it is not hidden that all other relations then apply. Notice: The difference between essence and attribute only repels the eternality of other than the essence, not a multiplicity of eternal things. It has already preceded that separation from one side is not sufficient for difference, otherwise it would be refuted by the part with the whole and the described with the attribute. And some claimed that it is sufficient, such that the non-difference of two things is only realized if each of them is such that it is impossible without the other, and the refutation is not applicable. As for the described with the attribute, non-difference is only in attributes which the essence is impossible without, just as they are impossible without the essence, like the attributes of the Eternal, due to the impossibility of non-existence upon the Eternal, and likewise some attributes with others, unlike something like whiteness with body, for they are other. And as for the part with the whole, just as ten is impossible without one, likewise the one from ten is impossible without it, for if it existed without it, it would not be one from ten. The upshot is that the part, under the description of part-hood, is impossible without the whole. Then all relational matters apply, such as father with son, brother with brother, and maker with made, and it would follow that they are not other, and it would follow that the two others, indeed the two opposites, are not other, because difference and opposition are among relations. If they accept that, they build it on the basis that relation is non-existent, and there is no distinction among non-existents.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage discusses the criterion for two things being 'other' (mutaghayir) or not. The author first reports a position: some argue that for two things not to be other than each other, each must be impossible without the other (mutual impossibility), using the example of God's eternal attributes (e.g., knowledge and power) which are inseparable from the essence. In contrast, whiteness is other than body because it can separate. These proponents claim that the number one is impossible without the number ten. The author notes that this leads to problems with all relational concepts (idadaat). A 'tanbih' (notice) clarifies that the issue of difference between essence and attribute only prevents the eternity of something other than the essence, not a plurality of eternal entities. The author then states the earlier teaching: separation from one side (infikak min janib) is insufficient for difference, otherwise it would be refuted by part-whole and subject-attribute relationships. Some claim that separation from one side is enough, so two things are not different only if each is impossible without the other. They argue the refutation does not apply: for subject-attribute, non-difference only occurs for attributes that the essence cannot exist without (like God's attributes), not for contingent attributes like whiteness. For part-whole, the part under the description of 'part' (e.g., one as part of ten) is impossible without the whole, so the whole and part are not other. But then the author points out a vicious consequence: all relational pairs (father-son, brother-brother, maker-made) would become not other, and even opposites would collapse, because difference and opposition are themselves relations. If they accept this, they must hold that relations are non-existent, leading to no distinction among non-existents, which is absurd. Thus, the author implicitly rejects the mutual-impossibility criterion as too broad.

Key Terms
الصفات القديمة
الذات والصفة
الامتناع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that any attribute of God is other than His essence, leading to multiplicity.
Correction: The author shows that because of mutual impossibility, the eternal attributes are not other than the essence, avoiding a multiplicity of eternal entities.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what makes God's attributes not other than His essence?
A) They are created after the essence.
B) The essence is impossible without them and they are impossible without the essence.
C) They are identical to the essence in every way.
D) They are known only through revelation.

Key Takeaway
Eternal attributes are not other than God's essence because of mutual dependence, contrasting with contingent attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that God's attributes are inseparable from His essence. If you find yourself doubting God's mercy in a trial, are you not, according to this lesson, actually doubting an eternal attribute that is impossible to separate from God? How will you remind yourself of this truth when your heart wavers?
?
The lesson contrasts eternal attributes with contingent attributes like whiteness that can be separated. Your own good qualities—patience, generosity, faith—are contingent and can leave you. What does that reality demand from you today in terms of humility and reliance on God?
?
The mutual impossibility means that if you truly know God, you cannot claim to know Him while denying any of His eternal attributes. Have you ever conceptually separated Allah's power from His mercy, acting as if one could exist without the other? What must you change in your understanding of God?

The Part-Whole Argument and Relational Absurdities
Author extends the argument to part-whole and relational concepts, showing absurdity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the mutual-impossibility criterion leads to absurd consequences for relational concepts like father-son and opposite pairs.

Core Idea
Applying mutual impossibility to all relations collapses distinction into sameness.

The author continues to examine the mutual-impossibility criterion by applying it to the part-whole relationship. He shows that if the criterion is taken strictly, then all relational pairs (father-son, brother-brother, maker-made) would not be other, and even opposites would not be other, because difference and opposition are themselves relations. This is absurd, and the only escape is to deny the reality of relations, which is problematic.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on part-whole
Read the Arabic and translation carefully, noting the example of ten and one.
2
Trace the argument from part-whole to relational pairs
The author extends the criterion to all relational concepts, showing that they would all be considered not other if mutual impossibility is required.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Understand the absurd consequence
If father and son are not other, then all distinct entities collapse. The author notes this is based on viewing relations as nonexistent, which removes distinction.
The author implicitly rejects the mutual-impossibility criterion as too broad.

The Condition for Taghayyur and the Objection from Part and Whole
The author discusses the criterion for difference and addresses an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the criterion for true difference (taghayyur) between two things and how the part-whole relationship challenges it.

Core Idea
True difference requires separability from at least one side, but some relations are inseparable.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid discusses a key principle in kalam: what does it mean for two things to be truly different? The author examines the view that mutual dependency negates difference and responds to an objection using part-whole relations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Let the author's argument flow before interpreting.
Notice the footnote markers and the dense style.
2
Identify the two views on taghayyur
The passage first states that separation from one side is not sufficient (because of part/whole and attribute/essence). Then it presents a counterclaim that mutual impossibility of existence is the criterion.
The author defends the sufficiency of one-sided separability.
3
Understand the objection from part and whole
The objector argues that the part cannot exist without the whole and vice versa, so they should not be different. The author replies that the part under the description of partness cannot exist without the whole, but in itself it may.
This is the core of the defense.
4
See the answer about relations
The passage ends with an answer that the discussion is about the subject of the relation, not the composite of subject and accident.
This clarifies the scope of the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بدون الآخر (١) كما في صفات القديم بخلاف مثل البياض مع الجسم) وزعموا أن الواحد من العشرة يمتنع بدونها . ولا خفاء في ورود سائر الإضافات حينئذ تنبيه غير (۳) التغاير بين الذات والصفة، إنما يدفع أزلية غير الذات لا تعدد الأزليات) . قد سبق أنه لا يكفي في التغاير الانفكاك من جانب، وإلا انتقض بالجزء مع الكل والموصوف مع الصفة، وزعم بعضهم أنه كاف حتى إن عدم تغاير الشيئين إنما يتحقق إذا كان كل منهما بحيث يمتنع بدون الآخر، والنقض غير وارد. أما الموصوف مع الصفة فلأن عدم التغاير إنما هو في الصفات التي تمتنع الذات بدونها، كما تمتنع هي بدون الذات كصفات القديم، لامتناع العدم على القديم، وكذا الصفات بعضها مع البعض، بخلاف مثل البياض مع الجسم، فإنهما غيران وأما الجزء مع الكل فلأنه، كما تمتنع العشرة بدون الواحد، كذلك الواحد من العشرة يمتنع بدونها، إذ لو وجد بدونها لم يكن واحداً من العشرة، وحاصله أن الجزء بوصف الجزئية يمتنع بدون الكل، وحينئذ ترد سائر الأمور الإضافية، كالأب مع الابن والأخ مع الأخ والصانع مع المصنوع، ويلزم أن لا يكونا غيرين ويلزم أن لا يكون الغيران بل الضدان غيرين، لأنه التغاير والتضاد من الإضافات، فإن (٦) التزموا ذلك بناء على أن الإضافة عدمية، ولا تمايز بين الإعدام . أجيب بأن الكلام في معروض الإضافة من حيث إنه معروض، لا في المجموع المركب من المعروض والمعارض .
Without the other (1) as in the attributes of the Eternal, contrary to whiteness with the body. And they claimed that the one of ten cannot exist without them. And there is no hiddenness in the occurrence of the rest of the relations then. A note: other than the difference between essence and attribute only repels the eternity of other-than-essence, not the multiplicity of eternal things. It has preceded that separation from one side is not sufficient for difference; otherwise it would be refuted by the part with the whole and the described with the attribute. And some claimed that it is sufficient, such that the non-difference of two things only obtains when each of them is such that it cannot exist without the other, and the refutation is not valid. As for the described with the attribute, non-difference is only in attributes which the essence cannot exist without, just as they cannot exist without the essence, like the attributes of the Eternal, due to the impossibility of non-existence upon the Eternal; likewise some attributes with others, contrary to whiteness with the body, for they are different. And as for the part with the whole, because just as ten cannot exist without the one, likewise the one of ten cannot exist without them; for if it existed without them, it would not be one of ten. In sum, the part under the description of partness cannot exist without the whole. Then the rest of relational matters apply, such as father with son, brother with brother, maker with made. And it would follow that they are not two different things, and that two different things, rather two opposites, are not two different things, because difference and opposition are relations. Then if they commit to that based on the claim that relation is non-existent and there is no distinction among non-existents. The answer: the discussion is about the subject of the relation insofar as it is a subject, not about the composite of the subject and the accident.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage is a discussion on the criterion for difference (taghayyur) between two things. The author begins by referencing the view that difference requires the possibility of one existing without the other, exemplified in the attributes of the Eternal (God) unlike whiteness with the body. He then notes a nuance: the denial of difference between essence and attribute only negates the eternity of something other than the essence, not the multiplicity of eternal things. He then states a principle: separability from one side is not sufficient for difference; otherwise it would be refuted by the part-whole relationship and the attribute-essence relationship. Some claim that it is sufficient, so that two things are only not different if each cannot exist without the other. The author argues that the refutation is not valid. He explains: for attribute and essence, non-difference applies only to attributes that the essence cannot exist without (like eternal attributes). For part and whole, just as the whole cannot exist without the part, the part under the description of partness cannot exist without the whole. This leads to absurdities if applied to all relations like father-son, etc. Then an answer is given: the discussion concerns the subject of the relation qua subject, not the composite of subject and accident.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If two things cannot exist without each other, they are not truly different.
Correction: The author shows that separability from one side is sufficient for difference, even if mutual necessity exists for part/whole under a certain description.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the condition for true difference (taghayyur) between two things?
A) Each can exist without the other entirely.
B) Separability from at least one side is sufficient.
C) Both sides must be separable.
D) They must share all attributes.

Key Takeaway
True difference (taghayyur) requires that one can exist without the other from at least one side, but mutual necessity under a certain description does not negate difference.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You believe Allah's attributes are eternal and inseparable from His essence. How does this affect the way you understand His mercy or anger? Are you ever tempted to think of His attributes as separate from Him? What would that imply about your concept of tawhid?
?
The lesson shows that two things can be inseparable yet different. Think of a relationship in your life—like being a parent or a spouse—that feels inseparable from who you are. Is that relationship truly part of your essence, or is it a description? How does recognizing the distinction change how you prioritize your identity before Allah?

Definition of Tamathul: Sharing in Essential Attributes
Sanusi defines tamathul and its entailments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of tamathul as sharing in essential attributes and its key entailments in kalam.

Core Idea
Tamathul means sharing in essential attributes but requires a point of difference.

In this passage, the author defines tamathul (likeness) as a specific type of relationship between two entities. He clarifies that it is not complete identity; rather, it involves sharing in the essential attributes that define their essence, and he lists necessary entailments that follow from this sharing. Understanding this definition is crucial for grasping later discussions on divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to get a sense of the author's definition and list.
Notice the footnoted numbers; they refer to explanations.
2
Identify the definition of tamathul
Find the explicit definition: 'it is sharing in essential attributes.' Understand what 'essential attributes' means as described in the commentary.
3
List the entailments
The passage states three entailments: sharing in necessary/possible/impossible, each substituting the other, and need for a point of difference.
The third entailment is about distinctiveness, not elimination of difference.
4
Understand the point of difference
The author emphasizes that there must be a point of difference to achieve distinctiveness; otherwise they would be identical.
This guards against the misconception of complete identity.
5
Connect to common misunderstanding
Reflect on the common misunderstanding that tamathul means total identity. The passage clarifies that similarity allows for differentiation.
This is important for later discussions on divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاشتراك في الصفات النفسية
قال : ومنها التماثل وهو الاشتراك (۲) في الصفات النفسية (۳)، ويلزمه الاشتراك فيما يجب ( ) ، ويمكن ويمتنع ، وأن يسد ( ) كل من الآخر (٦)، ولا بد من جهة اختلاف لتحقيق التمايز (۷) ، ومن اشتراط عدمه (۸) أراد في المعنى الذي به التماثل).
Sharing in Essential Attributes. He said: Among them is similarity (tamathul), which is sharing (2) in essential attributes (3). And it entails sharing in what is necessary ( ), possible, and impossible, and that each of them substitutes ( ) the other (6), and there must be a point of difference to achieve distinctiveness (7), and whoever stipulates its absence (8) intended in the meaning by which similarity occurs.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage defines tamathul (likeness/similarity) as sharing in essential attributes (al-sifat al-nafsiyya). Essential attributes are those whose description indicates the essence itself without any additional meaning (as explained in footnote 3). The author then lists the entailments of this sharing: it entails sharing in what is necessary, possible, and impossible for the essence. Additionally, it entails that each of them substitutes for the other in terms of effects (so that the same consequences follow from each). Then it states that there must be a point of difference to achieve distinctiveness (tamayyuz) between them; otherwise they would not be multiple. Whoever stipulates the absence of any difference between them intended that in the meaning by which similarity occurs, i.e., they are similar in that specific meaning.

Key Terms
تماثل
اشتراك
صفات نفسية
تمايز
واجب
ممكن
ممتنع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Tamathul means complete identity without any difference.
Correction: Tamathul requires a point of difference to achieve distinctiveness; it is not identity.

Checkpoint
What does tamathul (likeness) require besides sharing in essential attributes?
A) Complete identity in all respects.
B) A point of difference to achieve distinctiveness.
C) That both things have the same physical form.
D) That they share all accidental attributes.

Key Takeaway
Tamathul is sharing in essential attributes, which entails shared modalities and substitution, but requires a point of difference for multiplicity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your relationships, do you assume that sharing core beliefs means complete agreement? Where have you ignored necessary differences, leading to tension? How can you honor both similarity and distinctiveness in light of tamathul?
?
The passage says tamathul entails substitution—each can stand for the other in effects. Consider a brother or sister in faith: would you trust them to represent you in matters of religion? Are you prepared to be accountable for their actions as if they were your own? What does that demand of your mutual care?

Definition and Conditions of Tamathul
Al-Taftazani defines tamathul, explains essential attributes, and discusses its conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of tamathul as sharing in essential attributes, its necessary consequences, and the condition that difference in other aspects is required for multiplicity.

Core Idea
Tamathul requires equality in essential attributes but difference in other aspects.

In classical kalam, the concept of tamathul (likeness) is crucial for understanding how multiple entities can be similar while remaining distinct. Al-Taftazani establishes a precise definition grounded in essential attributes, distinguishes them from meaning-based attributes, and clarifies the logical implications of similarity. He then addresses a key objection about whether equality must be absolute or limited to the aspect of likeness.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the passage once without pausing. Then read the translation. Let the author's flow come through before you analyze.
Note the technical terms: التماثل, الصفات النفسية, الصفة المعنوية.
2
Define tamathul and contrast attributes
Identify the definition of tamathul. Then locate the two types of attributes: essential (nafsiyya) and meaning-based (ma'nawiyya). Understand the difference.
Essential attributes describe the essence without extra meaning, e.g., substance being a substance.
3
List the two necessary consequences
The passage lists two lawazim (necessary consequences) of sharing in essential attributes. Find them and paraphrase.
One is modal sharing, the other is mutual substitutability.
4
Understand the condition of difference
The author states that despite sharing essential attributes, similars must differ in some other aspect. Why?
Without difference, they would be identical, not two distinct similars.
5
Examine the objection and answer
Read the objection that absolute equality would negate multiplicity, and the rebuttal that equality is only in the aspect of likeness.
The Hadith about wheat is used to support this point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أي ومن خواص الكثرة التماثل وهو الاشتراك في الصفات النفسية، ومرادهم بالصفة النفسية، صفة ثبوتية، يدل الوصف بها على نفس الذات دون معنى زائد عليها ككون الجوهر جوهراً، وذاتاً وشيئاً وموجوداً، وتقابله المعنوية، صفة ثبوتية دالة على معنى زائد على الذات ككون الجوهر حادثاً ومتحيزاً وقابلا للأعراض. ومن لوازم الاشتراك في الصفات النفسية أمران : أحدهما : الاشتراك فيما يجب ويمتنع ويجوز . وثانيهما: أن يسد كل منهما من الآخر، وينوب منابه، فمن ههنا يقال : المثلان موجودان يشتركان فيما يجب ويجوز ويمتنع، أو موجودان سد كل منهما مسد الآخر، والمتماثلان، وإن اشتركا في الصفات النفسية، لكن لا بد من اختلافهما بجهة أخرى ليتحقق التعدد والتمايز فيصح التماثل، ونسب إلى الشيخ أنه يشترط في التماثل التساوي، من كل وجه . واعترض بأنه لا تعدد حينئذ فلا تماثل وبأن أهل اللغة مطبقون على صحة قولنا : زيد مثل عمرو في الفقه إذ كان يساويه فيه، ويسد مسده وإن اختلفا في كثير من الأوصاف، ولذا قال النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم : «الحنطة بالحنطة مثل بمثل وأراد به الاستواء في الكيل دون الوزن وعدد الحبات وأوصافها . والجواب : أن المراد التساوي في الجهة التي بها التماثل، حتى إن زيداً وعمراً لو اشتركا في الفقه، وكان بينهما مساواة في ذلك بحيث ينوب أحدهما مناب الآخر صح القول بأنهما مثلان في الفقه وإلا فلا.
That is, among the properties of multiplicity is tamathul (likeness), which is sharing in the essential attributes (sifat nafsiyya). And by 'essential attribute' they mean a positive attribute (sifa thubutiyya) whose description indicates the essence itself without an additional meaning, such as the substance being a substance, an essence, a thing, and an existent. Its opposite is the meaning-based attribute (sifa ma'nawiyya), a positive attribute indicating an additional meaning beyond the essence, such as the substance being originated, spatially located, and receptive to accidents. Among the necessary consequences (lawāzim) of sharing in essential attributes are two matters: one is sharing in what is necessary, possible, and impossible; the second is that each one takes the place of the other and acts as its substitute. Hence it is said: 'Two similars (mithlān) are two existents that share in what is necessary, possible, and impossible,' or 'two existents each of which takes the place of the other.' And two things that are similar (mutamāthilān), even if they share in essential attributes, must nevertheless differ in some other aspect so that multiplicity and distinction (tamāyuz) are actualized, and thus tamathul is valid. It is attributed to al-Shaykh [Abū al-Hasan al-Ash'arī?] that he conditions in tamathul equality from every aspect. It is objected that then there would be no multiplicity, so no tamathul, and that linguists universally agree on the validity of saying 'Zayd is like 'Amr in jurisprudence' when he equals him in it and takes his place, even if they differ in many attributes. Thus the Prophet ﷺ said: 'Wheat for wheat like for like (mithl bi-mithl),' meaning equality in measure (kayl) not in weight, number of grains, or their qualities. The answer: what is meant is equality in the aspect by which tamathul holds, such that if Zayd and 'Amr share in jurisprudence and are equal in that to the extent that one substitutes for the other, it is correct to say they are two similars in jurisprudence; otherwise not.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage defines tamathul (likeness) and explains its conditions. It begins by stating that tamathul is a property of multiplicity (kathra), and it is defined as sharing in essential attributes (sifat nafsiyya). An essential attribute is a positive attribute that describes the essence itself without adding an extra meaning, e.g., substance being substance, essence, thing, existent. Its opposite is the meaning-based attribute (sifat ma'nawiyya) which does add an extra meaning, like being originated or spatially located. Two necessary consequences (lawazim) follow from sharing in essential attributes: (1) sharing in what is necessary, possible, and impossible (i.e., the same modal status), and (2) each can substitute for the other. From this, it is said that two similars share in modal status or that each takes the place of the other. However, for tamathul to be valid, there must also be some difference in another aspect to allow multiplicity and distinction; otherwise they would be identical. There is a reported view from al-Shaykh (possibly al-Ash'ari) that tamathul requires equality from every aspect. An objection is raised: if they are equal in every aspect, there would be no multiplicity, hence no tamathul (since tamathul requires two distinct entities). Moreover, linguistic usage shows that we say 'Zayd is like 'Amr in jurisprudence' even though they differ in many other attributes. The Prophet's ﷺ hadith about wheat-for-wheat being 'like for like' refers to equality in measure only. The answer is that the required equality is only in the specific aspect that grounds the tamathul (e.g., jurisprudence), not in all aspects.

صفات نفسية
صفة معنوية
سد مسد
تمايز
تساوي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Tamathul requires absolute equality in all respects.
Correction: Tamathul requires equality only in the aspect of likeness; difference in other aspects is necessary for multiplicity.

Checkpoint

B) Sharing in what is impossible
C) Each takes the place of the other
D) Distinction in all aspects

Key Takeaway
Tamathul is defined by equality in essential attributes, which implies shared modal status and substitutability, but requires a distinct aspect to avoid identity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

What one essential attribute will you sincerely work on today to become a true 'similar'?
?
The hadith shows that in trade, 'like for like' refers to equality in the relevant measure, not in every detail. Consider your own transactions—whether business or personal exchanges. Do you sometimes insist on superficial equality while overlooking the deeper fairness? Is there a relationship where you demand exactness in trivial matters but neglect the essence of justice? How will you adjust your measure today?
?
The objection to absolute equality reminds us that claiming likeness requires distinction to avoid identity. In your spiritual journey, do you ever fall into the trap of wanting to be exactly like someone else, forgetting that Allah created you unique? What is the distinctive aspect Allah has given you that you are neglecting because you're focused on being a carbon copy? How will you embrace your unique role in serving Him?

Definition of Tamathul and Its Two Implications
Defines tamathul and lists its two necessary implications, with discussion of equality condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of tamathul as sharing psychological attributes and its two necessary implications, as well as the correct condition for similitude.

Core Idea
Tamathul means sharing essential attributes, not every aspect.

In this passage, the author clarifies the concept of tamathul (similitude) as a property of multiplicity. He defines it as sharing psychological attributes and identifies two necessary implications. He also addresses a potential misunderstanding about equality, using linguistic evidence and a hadith to show that similitude only requires equality in the relevant aspect.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of tamathul
Read the opening sentence where tamathul is defined as sharing psychological attributes. Understand what psychological attributes are.
Psychological attributes are those that indicate the essence itself without additional meaning.
2
Identify the two implications
Find the two necessary implications listed: sharing in the three modal categories and mutual substitution.
3
Examine the condition of difference
Notice that similitude requires difference in some aspect to avoid identity. Then read the objection and answer about equality.
The objection claims equality in every respect negates multiplicity; the answer clarifies equality is only in the aspect of similitude.

That is, among the properties of multiplicity is similitude, which is sharing in psychological attributes. And what they mean by 'psychological attribute' is a positive attribute whose description indicates the essence itself without an additional meaning, such as substance being substance, essence, thing, and existent. Its opposite is 'meaning-derived attribute,' which is a positive attribute indicating an additional meaning beyond the essence, such as substance being accidental, occupying space, and receptive to accidents. From the necessary implications of sharing in psychological attributes are two things: one of them is sharing in what is necessary, impossible, and contingent. And the second is that each takes the place of the other and substitutes for it. Hence it is said: two similars are two existents that share in what is necessary, contingent, and impossible, or two existents each of which takes the place of the other. And the two similars, even if they share in psychological attributes, must differ in another aspect so that multiplicity and distinction are realized, and thus similitude is correct. It is attributed to the Shaykh that he stipulates equality in every respect for similitude. And it was objected that then there is no multiplicity, so no similitude, and that the people of language agree on the validity of our saying 'Zayd is like Amr in jurisprudence' when he equals him in it and takes his place, even if they differ in many descriptions. Hence the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Wheat for wheat, like for like,' meaning equality in measure, not in weight or number of grains or their descriptions. The answer: that what is meant is equality in the aspect in which similitude exists, such that if Zayd and Amr share in jurisprudence and there is equality between them in that, to the extent that each substitutes for the other, then the statement that they are similars in jurisprudence is valid; otherwise not.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of tamathul (similitude) and explains its necessary implications. The author begins by stating that tamathul is a property of multiplicity, specifically the sharing of psychological attributes (al-sifat al-nafsiyya). Psychological attributes are defined as positive attributes that refer to the essence itself without any additional meaning, such as substance being substance, or being a thing, essence, or existent. In contrast, meaning-derived attributes (al-ma'nawiyya) are positive attributes that indicate an additional meaning beyond the essence, such as substance being accidental, occupying space, or receptive to accidents. 

Then the author lists two necessary implications (lawazim) of sharing in psychological attributes: 
1. Sharing in what is necessary (wajib), impossible (mumtani'), and contingent (ja'iz). 
2. Each of the two similars takes the place of the other and substitutes for it. 
Hence, it is said that two similars are two existents that share in the three modal categories, or that each can stand in for the other.

The author notes that for two things to be similar while sharing psychological attributes, they must also differ in some other aspect to realize multiplicity and distinction; otherwise, if they were equal in every respect, they would be identical, not similar. The author attributes to the Shaykh (possibly Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) the condition that tamathul requires equality in every respect. An objection is raised: if equality is in every respect, then there is no multiplicity, so no similitude. Moreover, linguists accept saying 'Zayd is like Amr in jurisprudence' even if they differ in many other descriptions. The Prophet's hadith about wheat being like for like is cited, indicating equality in measure not in all attributes. The answer clarifies that the required equality is only in the specific aspect of similitude; for example, if Zayd and Amr share jurisprudence equally, one substitutes for the other in that respect, they are similar in jurisprudence, not in all attributes.

Definition of Contrariety and Its Conditions
Al-Taftazani defines contrariety and clarifies its scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of contrariety (al-taḍād) as a property of multiplicity, its essential conditions, and what it excludes.

Core Idea
Contrariety is the impossibility of two accidents coexisting in the same substrate.

Al-Taftazani now defines one of the key properties of multiplicity: contrariety. This concept is essential for understanding how attributes and accidents relate to each other. The definition is precise and comes with several clarifications that rule out common misapplications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the concise style of the definition and the subsequent clarifications.
The passage begins with 'قال: ومنها التضاد'.
2
Identify the core definition
Find the exact definition: contrariety is the state of two meanings (accidents) such that it is impossible in their essence for them to coexist in one substrate in one respect. Note the three key components: two meanings, impossibility of coexistence, and same substrate/respect.
The definition uses 'بحيث يستحيل لذاتيهما اجتماعهما في محل من جهة'.
3
Understand the exclusions
The passage lists several pairs that are not contrary: non-accidents (substances), pairs like blackness and sweetness, knowledge of movement and stillness, and relative properties like smallness and largeness. Ask why each is excluded.
These exclusions highlight the precise scope of contrariety.
4
Grasp the nuance of 'in their essence'
The restriction 'in their essence' excludes pairs that are not opposites inherently, like knowledge of movement and stillness (they can coexist). Contrast with impossibility of coexistence (e.g., blackness and whiteness) which is essential.
This nuance prevents overextension of the term.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: ومنها التضاد، وهو كون المعنيين بحيث يستحيل لذاتيهما اجتماعهما في محل من جهة فلا تضاد بين غير العرضين ولا بين مثل السواد والحلاوة ولا بين العلم بالحركة والسكون معاً، ولا بين مثل الصغر والكبر ما لم تعتبر الإضافة إلى معين. أي من خواص الكثرة التضاد وهو كون المعنيين بحيث يستحيل لذاتيهما اجتماعهما في محل واحد من جهة واحدة. والمراد بالمعنى ما يقابل العين أي ما لا يكون قيامه بنفسه، وذكر الاجتماع مغن عن وحدة الزمان والتقييد بالمعنيين يخرج العينين، والعين مع المعنى، والعدمين والعدم مع الوجود. ولهذا قالوا بعدم التضاد في الأحكام وسائر الإضافات لكونها اعتبارية لا تحقق لها في الأعيان ولا يخرج القديم والحادث إذا كانا معنيين كعلم الله تعالى وعلم زيد بل ظاهر التعريف متناول له، إذ لا إشعار فيه باشتراط التوارد على محل واحد. وقد يقال إن معنى امتناع الاجتماع أنهما يتواردان على محل، ولا يكونان معاً ليخرج مثل ذلك، لأن محل القديم قديم، فلا يتصف بالحادث وبالعكس ولأن القديم لا يزول عن المحل حتى يرد عليه المقابل، واحترز بقيد استحالة الاجتماع عن مثل السواد والحلاوة مما يمكن اجتماعهما في محل واحد، وبقيد لذاتهما عن مثل العلم بحركة الشيء وسكونه
He said: And among them is contrariety (al-taḍād), which is the state of two meanings such that it is impossible in their essence for them to coexist in one substrate in one respect. So there is no contrariety between non-accidents, nor between things like blackness and sweetness, nor between knowledge of movement and knowledge of stillness together, nor between things like smallness and largeness unless the addition to a specific [thing] is considered. That is, among the properties of multiplicity is contrariety, which is the state of two meanings such that it is impossible in their essence for them to coexist in one substrate from one respect. And by 'meaning' is meant what is opposite to an entity (al-ʿayn), i.e., what does not subsist by itself. And the mention of coexistence suffices for the unity of time. And the restriction to two meanings excludes two entities, an entity and a meaning, two non-existences, and non-existence with existence. For this reason they said there is no contrariety in rulings and all relations because they are mental considerations with no reality in external entities. And it does not exclude the eternal and the temporal if they are meanings, like the knowledge of God and the knowledge of Zayd; rather the apparent definition includes it, since there is no indication in it of the condition of occurring upon one substrate. And it may be said: the meaning of the impossibility of coexistence is that they occur upon a substrate and are not together, so as to exclude such [cases], because the substrate of the eternal is eternal, so it is not described by the temporal, and vice versa, and because the eternal does not leave the substrate until its opposite comes upon it. And he restricted by the condition of impossibility of coexistence to exclude things like blackness and sweetness which can coexist in one substrate, and by the condition of 'in their essence' to exclude things like knowledge of movement and knowledge of stillness.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage defines contrariety (al-taḍād) as one of the properties of multiplicity. Al-Taftazani states the definition: contrariety is the state of two meanings (i.e., accidents) such that it is impossible in their essence for them to coexist in one substrate in one respect. He then clarifies what is excluded: there is no contrariety between non-accidents (substances), nor between pairs that can coexist like blackness and sweetness, nor between knowledge of movement and stillness, nor between smallness and largeness unless relative to a specific standard. He explains that 'meaning' refers to accidents (what does not subsist by itself). The mention of coexistence obviates the need to mention unity of time. The restriction to two meanings excludes: two substances, a substance and an accident, two non-existences, and non-existence with existence. Thus contrariety does not apply to rulings or relations, which are mental constructs. The definition does not exclude the eternal and temporal if they are meanings (like God's knowledge and Zayd's knowledge) because there is no condition of occurring on the same substrate; rather, the impossibility of coexistence here is due to the substrate's nature: the eternal's substrate is eternal and cannot accept the temporal. Finally, the restriction 'impossibility of coexistence' excludes pairs that can coexist (e.g., blackness and sweetness), and 'in their essence' excludes pairs that are not opposites in themselves (e.g., knowledge of movement and stillness).

المعنى
العين
الاستحالة
الاجتماع
المحل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that contrariety applies to substances or to any two different entities.
Correction: Contrariety is restricted to accidents (meanings). Substances, non-existences, and mental constructs are excluded.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is excluded from contrariety according to the definition?
A) Two accidents like blackness and whiteness.
B) Two non-existences.
C) Knowledge of movement and knowledge of stillness.
D) Blackness and sweetness.

Key Takeaway
Contrariety is a relation between two accidents that cannot coexist in one substrate in one respect.

?
The definition excludes non-existence with existence from contrariety. Reflect: Have you ever considered the absence of a blessing (e.g., health, wealth) as the 'opposite' of its presence, leading to despair? How does realizing that non-existence is not a real attribute change your perspective on what you lack? What is one thing you can thank Allah for today precisely because it is not absent?
