Definition of Opposition and Its Conditions
Defines opposition and explains its conditions and exclusions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of opposition in Islamic theology and the three conditions necessary for two attributes to be considered opposites.

Core Idea
Opposition requires two attributes that cannot coexist by their very nature, not just by accident.

This lesson presents the classical definition of opposition (al-Ṭaḍādd) as one of the properties of multiplicity. We will examine the precise wording of the definition and the conditions that must be met for two attributes to be considered opposites. Special attention is given to what is excluded from the definition and why.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the key terms: 'opposition', 'two meanings', 'impossible', 'substrate', 'aspect', 'essences'.
The definition is: 'two meanings such that their coexistence in one substrate from one aspect is impossible by virtue of their essences.'
2
Understand the conditions
Identify the three conditions: (1) impossibility of coexistence, (2) by virtue of their essences, (3) from one aspect. Think about why each is needed.
These conditions exclude accidental impossibilities and relative opposites.
3
See what is excluded
Note the exclusions: non-accidents, two entities, an entity with a meaning, two non-existences, non-existence with existence, rulings, and relative accidents. Understand why they are not opposites.
4
Address the objection
Work through the objection about two blacknesses and the answer. This clarifies that similarity requires different substrates, so they are not opposites.
Opposition requires essential incompatibility, not just inability to occupy the same spot.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التضاد من خواص الكثرة قال : ومنها التضاد، وهو كون المعنيين بحيث يستحيل لذاتيهما، اجتماعهما في محل من جهة فلا تضاد بين غير العرضين ولا بين مثل السواد والحلاوة ولا بين العلم بالحركة والسكون معاً، ولا بين مثل الصغر والكبر ما لم تعتبر الإضافة إلى معين. أي من خواص الكثرة التضاد وهو كون المعنيين بحيث يستحيل لذاتيهما اجتماعهما في محل واحد من جهة واحدة. والمراد بالمعنى ما يقابل العين أي ما لا يكون قيامه بنفسه، وذكر الاجتماع مغن عن وحدة الزمان والتقييد بالمعنيين يخرج العينين، والعين مع المعنى، والعدمين والعدم مع الوجود. ولهذا قالوا بعدم التضاد في الأحكام وسائر الإضافات لكونها اعتبارية لا تحقق لها في الأعيان ولا يخرج القديم والحادث إذا كانا معنيين كعلم الله تعالى وعلم زيد بل ظاهر التعريف متناول له، إذ لا إشعار فيه باشتراط التوارد على محل واحد . وقد يقال إن معنى امتناع الاجتماع أنهما يتواردان على محل ، ولا يكونان معاً ليخرج مثل ذلك، لأن محل القديم قديم، فلا يتصف بالحادث وبالعكس ولأن القديم لا يزول عن المحل حتى يرد عليه المقابل، واحترز بقيد استحالة الاجتماع عن مثل السواد والحلاوة مما يمكن اجتماعهما في محل واحد، وبقيد لذاتهما عن مثل العلم بحركة الشيء وسكونه معاً أي العلم بأن هذا الشيء متحرك، والعلم بأن هذا ساكن في آن واحد، فانهما لا يجتمعان لكن لا لذاتهما بل لامتناع الحركة والسكون وأما تصور حركة الشيء وسكونه معاً، فممكن ولذا يصح الحكم باستحالتهما، وبقيد من جهة واحدة عن مثل الصغر والكبر والقرب والبعد على الإطلاق، فإنهما لا يتضادان وإن امتنع اجتماعهما في الجملة وإنما يتضادان إن اعتبر إضافتهما إلى معين ككون الشيء صغيراً وكبيراً بالنسبة إلى زيد ولا خفاء في أنه لا حاجة إلى هذا القيد حينئذ لأن مطلق الصغر والكبر لا يمتنع اجتماعهما، وعند اتحاد الجهة يمتنع فالأقرب أن القيد احتراز عن خروج مثل ذلك. وربما يعترض على تعريف المتضادين بالتماثلين كالسوادين عند من يقول بامتناع اجتماعهما. ويجاب بأن اتحاد المحل شرط في التضاد ولا تماثل إلا عند اختلاف المحل.
Opposition is among the properties of multiplicity. He said: Among them is opposition, which is that the two meanings are such that their coexistence in a substrate is impossible by virtue of their essences, [so] there is no opposition between non-accidents, nor between like blackness and sweetness, nor between knowledge of movement and knowledge of rest together, nor between like smallness and bigness unless addition to a specific thing is considered. That is, opposition is among the properties of multiplicity, and it is that the two meanings are such that their coexistence in one substrate from one aspect is impossible by virtue of their essences. And what is meant by 'meaning' is what is opposed to 'entity' (i.e., what does not subsist by itself). And the mention of 'coexistence' suffices for unity of time. And the restriction to 'two meanings' excludes two entities, an entity with a meaning, two non-existences, and non-existence with existence. For this reason they said there is no opposition in rulings and all relative accidents because they are subjective and have no realization in extramental entities. Nor does it exclude the eternal and the originated when they are two meanings, like God's knowledge and Zayd's knowledge; rather the apparent meaning includes it since there is no indication in it of the condition of occurring upon one substrate. It may be said that the meaning of impossibility of coexistence is that they both occur upon a substrate and are not together, to exclude such as that, because the substrate of the eternal is eternal, so it is not described by the originated, and vice versa, and because the eternal does not depart from the substrate until its opposite comes upon it. And he guarded by the condition 'impossibility of coexistence' against like blackness and sweetness, which can coexist in one substrate, and by the condition 'by virtue of their essences' against like knowledge of the movement of a thing and knowledge of its rest together, i.e., knowledge that this thing is moving and knowledge that it is resting at one moment; they do not combine but not by virtue of their essences but because of the impossibility of movement and rest. As for conceiving movement and rest together, it is possible, and therefore it is valid to judge them impossible. And by the condition 'from one aspect' against like smallness and bigness, closeness and distance in absolute terms; they are not opposites even if their coexistence is impossible in general, but they are opposites only if their addition to a specific thing is considered, like something being small and big relative to Zayd. It is clear that there is no need for this condition then because absolute smallness and bigness do not preclude their coexistence, and when the aspect is united it becomes impossible, so the closest thing is that the condition is a precaution to exclude such. Perhaps an objection is raised regarding the definition of opposites by two similar things like two blacknesses according to those who say their coexistence is impossible. And it is answered that unity of substrate is a condition in opposition, and there is no similarity except when the substrate is different.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage defines opposition (al-Ṭaḍādd) as a property of multiplicity. The author begins by stating that opposition is one of the properties of multiplicity, then gives the definition: two meanings such that their coexistence in one substrate from one aspect is impossible by virtue of their essences. He then explains each part of the definition. 'Meanings' are opposed to 'entities' – they are attributes that do not subsist by themselves. The word 'coexistence' excludes the need for mentioning unity of time. Specifying 'two meanings' excludes two entities (like two substances), an entity with a meaning, two non-existences, and non-existence with existence. The author notes that some categories like rulings and relative accidents are not considered opposites because they are subjective. He then addresses the case of eternal and originated attributes (like God's knowledge and man's knowledge) – they are not opposites because they do not occur on the same substrate. He explains the three conditions: (1) impossibility of coexistence distinguishes from cases where coexistence is possible (like blackness and sweetness), (2) by virtue of their essences distinguishes from accidental impossibility (like knowledge of movement and rest, which is impossible because the objects cannot coexist, not because the knowledges themselves are opposites), (3) from one aspect distinguishes from relative opposites like smallness and bigness which can coexist when considered relative to different things. The passage concludes with an objection: some say two similar things like two blacknesses cannot coexist, so they would be opposites. The answer is that similarity requires different substrates, so they cannot coexist in one substrate, but they are not opposites because opposition requires the two to be essentially incompatible, not just that they cannot occupy the same spot due to similarity (since they are essentially the same). The commentary preserves the argumentative order, explaining each term and condition as it appears.

Key Terms
تضاد
معنى
عين
محل
جهة
استحالة
اجتماع
مثلان
قديم
حادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any two things that cannot coexist are opposites.
Correction: Opposition requires the impossibility of coexistence to be by virtue of their essences and from one aspect. Accidental impossibilities (e.g., knowledge of movement and rest) or relative impossibilities (e.g., smallness and bigness relative to different things) are not true opposites.

Checkpoint
According to the definition in this passage, which of the following are considered opposites (mutadāddān)?
A) Blackness and sweetness
B) Knowledge of movement and knowledge of rest at the same moment
C) Smallness and bigness relative to the same thing
D) Two blacknesses in different substrates

Key Takeaway
Opposition is defined as two meanings that cannot coexist in one substrate from one aspect by virtue of their essences, with specific exclusions and conditions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the condition that opposition must be 'by virtue of their essences', how does this affect your understanding of Allah's attributes? Are there any attributes you have mistakenly thought were in opposition because of accidental factors in your own experience? How can this lesson correct that?
?
The passage excludes relative accidents like 'smallness' and 'bigness' from opposition unless considered from one aspect. Reflect on a time when you judged something to be contradictory in your faith because you considered it from different aspects without realizing it. How can you apply the condition of 'one aspect' to avoid such confusion?
?
The objection about two blacknesses being opposites is refuted because similarity requires different substrates. How does this precision in defining opposition challenge you to be more careful in how you use words like 'opposite' when describing your relationship with Allah or your spiritual state? Are there areas where you have been too quick to label things as opposed when they are not?

The Philosophers' Classification of Pairs
Philosophers classify pairs into similar, different, and opposite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how philosophers classify any two things into similar, different, and opposite based on their essence and possibility of coexistence.

Core Idea
Two things are always distinct; their relationship depends on essence and compatibility.

This lesson introduces the philosophical framework for analyzing pairs. Before we can discuss theological questions about divine attributes or creation, we need a precise vocabulary for how things relate to each other. Here, the philosophers present a threefold classification that will be essential for later arguments.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the fundamental principle
The passage begins with a foundational claim: any two things are inherently distinct ('other'). This sets the stage for all further classification.
This is a basic logical posit.
2
Understand the condition for similarity
Two things are similar if they share the complete essence (e.g., two humans). Note that this is not numerical identity but essential sameness.
3
Distinguish different vs opposite
If not similar, they are different. Among different pairs, some are opposite if they cannot coexist in the same subject from the same aspect. This introduces a further division.
Coexistence impossibility is key.
4
Contextualize within the broader discussion
The author notes that previous discussions were from the theologians' perspective, and now the philosophers' view is introduced. This helps orient the reader.
The text breaks off here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كل اثنين غيران عند الفلاسفة قال : وعند الفلاسفة كل اثنين فهما غيران، فإن اشتركا في تمام الماهية فمثلان وإلا فمتخالفان وهما متقابلان إن امتنع اجتماعهما في محل واحد من جهة واحدة. ما سبق من أقسام الكثرة وأحكامها على رأي المتكلمين، وأما على رأي
Every two are other (non-identical) according to the philosophers. He said: According to the philosophers, every two are other. If they share in the complete essence then they are similar, otherwise they are different. And they are opposite if their coexistence in one substrate from one aspect is impossible. What preceded of the divisions of multiplicity and their rulings is according to the opinion of the theologians, and as for according to the opinion of
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' classification of all pairs. It begins by stating a fundamental principle: for philosophers, any two things are 'other' (ghayrān), meaning they are distinct from each other. The author then explains the classification: if two things share in the complete essence (e.g., both are human), they are called 'similar' (mithlān). If they do not share the complete essence, they are 'different' (mukhālifān). Within the category of different things, some are 'opposite' (mutāqābilān) – those whose coexistence in one substrate from one aspect is impossible. The author then notes that the preceding discussion on the divisions of multiplicity and their rulings was from the perspective of the theologians, and now the philosophers' view is being presented, though the sentence is incomplete. The commentary follows the order of the Arabic: first the general principle, then the classification conditions, then the remark about the theologians.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some students think that 'similar' (mithlān) means numerically identical or exactly the same in all respects.
Correction: The passage specifies 'sharing in the complete essence' (اشتركا في تمام الماهية). For philosophers, two things can be similar while remaining distinct individuals (e.g., two humans). Similarity is about essential nature, not numerical identity.

Key Takeaway
Philosophers classify any two things as similar (sharing essence) or different, and among different, some are opposite (cannot coexist).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Conditions for Opposite Pairs and Their Exceptions
Commentator lists and explains the five restrictions defining opposites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five restrictions that define true opposites and why some are redundant or necessary to exclude apparent counterexamples.

Core Idea
True opposites require difference, impossibility of coexistence in the same subject, time, and aspect.

The definition of opposites needs careful refinement to include only pairs that are truly mutually exclusive. The commentator walks through each restriction, showing how it excludes certain pairs that might otherwise appear to be opposites. This clarifies the logical rules governing opposites in kalam and philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Purpose of the restrictions
Learn that the restrictions are meant to exclude pairs that do not meet the full definition of opposites, such as likes or things that can coexist.
2
The restriction of difference
Understand that the condition of being different (التخالف) excludes likes (المثلان), even if they cannot coexist.
3
Impossibility of coexistence in a subject
See how this restriction excludes pairs like blackness and sweetness, which can coexist. Also note the potential misunderstanding about excluding man and horse.
4
Unity of time and aspect
Analyze why unity of time is redundant, while unity of aspect is necessary to handle relative opposites like small/big or father/son.
5
Unity of subject
Recognize that opposites must be in the same subject; they can coexist in different subjects (e.g., whiteness in one body, blackness in another).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فخرج بقيد التخالف المثلان، وإن امتنع اجتماعهما، وبقيد امتناع الاجتماع في محل مثل السواد والحلاوة، مما يمكن اجتماعهما، وربما يفهم من امتناع الاجتماع في محل وردهما على المحل فيخرج مثل الإنسان والفرس، ومثل الإنسان والسواد، وفيه بحث سيجيء، وأما قيد وحدة الزمان فمستدرك على ما مر ، وكذا قيد وحدة الجهة إذا قصد به الاحتراز عن مثل الصغر مع الكبر والأبوة مع البنوة على الإطلاق. والحق أنه احتراز عن خروج مثل ذلك فإنهما متقابلان ولا يمتنع اجتماعها إلا عند اعتبار وحدة الجهة وأما التقييد بوحدة المحل، فلأن المتقابلين قد يجتمعان في الوجود وفي الجسم على الإطلاق كبياض الرومي، وسواد الحبشي.
Thus, by the restriction of difference, likes are excluded, even if their coexistence is impossible. And by the restriction of the impossibility of coexistence in a subject, things like black and sweetness, which can coexist, are excluded. One might understand from 'impossibility of coexistence in a subject' that it prevents the subject itself, so that things like man and horse, and man and blackness, are excluded, but there is a discussion to come. As for the restriction of unity of time, it is redundant based on what preceded. Likewise the restriction of unity of aspect, if intended to guard against things like smallness with bigness, and paternity with sonship in an absolute sense. The truth is that it guards against the exclusion of such things, for they are opposites and their coexistence is not impossible except when consideration of unity of aspect is made. As for the restriction of unity of subject, it is because opposites may coexist in existence and in the body absolutely, like the whiteness of the Roman and the blackness of the Abyssinian.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage refines the definition of opposites by discussing exceptions. The commentator explains that the condition of being differents excludes likes (even if they cannot coexist, likes are not opposites). The condition of impossibility of coexistence in a subject excludes things that can coexist, like black and sweetness. One might mistakenly think that 'impossibility of coexistence in a subject' also excludes things like man and horse (which are not in the same subject) or man and blackness (where blackness is an accident in man), but there is a later discussion. The condition of unity of time is redundant because the impossibility of coexistence already implies it. The condition of unity of aspect is needed to handle relative opposites like small/big (relative to a comparison) and father/son (relative to relationship). Without specifying aspect, such pairs would not seem to be opposites because they can coexist in different aspects. The truth is that they are opposites but their impossibility of coexistence only occurs when considering the same aspect. The condition of unity of subject is necessary because opposites can coexist in different subjects (e.g., whiteness in a Roman and blackness in an Abyssinian), so the definition requires the same subject.

Key Terms
قيد التخالف
قيد امتناع الاجتماع
وحدة الزمان
وحدة الجهة
وحدة المحل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Relative opposites like small and big are not true opposites because they can coexist in the same subject (e.g., a person can be both small and big relative to different things).
Correction: They are true opposites, but their impossibility of coexistence only applies when considering the same aspect (وحدة الجهة). With unity of aspect, small and big cannot coexist in the same subject at the same time.

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator say that the restriction of unity of time is redundant?
A) Because time is irrelevant.
B) Because the impossibility of coexistence already implies a single time.
C) Because opposites cannot exist in the same time.
D) Because the restriction of subject covers it.

Key Takeaway
True opposites must differ in kind, cannot coexist in the same subject, time, and aspect; unity of time is redundant, but unity of aspect prevents excluding relational opposites.

Division of Opposites: Correlatives and Contraries
The commentator classifies opposites based on existence and relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the classical theologians classify opposites into correlatives and contraries based on existence and relational intelligibility.

Core Idea
Opposites are not all alike; existence and relation determine their type.

In this passage, the commentator builds on the general definition of opposites by introducing a crucial subdivision. He distinguishes between opposites that depend on each other for meaning (correlatives) and those that do not (contraries). This distinction is foundational for many arguments in kalam about the attributes of Allah and the nature of creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and its literal translation. Notice the two conditions: both existent vs. one non-existent.
Pay attention to the terms 'vujudiyyan' and 'adamiyyan'.
2
Identify the two categories of existent opposites
The passage says: if both are existent and each is understood in relation to the other, they are correlatives; otherwise they are contraries. Examples: father/son are correlatives; black/white are contraries.
3
Understand the condition for non‑existent opposites
The passage introduces a condition: the subject must be prepared for the existent opposite. For example, blindness (non-existent) only applies if the subject is capable of sight.
This condition will be elaborated in subsequent text.
4
Reflect on why this distinction matters
This classification is used in arguments about attributes like knowledge and power, and in discussions of God's attributes being distinct yet not separate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : فإذا كانا وجوديين، فإن كان تعقل كل منهما بالقياس إلى الآخر فمتضايفان، وإلا فمتضادان وإن كان أحدهما عدمياً، فإن تقيد بكون الموضوع مستعداً للوجودي بحسب شخصه أو نوعه
He said: If they are both existent, then if each is intelligible with relation to the other, they are correlatives; otherwise they are contraries. And if one of them is non-existent, then if it is restricted to the subject being prepared for the existent according to its individual or its species.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage further classifies opposites. The commentator quotes the original text: If both opposites are existent (vujudiyyan), then if each one is conceptualized in relation to the other (e.g., father and son, double and half), they are called correlatives (mutaddayifan). If not (e.g., black and white, which are understood without reference to each other), they are called contraries (mutadaddan). If one of the opposites is non-existent ('adamiyyan), such as blindness (absence of sight) and sight, then it is further qualified by whether the subject is disposed to receive the existent in its individual form (like a specific person) or its species (like the species of animals for sight). This sets the stage for the standard kalam subdivision of opposites into correlatives, contraries, privation and possession, and affirmation and negation (though only part is given here).

Key Terms
وجوديين
متضايفان
متضادان
عدمي
مستعد
بالقياس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All opposites are the same type.
Correction: Opposites are classified: existent ones into correlatives and contraries; non-existent ones require a disposed subject.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what are the two types of opposites when both are existent?
A) Correlatives and privations.
B) Correlatives and contraries.
C) Contraries and possession-and-privation.
D) Affirmation and negation.

Key Takeaway
Opposites in kalam are classified based on existence and relational intelligibility into correlatives and contraries, with a special condition for non-existent ones.

Objection and Answer on Opposition in Propositions
Addresses objection and summarizes fourfold opposition classification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author resolves the objection that propositions can be opposites without a subject, and you will learn the classification of opposition into four types with examples.

Core Idea
Opposition in propositions is based on truth in themselves, not on a concrete subject.

This passage presents a subtle objection: if opposition requires a subject, how can propositions be opposites? The answer clarifies that propositions can be considered in terms of their truth values independently, or the subject is the locus of judgment. It then summarizes the four-fold classification of opposition, providing examples like correlatives, contraries, and privation/possession.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Carefully read the objection that propositions can be opposites without conceiving a subject, and the author's reply. Notice the two ways he offers to understand this.
The answer distinguishes between considering truth in itself vs. truth about something.
2
Understand the two approaches
The first approach is based on commonality (ishterak) of the truth values themselves. The second treats the subject as the locus of affirmation and negation. Grasp the difference.
3
Examine the classification of opposition
The author then enumerates the four divisions of opposition: correlatives, contraries, privation/possession, and contradiction. Note the basis: whether opposites are both existential or one existential and one non-existential.
The passage is cut off partway through the privation/possession example.
4
Identify the examples
Examples are given: correlatives like paternity/sonship, contraries like black/white, and privation/possession like lack of beard for a beardless person or woman. Connect each example to its definition.
The classification matches what was taught previously in the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : قد تتقابل القضايا تناقضاً وتضاداً من غير تصور محل . قلنا : بحسب الاشتراك كسائر نسب المفردات يكون في القضايا باعتبار صدقها في نفسها، لا صدقها على شيء، أو بحسب أن موضوع القضية مورد للإيجاب والسلب. يريد حصر أقسام التقابل في الأربعة ومبناه على أن المتقابلين يكونان وجوديين، أو وجودياً وعدمياً، فإن كانا وجوديين، فإن كان تعقل كل منهما بالقياس إلى تعقل الآخر، فمتضايفان كالأبوة والبنوة، وإلا فمتضادان كالسواد والبياض وإن كان أحدهما عدمياً، والآخر وجوديا، فإن اعتبر في العدمى، كون الموضوع قابلاً للوجودي بحسب شخصه كعدم اللحية عن الشخص الأ.مرد أو نوعه كعدم اللحية عن المرأة، أو جنسه القريب كعدم اللحية عن الشخص الأ.مرد أو نوعه كعدم اللحية عن المرأة، أو جنسه القريب
For it may be said: Propositions may be opposed by contradiction and contrariety without conceiving a subject. We say: [This is] in terms of commonality, like the other relations of terms, in propositions by considering their truth in themselves, not their truth about something, or in terms of that the subject of the proposition is the locus of affirmation and negation. He intends to enumerate the divisions of opposition into four, and its basis is that the two opposites are either both existential, or one existential and one non-existential. If they are both existential, then if each is understood relative to the understanding of the other, they are correlatives like paternity and sonship; otherwise they are contraries like blackness and whiteness. If one is non-existential and the other existential, then if consideration is given in the non-existential to the subject being capable of the existential according to its individual—like lack of a beard for an individual beardless person—or its species—like lack of a beard for a woman—or its proximate genus...
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage contains an objection and answer regarding whether opposition applies to propositions (qadaya) without a subject. The objection is that propositions can be contradictory or contrary without needing a subject. The answer provides two ways to understand this: either through commonality (ishterak) like other relations, considering the truth values themselves, or by considering that the subject of the proposition is the locus of affirmation/negation. Then the passage continues to summarize the classification of the four divisions of opposition, reiterating the basis: both opposites are existential or one existential one non-existential, and then applying the conditions. The summary covers the same classification as before but in a more concise form, adding examples: correlatives like paternity/sonship, contraries like black/white, and privation/possession with examples of beard for individual/man/woman. The text is cut off mid-sentence due to the page break, but the summary intention is clear.

Correction: In propositional logic, opposition can be based on the truth values of the propositions themselves (considered in themselves) or by taking the subject as the locus of judgment, so a concrete external subject is not always needed.

Classifying Opposition in Logic: The Four Types
Classification of opposition when one opposite negates the other.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Islamic logicians classify opposition into four types based on whether one opposite is a removal of the other.

Core Idea
Opposition types depend on whether one opposite negates the other.

In logic and kalam, opposition (taqābul) is a key concept for understanding relations between terms. This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid presents a classification system that distinguishes four types based on a single criterion: whether one opposite is a removal (rafʿ) of the other. This criterion gives rise to two pairs of opposition types.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without overthinking. Then read the literal translation to get a sense of the author's meaning.
Pay attention to the examples: impossibility/non-impossibility and blindness/non-blindness.
2
Identify the criterion: removal (rafʿ)
Notice that the author sets up a condition: if one opposite is a removal (rafʿ) of the other, then the opposition falls into one of two categories. Otherwise, it falls into the other two.
'Removal' means the negation or absence of the other opposite.
3
Map out the four types
From the criterion, derive the four types: possession and privation (if the subject has capacity), affirmation and negation (if no capacity), correlation (mutual relation), and contrariety (extreme difference).
The passage only names them; the commentary explains their definitions.
4
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to see if you can apply the classification rule correctly.
The question is based directly on the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا لم يمتنع أن يكون نقيض العدمي عدمياً كالامتناع واللامتناع، والعمى واللا عمى بمعنى رفعه أعم من البصر، وعدم الاستعداد له، فالأقرب أن يقال إن كان أحد المتقابلين رفعاً للآخر فملكة وعدم، أو إيجاب وسلب، وإلا فتضايف، أو تضاد على ما ذكر
And if it is not impossible that the opposite of a privative be privative – like impossibility and non-impossibility, and blindness and non-blindness in the sense of its removal being more general than sight, and the lack of disposition for it – then the nearest [approach] is to say: if one of the two opposites is a removal of the other, then it is [opposition of] possession and privation, or affirmation and negation; otherwise, it is correlation or contrariety, according to what has been mentioned.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage provides a classification of the four types of opposition (taqābul). It begins by establishing that it is not impossible for the opposite of a privative (ʿadamī) to itself be privative. Examples given are imtināʿ (impossibility) and lā-imtināʿ (non-impossibility), and ʿamā (blindness) and lā-ʿamā (non-blindness) where the negation is broader than the positive attribute. Given this possibility, the author proposes the best way to classify: if one of the two opposites is a removal (rafʿ) of the other, then the opposition is either possession and privation (milk wa ʿadam) if the subject has the capacity for the positive, or affirmation and negation (ījāb wa salb) if no such capacity is considered. If neither is a removal of the other, then the opposition is either correlation (taḍāyuf) if each is conceived relative to the other, or contrariety (taḍād) otherwise. This classification is established 'according to what has been mentioned,' referring to prior discussion.

Key Terms
تقابل (taqābul)
عدميّ (ʿadamī)
رفع (rafʿ)
ملكة وعدم (milk wa ʿadam)
إيجاب وسلب (ījāb wa salb)
تضايف (taḍāyuf)
تضاد (taḍād)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A privative (negative) term cannot be opposite to another privative.
Correction: The passage shows it is possible, as in 'impossibility' and 'non-impossibility', both privatives.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, if one opposite is the removal (rafʿ) of the other, what are the two possible types of opposition?
A) Correlation and contrariety
B) Possession and privation, or affirmation and negation
C) Possession and privation only
D) Affirmation and negation only

Key Takeaway
The classification of opposition depends on whether one opposite is a removal of the other, leading to two pairs: possession/privation and affirmation/negation (if removal), or correlation and contrariety (if not).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily struggles with sin, do you treat your failings as mere absences of virtue (privation) or as active contraries? How does this classification help you identify whether you need to acquire a virtue or uproot a vice?
?
When you encounter someone who holds a belief opposite to yours, do you automatically assume their position is simply the negation ('removal') of yours? Could it be a genuine contrary or a correlative perspective? How can you apply this taxonomy to listen more carefully?
?
Have you ever mistaken a case of affirmation/negation (like true/false) for a mere privation (like lacking knowledge)? Where in your life might you be confusing your own absence of understanding with a positive error, or vice versa? What will you do differently now?

Objection Against Opposition of Two Privatives
Raises and answers the objection that two privatives cannot be opposites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical objection that two privatives cannot be opposites and the nuanced response involving propositional opposition.

Core Idea
Two privatives can oppose each other in propositions, not just in subjects.

This passage presents a refined logical objection in kalam: how can two privatives (like non-blackness and non-whiteness) be opposites if they can coexist in a third thing? The objection is that the condition of unity of subject fails. The author then provides a counter and answer, showing that opposition can occur in propositions where the subject is implied by the proposition itself. Understanding this deepens your grasp of how kalam treats negation and opposition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation to grasp the flow.
2
Identify the objection
The objection states no opposition between privatives. Note the two subcases: absolute vs qualified and two qualified.
3
Understand the counterexample
The example of non-blackness and non-whiteness both applying to redness shows coexistence, violating the requirement of a single subject.
4
Grasp the answer about propositions
The answer uses propositional logic: opposition can be based on truth values in themselves or subject of proposition as locus.
5
Reflect on the significance
This shows that opposition is not limited to subjects but extends to propositions, crucial for kalam's use of logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يقال : لا تقابل بين العدميين، أما المطلق والمضاف فظاهر، وأما المضافان فلاجتماعهما في غير ما أضيفا إليه كاللاسواد واللابياض في الأحمر، ولكون التقابل مشروطاً بوحدة الموضوع، وبهذا خرج مثل الإنسانية مع الفرسية، والملزوم مع عدم اللازم. وفيه نظر . فإن قيل : قد تتقابل القضايا تناقضاً وتضاداً من غير تصور محل . قلنا : بحسب الاشتراك كسائر نسب المفردات يكون في القضايا باعتبار صدقها في نفسها، لا صدقها على شيء، أو بحسب أن موضوع القضية مورد للإيجاب والسلب.
And it may be said: There is no opposition between two privatives. As for the absolute and the qualified, it is obvious; as for two qualified [privatives], because they come together in something other than that to which they are attributed, like non-blackness and non-whiteness in redness, and because opposition is conditioned by unity of subject. By this, examples like humanity and horseness, and the necessary [condition] with the absence of the necessary, are excluded. But there is consideration [of this]. For if it is said: Propositions can be opposed by contradiction and contrariety without conceiving a subject. We say: By virtue of commonality, like other relations of singulars, this occurs in propositions with respect to their truth in themselves, not their truth about something; or by virtue that the subject of the proposition is the locus of affirmation and negation.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer regarding the possibility of opposition between two privatives (ʿadamiyyān). The objection states that there is no opposition between two privatives. First, if one privative is absolute (muṭlaq) and the other qualified (muḍāf), it is obvious that they are not opposites. Second, if both are qualified, they can coexist in something else—for example, non-blackness and non-whiteness both apply to redness. Since opposition requires a single subject, they cannot be opposites. Examples like humanity and horseness (which are contraries but not privatives?) and the necessary condition with its absence are also excluded. The author then notes that this objection is open to scrutiny. An interlocutor might argue that propositions (qaḍāyā) can be opposed by contradiction (tanāquḍ) or contrariety (taḍād) without requiring a specific subject. The answer is that such propositional opposition is based on commonality (ishtirāk) similar to relations of singular terms: it considers the truth of the propositions in themselves, not their truth about something; alternatively, the subject of the proposition serves as the locus for affirmation and negation, thus maintaining unity of subject indirectly.

Key Terms
عدمان
عدميان
مطلق
مضاف
وحدة الموضوع
تقابل
تناقض
تضاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Two privatives can never be opposites because they can coexist in a third thing.
Correction: The answer shows that two privatives can be opposites in propositions, where the subject is not required to be single; opposition is based on truth values or the subject as locus.

Checkpoint
What reason does the objection give for why two qualified privatives cannot be opposites?
A) They are always absolute
B) They can coexist in something else, like non-blackness and non-whiteness in redness
C) They require a single subject but the subject is not specified
D) They are not real privatives

Key Takeaway
Two privatives can be opposed in propositions when considering their truth values, not just their application to a subject.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself denying both a claim and its opposite (two privatives in your own reasoning), are you comfortable with that logical stance? How does this lesson's distinction between subject-level and proposition-level opposition challenge your tendency to avoid holding such a position?
?
In your daily decisions, do you ever treat a lack of evidence as evidence of lack (a privative), and if so, how does the lesson's treatment of opposition in propositions force you to reconsider the truth value of such judgments?
?
The lesson argues that propositions can oppose each other based on truth in themselves, not just about a subject. Where in your own beliefs do you accept contradictory propositions because you think they 'apply to different subjects'? Are you willing to examine whether they actually oppose each other in truth?

The Fourfold Division of Opposition: Existential and Privative
Presents the standard fourfold division of opposition based on existential and privative status.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the standard classification of opposites into four categories based on whether the opposites are both existential or one existential and one privative.

Core Idea
Opposition classification starts with existence: both existing or one missing.

Before we can discuss the details of each type of opposition, we need a clear foundation. The author begins by presenting the standard division that has been widely accepted among scholars. This division is built on a simple distinction: are both opposites existential, or is one of them privative? This lesson will walk through the first two levels of this classification.

Guided Path
1
Understand the basis
The classification depends on whether the two opposites are both existential or one existential and one privative.
2
Identify correlatives and contraries
If both are existential, they are either correlatives (if each conceived relative to the other) or contraries (otherwise).
Examples: fatherhood/sonship (correlatives), blackness/whiteness (contraries).
3
Explore the privative case
If one is privative and the other existential, consider the subject's capacity for the existential attribute.
4
Differentiate possession/privation from affirmation/negation
If capacity is considered, it's possession and privation; otherwise, it's affirmation and negation.
Capacity can be at individual, species, or genus levels.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يريد حصر أقسام التقابل في الأربعة ومبناه على أن المتقابلين يكونان وجوديين، أو وجودياً وعدمياً، فإن كانا وجوديين، فإن كان تعقل كل منهما بالقياس إلى تعقل الآخر، فمتضايفان كالأبوة والبنوة، وإلا فمتضادان كالسواد والبياض وإن كان أحدهما عدمياً، والآخر وجوديا، فإن اعتبر في العدمي، كون الموضوع قابلاً للوجودي بحسب شخصه كعدم اللحية عن الشخص الأملح أو نوعه كعدم اللحية عن المرأة، أو جنسه القريب كعدم اللحية عن الفرس أو جنسه البعيد كعدم اللحية عن الشجر، فهما متقابلان تقابل الملكة والعدم وإن لم يعتبر ذلك السواد واللاسواد، فتقابل الإيجاب والسلب، إلا أنه لا دليل على امتناع أن يكون المتقابلان عدميين . كيف وقد أطبق المتأخرون على أن نقيض العدمي، قد يكون عدمياً، كالامتناع واللامتناع والعمى واللاعمى، بمعنى رفع العمى وسلبه أعم من أن يكون هو باعتبار الاتصاف بالبصر، أو باعتبار عدم القابلية له، فما يقال أن اللاعمى إما عبارة عن البصر فيكون وجودياً، وإما عن عدم قابلية المحل للبصر، فيكون سلباً لأمر وجودي، ليس بشيء، وإذا جاز أن يكونا عدميين . فالأولى أن يبين الحصر بوجه يشملهما كما يقال : المتقابلان إن كان أحدهما سلباً للآخر، فإن اعتبر في السلب استعداد المحل في الجملة لما أضيف إليه السلب فتقابلها تقابل الملكة والعدم، وإلا فتقابل الإيجاب والسلب، وإن لم يكن أحدهما سلباً للآخر، فإن كان تعقل كل منهما بالقياس إلى الآخر، فتقابلها التضايف، وإلا فالتضاد، وقد يستدل على لزوم كون أحد المتقابلين وجودياً، بأنه لا تقابل بين العدم المطلق والمضاف، ضرورة صدق المطلق على المقيد، ولا بين العدمين المضافين لوجهين أحدهما : أنهما يجتمعان في غير ما وقع الإضافة إليه إما بطريق الصدق، فلأنه يصدق على الأحمر، أنه لا أسود ولا أبيض، وإما بطريق الوجود فلأنه قد وجد فيه الحمرة التي هي لا سواد، ولا بياض.
He intends to enumerate the divisions of opposition into four, and its basis is that the two opposites are either both existential, or one existential and one privative. If both are existential, then if each is conceived relative to the conception of the other, they are correlatives (e.g., fatherhood and sonship); otherwise they are contraries (e.g., blackness and whiteness). If one is privative and the other existential, then if consideration is given in the privative to the subject being capable of the existential according to its individual (e.g., absence of beard from a bald person) or its type (e.g., absence of beard from a woman) or its proximate genus (e.g., absence of beard from a horse) or its remote genus (e.g., absence of beard from a tree), then they are opposites in the opposition of possession and privation; and if that consideration is not made (like blackness and non-blackness), then it is the opposition of affirmation and negation. However, there is no proof that it is impossible for the two opposites to be both privative. Indeed, the later scholars have universally agreed that the opposite of a privative may be privative, like impossibility and non-impossibility, and blindness and non-blindness, meaning the removal and negation of blindness which is more general than [the opposite] being either by virtue of being characterized by sight or by virtue of the subject's incapacity for sight. So what is said – that non-blindness either expresses sight (hence existential) or expresses the subject's incapacity for sight (hence a negation of an existential) – is nothing. And if it is permissible for them to be both privative, then it is better to present the enumeration in a way that includes them both, as it is said: The two opposites, if one is a negation of the other: if consideration is given in the negation to the subject's disposition in general for what the negation is attributed to, then their opposition is the opposition of possession and privation; otherwise it is the opposition of affirmation and negation. And if neither is a negation of the other: if each is conceived relative to the other, then their opposition is correlation; otherwise it is contrariety. And a proof is given for the necessity that one of the two opposites be existential: because there is no opposition between an absolute privative and a qualified privative (since the absolute is true of the qualified), nor between two qualified privatives for two reasons: one, because they can come together in something other than that to which they are attributed – either by way of truth, because it is true of redness that it is neither black nor white; or by way of existence, because redness itself is found in it, which is non-black and non-white.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage is a comprehensive exposition of the fourfold division of opposition (taqābul) and a proof that one opposite must be existential. It begins by stating that the division is based on whether the two opposites are both existential (wujūdiyyān), or one existential and one privative (ʿadamī). If both existential, they are either correlatives (e.g., fatherhood and sonship) if each is conceived relative to the other, otherwise they are contraries (e.g., blackness and whiteness). If one is existential and the other privative, the privative is further categorized by whether the subject has capacity for the existential attribute – at the level of individual, species, proximate genus, or remote genus. If such capacity is considered, the opposition is possession and privation (milk wa ʿadam); otherwise it is affirmation and negation (ījāb wa salb). The author then notes that there is no proof against two privatives being opposites. In fact, later scholars (al-mutaʾakhkhirūn) have agreed that the opposite of a privative can be privative, as with impossibility/non-impossibility and blindness/non-blindness. The common objection that non-blindness must be either sight (existential) or incapacity (a negation) is dismissed as baseless. Since two privatives can be opposites, the author provides a broader classification: if one is the negation of the other, then depending on whether the subject's disposition is considered, it is either possession/privation or affirmation/negation; otherwise, based on mutual conceptual dependence, it is either correlation or contrariety. Finally, a proof is given that one of the two opposites must be existential: an absolute privative cannot oppose a qualified privative because the absolute applies to the qualified, and two qualified privatives cannot oppose each other because they can coexist in a third thing (e.g., non-blackness and non-whiteness in redness), either by truth or by existence.

Key Terms
وجودي (wujūdī)
عدمي (ʿadamī)
تضايف (taḍāyuf)
تضاد (taḍād)
ملكة وعدم (milk wa ʿadam)
إيجاب وسلب (ījāb wa salb)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All opposites where one is privative are of the type affirmation and negation.
Correction: The passage shows that if the subject is considered capable of the existential attribute, it is possession and privation, not simple affirmation and negation.

Checkpoint
If both opposites are existential and each is conceived relative to the other, what is the type of opposition?
A) Contrariety
B) Correlation
C) Possession and privation
D) Affirmation and negation

Key Takeaway
The standard fourfold division categorizes opposites based on existential status and, when one is privative, on the subject's capacity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own ignorance, do you treat it as a simple negation ('I do not know') or as a privation of knowledge in a subject capable of knowing? How does that distinction change your sense of responsibility to seek knowledge?
?
In your relationships, do you sometimes see opposition as mere contradiction (right/wrong) when it might actually be a case of correlatives (each exists relative to the other)? How might that shift your approach to conflict?
?
The classification of opposition depends on the capacity of the subject. In what area of your life are you assuming you lack a capacity (and thus excuse yourself) when in fact you possess it and are failing to act? What will you do about it this week?

Broader Classification and the Proof for One Existential Opposite
Presents the later scholars' amendment and the broader classification with proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that later scholars accept privative-privative opposition and how the classification can be extended, along with a proof that one opposite must be existential.

Core Idea
Later scholars allow two privatives as opposites; yet proof still requires one existential.

The standard classification seems to imply that one opposite must always be existential, but later scholars recognized that two privatives can also be opposites. This lesson examines their reasoning and presents a broader classification that accommodates this, as well as a proof that still anchors one opposite in existence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the exception
Later scholars agree that two privatives can be opposites, e.g., impossibility and non-impossibility.
2
Refute the objection
The claim that non-blindness must be existential is dismissed as baseless.
3
Learn the broader classification
The author provides a more general division that includes cases where both are privative.
The classification is based on whether one is negation of the other and whether the subject's disposition is considered.
4
Explore the proof
A rational proof shows that one opposite must be existential: absolute and qualified privation cannot oppose, and two qualified privations can coexist.
The example of redness being neither black nor white demonstrates coexistence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يريد حصر أقسام التقابل في الأربعة ومبناه على أن المتقابلين يكونان وجوديين، أو وجودياً وعدمياً، فإن كانا وجوديين، فإن كان تعقل كل منهما بالقياس إلى تعقل الآخر، فمتضايفان كالأبوة والبنوة، وإلا فمتضادان كالسواد والبياض وإن كان أحدهما عدمياً، والآخر وجوديا، فإن اعتبر في العدمي، كون الموضوع قابلاً للوجودي بحسب شخصه كعدم اللحية عن الشخص الأملح أو نوعه كعدم اللحية عن المرأة، أو جنسه القريب كعدم اللحية عن الفرس أو جنسه البعيد كعدم اللحية عن الشجر، فهما متقابلان تقابل الملكة والعدم وإن لم يعتبر ذلك السواد واللاسواد، فتقابل الإيجاب والسلب، إلا أنه لا دليل على امتناع أن يكون المتقابلان عدميين . كيف وقد أطبق المتأخرون على أن نقيض العدمي، قد يكون عدمياً، كالامتناع واللامتناع والعمى واللاعمى، بمعنى رفع العمى وسلبه أعم من أن يكون هو باعتبار الاتصاف بالبصر، أو باعتبار عدم القابلية له، فما يقال أن اللاعمى إما عبارة عن البصر فيكون وجودياً، وإما عن عدم قابلية المحل للبصر، فيكون سلباً لأمر وجودي، ليس بشيء، وإذا جاز أن يكونا عدميين . فالأولى أن يبين الحصر بوجه يشملهما كما يقال : المتقابلان إن كان أحدهما سلباً للآخر، فإن اعتبر في السلب استعداد المحل في الجملة لما أضيف إليه السلب فتقابلها تقابل الملكة والعدم، وإلا فتقابل الإيجاب والسلب، وإن لم يكن أحدهما سلباً للآخر، فإن كان تعقل كل منهما بالقياس إلى الآخر، فتقابلها التضايف، وإلا فالتضاد، وقد يستدل على لزوم كون أحد المتقابلين وجودياً، بأنه لا تقابل بين العدم المطلق والمضاف، ضرورة صدق المطلق على المقيد، ولا بين العدمين المضافين لوجهين أحدهما : أنهما يجتمعان في غير ما وقع الإضافة إليه إما بطريق الصدق، فلأنه يصدق على الأحمر، أنه لا أسود ولا أبيض، وإما بطريق الوجود فلأنه قد وجد فيه الحمرة التي هي لا سواد، ولا بياض.
He intends to enumerate the divisions of opposition into four, and its basis is that the two opposites are either both existential, or one existential and one privative. If both are existential, then if each is conceived relative to the conception of the other, they are correlatives (e.g., fatherhood and sonship); otherwise they are contraries (e.g., blackness and whiteness). If one is privative and the other existential, then if consideration is given in the privative to the subject being capable of the existential according to its individual (e.g., absence of beard from a bald person) or its type (e.g., absence of beard from a woman) or its proximate genus (e.g., absence of beard from a horse) or its remote genus (e.g., absence of beard from a tree), then they are opposites in the opposition of possession and privation; and if that consideration is not made (like blackness and non-blackness), then it is the opposition of affirmation and negation. However, there is no proof that it is impossible for the two opposites to be both privative. Indeed, the later scholars have universally agreed that the opposite of a privative may be privative, like impossibility and non-impossibility, and blindness and non-blindness, meaning the removal and negation of blindness which is more general than [the opposite] being either by virtue of being characterized by sight or by virtue of the subject's incapacity for sight. So what is said – that non-blindness either expresses sight (hence existential) or expresses the subject's incapacity for sight (hence a negation of an existential) – is nothing. And if it is permissible for them to be both privative, then it is better to present the enumeration in a way that includes them both, as it is said: The two opposites, if one is a negation of the other: if consideration is given in the negation to the subject's disposition in general for what the negation is attributed to, then their opposition is the opposition of possession and privation; otherwise it is the opposition of affirmation and negation. And if neither is a negation of the other: if each is conceived relative to the other, then their opposition is correlation; otherwise it is contrariety. And a proof is given for the necessity that one of the two opposites be existential: because there is no opposition between an absolute privative and a qualified privative (since the absolute is true of the qualified), nor between two qualified privatives for two reasons: one, because they can come together in something other than that to which they are attributed – either by way of truth, because it is true of redness that it is neither black nor white; or by way of existence, because redness itself is found in it, which is non-black and non-white.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage is a comprehensive exposition of the fourfold division of opposition (taqābul) and a proof that one opposite must be existential. It begins by stating that the division is based on whether the two opposites are both existential (wujūdiyyān), or one existential and one privative (ʿadamī). If both existential, they are either correlatives (e.g., fatherhood and sonship) if each is conceived relative to the other, otherwise they are contraries (e.g., blackness and whiteness). If one is existential and the other privative, the privative is further categorized by whether the subject has capacity for the existential attribute – at the level of individual, species, proximate genus, or remote genus. If such capacity is considered, the opposition is possession and privation (milk wa ʿadam); otherwise it is affirmation and negation (ījāb wa salb). The author then notes that there is no proof against two privatives being opposites. In fact, later scholars (al-mutaʾakhkhirūn) have agreed that the opposite of a privative can be privative, as with impossibility/non-impossibility and blindness/non-blindness. The common objection that non-blindness must be either sight (existential) or incapacity (a negation) is dismissed as baseless. Since two privatives can be opposites, the author provides a broader classification: if one is the negation of the other, then depending on whether the subject's disposition is considered, it is either possession/privation or affirmation/negation; otherwise, based on mutual conceptual dependence, it is either correlation or contrariety. Finally, a proof is given that one of the two opposites must be existential: an absolute privative cannot oppose a qualified privative because the absolute applies to the qualified, and two qualified privatives cannot oppose each other because they can coexist in a third thing (e.g., non-blackness and non-whiteness in redness), either by truth or by existence.

امتناع (imtināʿ)
عمى (ʿamā)
سلب (salb)
استعداد (istiʿdād)
محل (maḥall)

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Two privatives can never be true opposites.
Correction: Later scholars agree that two privatives can be opposites (e.g., impossibility and non-impossibility), and the broader classification includes such cases.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why can two qualified privatives not be opposites?
A) They are always identical
B) They can coexist in a single subject
C) The absolute always applies
D) They are both existential

Key Takeaway
Later scholars expanded the fourfold division to include privative-privative opposition, and rational proof still requires one opposite to be existential.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you avoid a sin, do you consider that avoidance as merely an absence of sin (a privation) or as an active existential good deed? The lesson shows that for true opposition, one must be existential. What does your avoidance reflect about your faith?
?
The proof states that two qualified privatives can coexist, like redness being both non-black and non-white. In your heart, can you hold humility and gratitude simultaneously? Are there any virtues you mistakenly treat as opposites? How does this lesson challenge you to embrace the coexistence of good qualities?
?
Later scholars expanded the categories to include privative-privative opposition. Have you ever reduced a complex spiritual truth to a false dichotomy, such as 'either you are perfect or you are a failure'? Where in your understanding of tawheed or iman might you be applying a simplistic either-or when the reality is more nuanced?

The Four Types of Opposition (Taqābul) Defined
Sanusi classifies the four types of opposition using two binary criteria.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four types of logical opposition and how they are distinguished based on the presence of negation and the role of subject disposition.

Core Idea
Opposition is divided by negation and subject disposition into four distinct types.

This passage from Sanusi's Sharh al-Maqasid introduces a foundational logical classification: the four types of opposition (taqābul). Before diving into theological arguments, a student must grasp these precise distinctions, as they govern how propositions relate. Read closely to see how two binary splits generate the complete set.

Guided Path
1
Understand the initial division
Identify the first split: one opposite is a negation of the other vs. not.
2
Explore the first branch
When one is a negation, note the condition about subject disposition.
3
Explore the second branch
When neither is a negation, look at conceptual interdependence.
4
Synthesize the four types
List all four types and their defining criteria.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المتقابلان إن كان أحدهما سلباً للآخر، فإن اعتبر في السلب استعداد المحل في الجملة لما أضيف إليه السلب فتقابلها تقابل الملكة والعدم، وإلا فتقابل الإيجاب والسلب، وإن لم يكن أحدهما سلباً للآخر، فإن كان تعقل كل منهما بالقياس إلى الآخر، فتقابلها التضايف، وإلا فالتضاد.
The two opposites: if one is a negation of the other, then if a disposition of the subject is considered in the negation for that to which the negation is added, their opposition is the opposition of possession and privation (taqābul al-mulka wa al-ʿadam); otherwise it is the opposition of affirmation and negation (taqābul al-ījāb wa al-salb). If one is not a negation of the other, then if each is conceived in relation to the other, their opposition is the opposition of correlation (taqābul al-taḍāyuf); otherwise it is the opposition of contrariety (taqābul al-taḍādd).
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage defines the four recognized types of opposition (taqābul) in logic. It begins by dividing opposites into two categories: those where one is a negation of the other, and those where neither is a negation. In the first category, a further distinction is made based on whether the negation considers the disposition (istiʿdād) of the subject. If it does, the opposition is called 'possession and privation' (e.g., sight and blindness, where the subject must be capable of sight). If not, it is simply 'affirmation and negation' (e.g., 'Socrates is sitting' vs. 'Socrates is not sitting'). In the second category, if the two opposites are conceptually interdependent (each understood only by relation to the other), they are 'correlatives' (e.g., father and son). Otherwise, they are 'contraries' (e.g., black and white). The passage thus presents a complete classification based on clear logical criteria.

Key Terms
تقابل
تقابل الملكة والعدم
تقابل الإيجاب والسلب
تقابل التضايف
تقابل التضاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All types of opposition are fundamentally the same.
Correction: The four types differ in logical structure: some involve negation, others do not; among those with negation, subject disposition matters; among those without, conceptual interdependence matters.

Checkpoint
Which type of opposition requires that one opposite be a negation of the other and that the subject have a natural disposition for the opposite?
A) Contrariety
B) Possession and privation
C) Correlation
D) Affirmation and negation

Key Takeaway
The four types of opposition are derived from two binary distinctions: whether one is a negation of the other, and within that, whether subject disposition is considered; or if no negation, whether interdependence exists.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Sanusi teaches that correlatives (like father and son) are only understood in relation to each other. Consider your relationship with Allah: are you aware that your very existence is correlative to Him as Creator? Does that make you feel dependent or secure? How can you live today with that awareness, not as an abstract fact but as a reality that shapes your actions?
?
The category of possession and privation includes things like sight and blindness—privations that only exist where there is a natural capacity. Think of a good quality you lack, like patience or gratitude. Do you have a disposition for it? If so, your lack is a privation, not just a simple absence. How does that call you to take responsibility to develop that quality, knowing that you were created with the capacity for it?

Both Opposites Can Be Non-Existential
Argues that both opposites can be non-existential, citing examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that later scholars of kalam allow both opposites to be non-existential, as illustrated by the examples of impossibility and non-impossibility, and blindness and non-blindness.

Core Idea
Both opposites can be non-existential; impossibility and its negation prove it.

This passage tackles a subtle point in the logic of opposites: can both sides of a contradiction be non-existential? The author cites the consensus of later scholars that yes, they can, and provides concrete examples. Before diving into the text, consider how you would classify 'impossibility'—is it a positive state or a simple absence? The passage will show that both impossibility and its opposite are absences.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage with translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own argument come through before you interpret.
The passage begins with 'kayfa' which signals a question-objection style.
2
Identify the two examples
Find the two examples given: impossibility/non-impossibility and blindness/non-blindness. Notice that both are presented as pairs where each side is non-existential.
The author also addresses a potential objection about blindness/non-blindness.
3
Reflect on the misconception
Consider the common wrong belief that one opposite must be existential. The passage corrects this by showing that both can be pure absences.
This connects to the broader lesson on rational judgments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كيف وقد أطبق المتأخرون على أن نقيض العدمي، قد يكون عدمياً، كالامتناع واللامتناع والعمى واللاعمى، بمعنى رفع العمى وسلبه أعم من أن يكون هو باعتبار الاتصاف بالبصر، أو باعتبار عدم القابلية له، فما يقال أن اللاعمى. إما عبارة عن البصر فيكون وجودياً، وإما عن عدم قابلية المحل للبصر، فيكون سلباً لأمر وجودي، ليس بشيء، وإذا جاز أن يكونا عدميين . فالأولى أن يبين الحصر بوجه يشملهما
How so, when the later scholars (al-mutaʾakhkhirūn) are in agreement that the contradictory (naqīḍ) of a non-existential can itself be non-existential, such as impossibility (imtināʿ) and non-impossibility (lā-imtināʿ), and blindness (ʿamā) and non-blindness (lā-ʿamā), meaning the removal (rafʿ) of blindness and its negation (salb) is more general than being either by consideration of the possession of sight (bi-iʿtibār al-ittiṣāf bi al-baṣar) or by consideration of the lack of potentiality (ʿadam al-qābiliyya) for it. So what is said that non-blindness is either an expression of sight, in which case it is existential (wujūdī), or an expression of the lack of potentiality of the subject for sight, in which case it is a negation of an existential thing, which is nothing. And if it is permissible that both be non-existential, then it is more appropriate that the enumeration (al-ḥaṣr) be shown in a way that includes them.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage challenges the claim that one opposite must be existential (wujūdī). The author points out that later scholars unanimously hold that the contradictory (naqīḍ) of a non-existential can itself be non-existential. Examples include impossibility (imtināʿ) and its negation (lā-imtināʿ), both of which are non-existential, and blindness (ʿamā) and non-blindness (lā-ʿamā). The author explains that 'non-blindness' is ambiguous: it could mean sight (existential) or the lack of potentiality for sight (non-existential). The point is that if both can be non-existential, then the earlier claim that one opposite must be existential is not universally true, and the classification of opposites should be presented in a way that accommodates this possibility.

الوجودي
العدميان
نقيض العدمي
المتأخرون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That in any pair of opposites, one side must be existential (wujūdī).
Correction: The later scholars show that both can be non-existential, as with impossibility and non-impossibility.

Key Takeaway
Later scholars agree that both opposites can be non-existential, as seen in the example of impossibility and its negation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The passage describes blindness as a non-existential – a lack. Consider your own spiritual 'blindness' to certain truths. Do you treat it as a mere absence, or as a positive deficiency that requires urgent therapy? How does this distinction affect the way you seek guidance?

Non-blindness can be interpreted as either sight or the removal of blindness. Write down one specific area where you have taken your faith for granted.

What concrete change in your attitude toward them will you make today?

Proof That One Opposite Must Be Existential
Proves one opposite must be existential and resolves two counterexamples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why genuine opposites require a single subject, and how this principle resolves apparent counterexamples from theology.

Core Idea
Genuine opposites must share one subject; that is the test for real opposition.

This passage from Sanusi tackles a foundational issue in the logic of opposites (al-mutaqabilan). It demonstrates why one opposite must be existential, then immediately applies that rule to two tricky cases from theology: the opposition between humanity and horseness, and between a thing and the absence of its necessary condition. The argument is tight and reveals the conceptual precision required in kalam reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Let the author's flow come through.
Notice the dense, technical style of kalam argumentation.
2
Identify the main claim and its support
The passage argues that one opposite must be existential. It first rules out non-existences as genuine opposites by showing they don't meet the condition of mutual exclusivity on a single subject.
Focus on the two reasons given for why two qualified non-existences are not opposites.
3
Understand the condition 'bearing on one subject'
For two things to be genuine opposites, they must both be able to inhere in the same subject. If one cannot, they are not truly opposite.
This condition is key to resolving the counterexamples.
4
Apply the condition to the two examples
See how humanity/horseness and the necessary-absence pair fail the subject condition, so they are not real opposites.
The passage uses 'taqsī' (resolution) to dismiss these apparent counterexamples.
5
Reflect on the theological significance
Not every pair of mutually exclusive things is a genuine opposite. This precision matters for understanding divine attributes and creation.
Ask yourself: What kinds of pairs are not opposites? How does this affect theological claims?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يستدل على لزوم كون أحد المتقابلين وجودياً، بأنه لا تقابل بين العدم المطلق والمضاف، ضرورة صدق المطلق على المقيد، ولا بين العدمين المضافين لوجهين أحدهما: أنهما يجتمعان في غير ما وقع الإضافة إليه إما بطريق الصدق، فلأنه يصدق على الأحمر، أنه لا أسود ولا أبيض، وإما بطريق الوجود فلأنه قد وجد فيه الحمرة التي هي لا سواد، ولا بياض . وثانيهما: أن من شرط المتقابلين أن يكونا متواردين على موضوع واحد كما أشرنا إليه، وقد صرح به بعض المتأخرين، وموضوع العدمين المضافين كاللاسواد واللابياض متعدد ضرورة أنهما لو أضيفا إلى واحد لم يكونا عدمين، وبهذا يخرج التقصي عن إشكالين : أحدهما: أن مثل الإنسانية مع الفرسية داخل في حد المتقابلين ضرورة امتناع اجتماعهما مع أنه ليس أحد الأقسام الأربعة، أما غير التضاد فظاهر. وأما التضاد، فللإطباق على أنه لا تضاد بين الجواهر لامتناع ورودها على الموضوع وثانيهما: أن الملزوم وعدم اللازم كالسواد واللالون متقابلان ضرورة امتناع اجتماعها وليسا أحد الأقسام . أما السلب والإيجاب فلاجتماعهما على الكذب كما في البياض، وأما غيرهما فظاهر ووجه التقصي أن مثل هذا ليس من التقابل ، لا انتفاء التوارد على موضوع واحد
And it is argued for the necessity that one of the two opposites be existential, on the grounds that there is no opposition between absolute non-existence (al-ʿadam al-muṭlaq) and qualified (muḍāf) non-existence, because the absolute is true of the qualified, nor between two qualified non-existences for two reasons: First, they coexist in that to which the qualification is not applied, either by way of truth—for example, it is true of red that it is neither black nor white—or by way of existence, for the redness that exists is a non-black and non-white. Second, one of the conditions of opposites is that they both bear upon one and the same subject, as we have indicated, and some later scholars have stated this explicitly. However, the subject of two qualified non-existences, such as non-black and non-white, is multiple, for if they were predicated of one subject they would not be non-existences. And by this the resolution (taqṣī) emerges concerning two problems: One is that something like humanity and horseness falls within the definition of opposites, since they cannot coexist, yet it is not one of the four types—as for other than contrariety it is obvious, and as for contrariety, there is consensus that there is no contrariety between substances (jawāhir) because they cannot bear upon a subject. The second is that the necessary (malzūm) and the absence of the necessary (ʿadam al-lāzim), such as blackness and non-color, are opposites since they cannot coexist, yet they are not among the types—as for affirmation and negation, they both can be false (as in whiteness), and as for others it is obvious. The resolution (wa jh al-taqṣī) is that such cases are not genuine opposition due to the lack of bearing upon a single subject.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage presents a demonstration (istidlāl) for the necessity that one opposite be existential. The argument begins by noting that there is no opposition between absolute non-existence and qualified non-existence, nor between two qualified non-existences. Two reasons are given: first, two qualified non-existences can coexist in the same subject (e.g., red is both non-black and non-white), so they are not mutually exclusive; second, genuine opposites must bear upon a single subject (sharṭ al-tawārud ʿalā mawḍūʿ wāḥid), but two qualified non-existences like non-black and non-white have multiple subjects. This argument then resolves two apparent counterexamples: (a) humanity and horseness are opposites (they cannot coexist) but are substances, and substances are not considered contraries; (b) the necessary (malzūm) and the absence of the necessary (ʿadam al-lāzim) like blackness and non-color appear to be opposites but are not a genuine case of opposition. The resolution is that these are not real opposition because they do not meet the condition of bearing upon one subject. Thus, the claim that one opposite must be existential is upheld.

العدم المضاف
العدمين المضافين
شرط المتقابلين
التوارد على موضوع واحد
التقصي
الإشكالين
الإنسانية والفرسية
الملزوم وعدم اللازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That any two mutually exclusive things are genuine opposites (e.g., humanity and horseness, or blackness and non-black).
Correction: Genuine opposites require both to bear upon a single subject. Substances like humanity and horseness, or the absence of a necessary condition, do not inhere in a subject in the required way, so they are not opposites.

Checkpoint
Why are two qualified non-existences like non-black and non-white not considered genuine opposites?
A) They cannot coexist
B) They have multiple subjects
C) They are both existential
D) They are absolute non-existences

Key Takeaway
Genuine opposites must both bear upon a single subject; this rule clarifies why certain theological pairs are not true opposites.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about what is opposed to your faith, do you automatically assume any two non-compatible ideas are true opposites? How might you apply the test of 'bearing upon a single subject' to ensure you're not falsely equating incompatibility with opposition? What step can you take today to examine one such assumption you hold?
?

Based on this lesson, do these share a single subject in a way that makes them genuine opposites? Or could they be coexistent in the same subject (God)?
