Two Criticisms of the Resolution on Subject Unity
Critics raise two objections to the resolution's conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two criticisms of the resolution regarding subject unity in opposition, and why they challenge the earlier solution.

Core Idea
Two critiques show that the resolution's view of subject and non-existence is too narrow.

The previous resolution attempted to solve two problems by requiring subject unity. However, this resolution itself faces criticism. The critic raises two objections: one concerning the coexistence of non-existences, and another concerning the understanding of what 'subject' means in opposition. This lesson examines those objections and their implications for the theory of opposition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the two points introduced by 'أما أو لا' and 'أما ثانيا'.
The passage is a critique with two distinct arguments.
2
Understand the first criticism
The first criticism challenges the idea that two non-existences can coexist. It argues that this only holds when the addition (iḍāfa) of one to the other is not considered.
Examples given: non-black and non-white vs. blindness and non-blindness.
3
Understand the second criticism
The second criticism challenges the understanding of 'subject' (mawḍūʿ) in opposition. It argues that subject does not have to be a material substratum; it can be a logical subject, as in substances, or even prime matter for forms.
Ibn Sīnā's view on contrariety between forms is invoked.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how these criticisms undermine the resolution's claim. The resolution's requirement of subject unity is either insufficient or too restrictive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي هذا الكلام نظر أما أو لا فلأن ما ذكر من اجتماع العدمين، إنما يكون إذا لم يعتبر إضافة أحدهما إلى الآخر، كما في اللاسواد واللابياض بخلاف مثل العمى واللاعمى والامتناع واللامتناع . وأما ثانياً : فلأن الموضوع في التقابل ليس بمعنى المحل المقوم للحال حتى يلزم أن يكون المتقابلان من قبيل الأعراض البتة للقطع بتقابل الإيجاب والسلب في الجواهر مثل الفرسية واللافرسية، بل صرح ابن سينا بالتضاد بين الصور اعتباراً بالورود على المحل الذي هو الهيولي )
And in this statement there is a consideration (naẓar). First, because what was mentioned about the coexistence of two non-existences only occurs when the addition (iḍāfa) of one to the other is not considered, as in non-black (lā-sawād) and non-white (lā-bayāḍ), unlike such cases as blindness and non-blindness (ʿamā and lā-ʿamā) and impossibility and non-impossibility (imtināʿ and lā-imtināʿ). Second, because the subject (al-mawḍūʿ) in opposition is not meant in the sense of the substratum (al-maḥall) that constitutes a state (al-ḥāl) such that the opposites must necessarily be accidents (aʿrāḍ) at all, given the certainty of the opposition of affirmation and negation in substances (jawāhir) such as horseness and non-horseness (farasiyya wa lā-farasiyya). Rather, Ibn Sīnā explicitly stated that there is contrariety (taḍādd) between forms (ṣuwar) by consideration of bearing upon the substratum which is prime matter (al-hayūlā).
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage offers a criticism of the previous resolution. The critic raises two points. First, the argument that two non-existences can coexist (e.g., non-black and non-white) only holds when we do not consider the addition (iḍāfa) of one to the other. But in cases like blindness and non-blindness (ʿamā and lā-ʿamā), or impossibility and non-impossibility (imtināʿ and lā-imtināʿ), the non-existences are directly opposed precisely because they are related to each other by way of negation. So the resolution does not handle these cases. Second, the condition that opposites must bear upon a single subject (al-tawārud ʿalā mawḍūʿ wāḥid) does not mean that the subject must be a substratum (maḥall) that underlies accidents; otherwise, substances like horseness and non-horseness could never be opposites, yet we know that affirmation and negation applies to substances. Moreover, Ibn Sīnā even allows contrariety between forms (ṣuwar) when they bear upon the same substratum, namely prime matter (hayūlā). Thus, the resolution's understanding of 'subject' is too restrictive.

ابن سينا

Correction: The passage shows that opposition can occur in substances (logical subjects) and even between forms inhering in prime matter, so the subject is not necessarily a material substratum.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The resolution's conditions for opposition are not sufficient; the first criticism shows that non-existences can be opposed when their relation is considered, and the second shows that the subject can be logical or prime matter, not just a material substratum.

Condition of Opposites and Resolution of Two Problems
Author states condition of opposites and resolves two problems.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key condition of opposites in logic and how it resolves apparent cases of opposition that are not among the standard divisions.

Core Idea
Opposites must share a single subject — this rule solves tricky cases.

In this passage, the author establishes the necessary condition that true opposites must be concurrent on a single subject. He then applies this condition to defuse two apparent problems: universals like humanity and horseness, and the pair of a necessary concomitant and its negation. These seem to be opposites but are not, because they fail the single-subject condition.

Guided Path
1
Understand the condition of opposites
Read the opening statement: opposites must be concurrent upon a single subject. This is the foundational rule.
The author refers back to an earlier point and cites later scholars.
2
Note the exception for attributed non-existences
The two attributed non-existences (like non-blackness and non-whiteness) do not share a subject; their subjects are multiple, otherwise they would not be non-existences.
This prepares for the resolution.
3
Examine the first problem
Humanity and horseness cannot coexist, so they seem like opposites, but they are not among the four divisions. The author explains why they are not contraries.
He invokes consensus that substances have no contrariety.
4
Examine the second problem
Blackness and non-color are mutually exclusive, so they seem like opposites, but they are not negation and affirmation because both can be false (as in whiteness).
5
Understand the resolution
Both problems are resolved because these pairs lack concurrence upon a single subject — the essential condition for true opposition.
The resolution is that such cases are not opposition at all.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثانيهما: أن من شرط المتقابلين أن يكونا متواردين على موضوع واحد كما أشرنا إليه، وقد صرح به بعض المتأخرين، وموضوع العدمين المضافين كاللاسواد واللابياض متعدد ضرورة أنهما لو أضيفا إلى واحد لم يكونا عدمين، وبهذا يخرج التقصي عن إشكالين : أحدهما: أن مثل الإنسانية مع الفرسية داخل في حد المتقابلين ضرورة امتناع اجتماعهما مع أنه ليس أحد الأقسام الأربعة، أما غير التضاد فظاهر. وأما التضاد، فللإطباق على أنه لا تضاد بين الجواهر لامتناع ورودها على الموضوع. وثانيهما: أن الملزوم وعدم اللازم كالسواد واللالون متقابلان ضرورة امتناع اجتماعها وليسا أحد الأقسام . أما السلب والإيجاب فلاجتماعهما على الكذب كما في البياض، وأما غيرهما فظاهر ووجه التقصي أن مثل هذا ليس من التقابل ، لا انتفاء التوارد على موضوع واحد
And the second point: that among the conditions of opposites is that they must be concurrent upon a single subject as we pointed out, and some later scholars stated it explicitly. And the subject of the two attributed non-existences like non-blackness and non-whiteness is multiple necessarily because if they were attributed to one they would not be non-existences. And by this the resolution emerges from two problems: One of them: that like humanity and horseness fall under the definition of opposites necessarily because of the impossibility of their coexistence, while it is not one of the four divisions. As for other than contrariety it is obvious. And as for contrariety, due to consensus that there is no contrariety between substances because of the impossibility of their occurrence upon a subject. And the second: that the necessary concomitant and the non-existence of the necessary concomitant like blackness and non-color are opposites necessarily because of the impossibility of their coexistence, and they are not one of the divisions. As for negation and affirmation, because they both can be false as in whiteness, and as for other than them it is obvious. And the aspect of the resolution is that such as this is not from opposition, because the lack of concurrence upon a single subject.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage defines a key condition of opposites (al-mutaqābilayn): they must be concurrent upon a single subject (mawḍūʿ wāḥid). The author then applies this condition to a difficult case: the two 'attributed non-existences' like non-blackness and non-whiteness. He argues that their subject is multiple, because if they were attributed to one subject they would not be non-existences. From this condition, the resolution (al-taqaṣṣī) solves two problems. The first problem is that universals like humanity and horseness fall under the definition of opposites since they cannot coexist, yet they are not one of the four classical divisions of opposites. The author explains that they are not contraries (ḍidd) because there is consensus that substances have no contrariety. The second problem is that a necessary concomitant and its negation, like blackness and non-color, are opposites in the sense of mutual exclusion but are not among the four divisions. The author clarifies that they are not negation and affirmation because both can be false, as in the case of whiteness. The resolution is that such pairs are not true opposites because they lack concurrence upon a single subject.

Correction: The problems are actually puzzles that the condition resolves; they show the condition's explanatory power.

Key Takeaway
True opposites require a single subject; otherwise, apparent opposition is not genuine.

Four Objections to the Resolution of the Opposites Condition
Author raises four objections to the attempted resolution of the opposites condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand four objections raised against a proposed resolution to the condition of opposites in kalam logic.

Core Idea
Four objections challenge the resolution by redefining 'subject' and 'non-existence'.

The author has just proposed a resolution to the condition that opposites must share a single subject. Here, he anticipates criticisms. He presents four distinct objections, each targeting a different aspect of the resolution. These objections force us to reconsider what 'subject' means in logical opposition and how non-existences relate to their attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get an overview of the four objections.
Notice the clear enumeration: 'first', 'second', 'third', 'fourth'.
2
Analyze the first objection
The first objection concerns the attribution of one non-existence to another. Understand why blindness and non-blindness differ from non-blackness and non-whiteness.
The key is whether the non-existence is considered relative to another.
3
Examine the second and third objections
These objections redefine the 'subject' (mawḍūʿ) of opposites. The second rejects that subject must be a physical substrate; the third shows the subject can be specific, generic, or even more general.
Note the mention of Ibn Sīnā supporting contrariety in forms via prime matter.
4
Understand the fourth objection
The fourth objection clarifies that the discussion concerns non-existences like non-blackness, not non-existence attributed to blackness. This changes the subject.
Ask: 'What is the subject of non-whiteness according to this objection?'
5
Reflect on the overall purpose
Consider that these objections do not reject the condition of opposites itself but challenge the specific resolution. Think about how each point strengthens the original condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي هذا الكلام نظر أما أولاً فلأن ما ذكر من اجتماع العدمين، إنما يكون إذا لم يعتبر إضافة أحدهما إلى الآخر، كما في اللاسواد واللابياض بخلاف مثل العمى واللاعمى والامتناع واللامتناع . وأما ثانياً : فلأن الموضوع في التقابل ليس بمعنى المحل المقوم للحال حتى يلزم أن يكون المتقابلان من قبيل الأعراض البتة للقطع بتقابل الإيجاب والسلب في الجواهر مثل الفرسية واللافرسية، بل صرح ابن سينا بالتضاد بين الصور اعتباراً بالورود على المحل الذي هو الهيولي . وأما ثالثاً : فلتصريح ابن سينا وغيره بأن موضوع المتقابلين قد يكون واحداً شخصياً كزيد للعدل والجور أو نوعياً كالإنسان للرجولية والمرئية، أو جنسياً كالحيوان للذكورة والأنوثة، أو أعم من ذلك كالشيء للخير والشر. وأما رابعاً : فلأن الكلام في اللاسواد واللابياض لا في العدم المضاف إلى السواد، والعدم المضاف إلى البياض، ألا ترى أنك لا تقول باختلاف الموضوع في البياض واللابياض، نظراً إلى أن اللابياض عدم مضاف إلى البياض فيكون موضوعه البياض
And in this statement there is consideration. As for the first: because what was mentioned of the coexistence of the two non-existences occurs only when the attribution of one to the other is not considered, as in non-blackness and non-whiteness, unlike such as blindness and non-blindness, and impossibility and non-impossibility. And as for the second: because the subject (mawḍūʿ) in opposition is not in the sense of the substrate (maḥall) constitutive of the state, such that it would necessarily follow that opposites are entirely of the kind of accidents, since it is certain that negation and affirmation oppose each other in substances like horseness and non-horseness. Rather, Ibn Sīnā explicitly stated that there is contrariety between forms by consideration of their occurrence upon the substrate which is prime matter (hayūlā). And as for the third: because Ibn Sīnā and others explicitly stated that the subject of opposites may be individual like Zayd for justice and injustice, or specific like man for masculinity and femininity, or generic like animal for maleness and femaleness, or more general than that like thing for good and evil. And as for the fourth: because the discussion concerns non-blackness and non-whiteness, not non-existence attributed to blackness and non-existence attributed to whiteness. Do you not see that you do not say that the subject differs in whiteness and non-whiteness, considering that non-whiteness is a non-existence attributed to whiteness, so its subject is whiteness?
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage presents four objections (nazar) to the resolution that was proposed earlier. The first objection: The resolution claimed that the two non-existences cannot be opposites because they lack a single subject. But this only holds if we don't consider one attributed to the other. In cases like blindness and non-blindness, the non-existence is precisely the absence of sight in the eye, so the subject is the same. The second objection: The term 'subject' (mawḍūʿ) in the condition of opposites does not mean the physical substrate that supports a quality (maḥall); otherwise opposites would only be accidents. But opposites like horseness and non-horseness are substances, yet they still oppose each other. Ibn Sīnā even affirmed contrariety between forms based on prime matter. The third objection: Ibn Sīnā and others stated that the subject of opposites can be individual (like Zayd), specific (like man), generic (like animal), or even more general (like thing). Thus the subject need not be a single physical object; it can be a concept. The fourth objection: The resolution confuses the two non-existences (non-blackness and non-whiteness) with the non-existence attributed to a specific thing (like non-blackness as the absence of blackness). In the latter case, the subject is indeed the thing to which the non-existence is attributed (e.g., whiteness is the subject of non-whiteness). So the two non-existences do not have different subjects; they each have their own subject.

Key Terms
نظر
الموضوع
المحل
الهيولى
ابن سينا
التضاد بين الصور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The four objections reject the fundamental condition of opposites.
Correction: The objections are not against the condition itself, but against the specific resolution's application.

Checkpoint
According to the third objection, what did Ibn Sīnā and others state about the subject of opposites?
A) It must always be a physical substance.
B) It can be individual, specific, generic, or more general.
C) It must be prime matter.
D) It must be an accident.

Key Takeaway
The four objections clarify that the subject of opposites can be understood in multiple ways, and non-existences must be carefully distinguished from absences attributed to something.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

In what area of your faith might you be making a similar logical slip that leads to faulty conclusions? What will you do today to refine your thinking?
?
This passage shows that the 'subject' of opposites can be much broader than a physical object. Objection and Answer on Opposition in Propositions
Author addresses objection and gives two-part answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how logicians resolve the apparent problem of applying opposition (like contradiction and contrariety) to propositions, and the two key answers given.

Core Idea
Opposition applies differently to terms and propositions: terms in a substrate, propositions in existence.

This passage presents a common objection: propositions, being abstract entities, cannot occupy a physical substrate, yet we speak of contradictory and contrary propositions. The author provides a two-fold answer that clarifies the nature of opposition in logical contexts. Let's follow the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objection states that opposition occurs in propositions (like contradiction and contrariety) but propositions cannot have a substrate. Notice the examples given.
The objector considers 'Every animal is a human' and its logical opposites.
2
Examine the first answer
The first answer uses homonymy: 'opposition' is said differently for terms (substrate) and propositions (existence). Understand the distinction between truth of a thing and truth in itself.
This parallels other relations like generality and particularity.
3
Examine the second answer
The second answer generalizes opposition of affirmation and negation to include both simple and composite cases, treating the subject of a proposition as a 'locus' or 'substrate' for the predicate.
This aligns with the approach of verifying philosophers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : من التقابل ما يجري في القضايا كالتناقض والتضاد فإن قولنا : كل حيوان إنسان نقيض لقولنا بعض الحيوان ليس بإنسان، وضد لقولنا لا شيء من الحيوان بإنسان، مع أنه لا يتصور اعتبار ورود القضايا على محل . فالجواب من وجهين : أحدهما أن ذلك بحسب اشتراك الاسم كسائر النسب من العموم والخصوص، والمباينة والمساواة ، فإنها تكون في المفردات باعتبار الصدق، أعني صدقها على شيء وفي القضايا باعتبار الوجود أعني صدقها في أنفسها، فالمعتبر في التناقض والتضاد بين المفردين امتناع الاجتماع في المحل، وبين القضيتين امتناع لا اجتماع في الوجود . وثانيهما: أن يجعل تقابل الإيجاب والسلب أعمم مما في المفردات والقضايا، ويعتبر موضوع القضية مورداً ومحلاً لثبوت المحمول له، وعدم الثبوت على ما قال المحققون من الحكماء : أن المتقابلين بالإيجاب والسلب، إن لم يحتمل الصدق والكذب فبسيط كالفرسية واللافرسية وإلا فمركب كقولنا زيد
If it is said: Among opposition is that which occurs in propositions, like contradiction and contrariety, for our statement 'Every animal is a human' is the contradictory of our statement 'Some animal is not human', and the contrary of our statement 'No animal is human', while it is inconceivable to consider the occurrence of propositions upon a substrate. Then the answer is from two aspects: One of them: that this is due to homonymy like the other relations of generality and particularity, disparity and equality. For they occur in terms by consideration of truth, i.e., their truth of something, and in propositions by consideration of existence, i.e., their truth in themselves. So what is considered in contradiction and contrariety between two terms is the impossibility of coexistence in a substrate, and between two propositions is the impossibility of coexistence in existence. And the second: that the opposition of affirmation and negation is made more general than what is in terms and propositions, and the subject of the proposition is considered a locus (mawrid) and a substrate (maḥall) for the affirmation of the predicate to it, and the non-affirmation, according to what the verifyers among the philosophers said: that the two opposites by affirmation and negation, if they do not admit truth and falsehood, then they are simple like horseness and non-horseness, and if they do, then composite like our saying 'Zayd'
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage handles an objection about whether opposition can apply to propositions, since propositions cannot 'occur' upon a physical substrate. The objector gives examples: 'Every animal is a human' is the contradictory of 'Some animal is not human', and the contrary of 'No animal is human'. Yet propositions are not located in a substrate. The answer has two parts. The first part explains that the term 'opposition' is used homonymously for terms and propositions. In terms, opposition concerns truth of a thing (ṣidq ʿalā shayʾ), while in propositions it concerns truth in themselves (ṣidq fī anfusihā). For terms, the condition is impossibility of coexistence in a substrate; for propositions, it is impossibility of coexistence in existence. The second part offers a more unified approach: consider the opposition of affirmation and negation in a broader sense that includes both simple terms (like horseness/non-horseness) and complex propositions. The subject of a proposition is treated as a 'locus' (mawrid) or 'substrate' (maḥall) for the affirmation or negation of the predicate. Thus, the same condition can be applied if we reinterpret the substrate as the subject of the proposition. The passage ends mid-sentence, noting the distinction between simple and composite opposites.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection proves that propositions cannot truly be opposites because they lack a substrate.
Correction: The answer shows that propositions can be opposites, but the condition for their opposition is coexistence in existence, not in a physical substrate.

Checkpoint
According to the first aspect of the answer, what is considered in the opposition of two propositions?
A) Impossibility of coexistence in a substrate.
B) Impossibility of coexistence in existence.
C) Impossibility of coexistence in truth value.
D) Impossibility of coexistence in meaning.

Key Takeaway
Opposition in propositions is valid but its condition is coexistence in existence, not in a physical substrate.

?

Third and Fourth Points on Opposites: Same Subject and Non-Black
Two additional arguments on the subject of opposites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two additional arguments about how opposites can share the same subject and why non-black/non-white are not mere negations but refer to the same subject as their positive counterparts.

Core Idea
Opposites can share the same subject; non-black is not mere non-existence.

The author advances two more considerations to refine our understanding of opposites. First, he cites Ibn Sina to show the variety of ways the subject of opposites can be identical. Second, he clarifies that terms like 'non-black' refer to real qualities inhering in the same subject as 'black', not to the absence of blackness. These points reinforce the underlying unity of the subject in opposite pairs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once to get an overview of the two points. Then read the English translation carefully.
Notice the footnotes in the Arabic text referencing 'non-existence'.
2
Analyze Ibn Sina's four examples
Identify the four types of identical subject: personal, specific, generic, and more general. For each, note the example given (Zayd, man, animal, thing).
Think about why each example fits its category.
3
Distinguish non-black from non-existence
The fourth point argues that 'non-black' is not the same as 'non-existence of black'. Understand that non-black is a quality that exists in the same subject as black.
The rhetorical question 'Do you not see...' emphasizes the logical point.
4
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider how these two points support the author's goal of establishing a unified subject for opposite pairs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما ثالثاً : فلتصريح ابن سينا وغيره بأن موضوع المتقابلين قد يكون واحداً شخصياً كزيد للعدل والجور أو نوعياً كالإنسان للرجولية والمرئية، أو جنسياً كالحيوان للذكورة والأنوثة، أو أعم من ذلك كالشيء للخير والشر. وأما رابعاً : فلأن الكلام في اللاسواد واللابياض لا في العدم (۱) المضاف (۲) إلى السواد، والعدم المضاف إلى البياض، ألا ترى أنك لا تقول باختلاف الموضوع في البياض واللابياض، نظراً إلى أن اللابياض عدم مضاف (۳) إلى البياض فيكون موضوعه البياض .
And as for the third: because of Ibn Sina's and others' explicit statement that the subject of opposites may be one in person, like Zayd for justice and injustice, or in species, like man for masculinity and femininity, or in genus, like animal for maleness and femaleness, or more general than that, like thing for good and evil. And as for the fourth: because the discourse is about non-black and non-white, not about the non-existence (1) attributed (2) to blackness and the non-existence attributed to whiteness. Do you not see that you do not say that the subject differs in whiteness and non-whiteness, considering that non-whiteness is a non-existence attributed (3) to whiteness, so its subject is whiteness.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage presents the third and fourth arguments regarding the subject of opposites. The author is building on earlier points. The third argument cites Ibn Sina to show that the subject (maḍūʿ) of opposites can be identical in various ways: personally (like Zayd is subject for both justice and injustice), specifically, generically, or more generally. The fourth argument deals with the terms 'non-black' and 'non-white'. The point is that when we speak of non-black, we are not speaking about the non-existence of black as an attribute, but about the thing that lacks blackness, whose subject is the same as the subject of blackness (i.e., the substance). So the subject of the opposite pairs (like black and non-black) is the same. The rhetorical question 'Do you not see...' emphasizes this.

Opposition in Propositions: Objection and Answer
Objection about opposition in propositions with a two-aspect answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection that propositions lack a locus is resolved through two distinct aspects: homonymy and a generalized view of opposition of affirmation and negation.

Core Idea
Opposition in propositions works analogously to terms with a shift from truth to existence.

The objector raises a problem: logical relations like contradiction and contrariety seem to apply to propositions, yet propositions lack a single subject or locus like terms do. How can two propositions be opposed without a common subject? The answer provides two complementary perspectives that clarify the nature of propositional opposition.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand the problem: the objector claims that opposition occurs in propositions (e.g., contradiction, contrariety), but propositions have no single locus (maḥall) like terms do. Why is this a problem?
2
Analyze the first aspect of the answer
The first answer uses homonymy: the same terms (like generality, particularity) apply differently to terms and propositions. For terms, they consider truth (ṣidq) of being true of something; for propositions, they consider existence (wujūd) of being true in themselves. Thus, opposition between terms involves impossibility of coexistence in the locus, while between propositions it involves impossibility of coexistence in existence.
This aspect distinguishes the two domains by shifting the criterion.
3
Examine the second aspect of the answer
The second aspect generalizes opposition of affirmation and negation to cover both terms and propositions. It treats the subject of the proposition as the locus for affirming or negating the predicate. The verifying philosophers further distinguish simple opposition (no truth value, e.g., horseness and non-horseness) from composite opposition (involving truth and falsity, e.g., 'Zayd is a horse' vs 'Zayd is not a horse').

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : من التقابل ما يجري في القضايا كالتناقض والتضاد فإن قولنا : كل حيوان إنسان نقيض لقولنا بعض الحيوان ليس بإنسان، وضد لقولنا لا شيء من الحيوان بإنسان، مع أنه لا يتصور اعتبار ورود القضايا على محل . فالجواب من وجهين : أحدهما أن ذلك بحسب اشتراك الاسم كسائر النسب من العموم والخصوص، والمباينة والمساواة (٥) ، فإنها تكون في المفردات باعتبار الصدق، أعني صدقها على شيء وفي القضايا باعتبار الوجود أعني صدقها في أنفسها، فالمعتبر في التناقض والتضاد بين المفردين امتناع الاجتماع في المحل، وبين القضيتين امتناع لا اجتماع في الوجود . وثانيهما: أن يجعل تقابل الإيجاب والسلب أعم مما في المفردات والقضايا، ويعتبر موضوع القضية مورداً ومحلاً لثبوت المحمول له، وعدم الثبوت على ما قال المحققون من الحكماء : أن المتقابلين بالإيجاب والسلب، إن لم يحتمل الصدق والكذب فبسيط كالفرسية واللافرسية وإلا فمركب كقولنا زيد فرس، وزيد ليس بفرس فإن إطلاق هذين المعنيين على موضوع واحد في زمان واحد محال .
For it is said: Among opposition is what occurs in propositions, like contradiction and contrariety. For our statement 'Every animal is a man' is contradictory to 'Some animal is not a man', and contrary to 'No animal is a man', while it is inconceivable that propositions are considered to have a locus. The answer is from two aspects. One: that is by homonymy, like the other relations of generality and particularity, distinctness and equality (5), for they occur in terms by truth—i.e., their being true of something—and in propositions by existence—i.e., their being true in themselves. So what is considered in contradiction and contrariety between two terms is impossibility of coexistence in the locus, and between two propositions is impossibility of coexistence in existence. The second: that the opposition of affirmation and negation is made more general than what occurs in terms and propositions, and the subject of the proposition is considered a locus for the affirmation of the predicate to it and its non-affirmation, according to what the verifying philosophers said: that the two opposites by affirmation and negation, if they do not admit truth and falsity, are simple, like horseness and non-horseness; otherwise they are composite, like our statement 'Zayd is a horse' and 'Zayd is not a horse.' Indeed, applying these two meanings to a single subject at a single time is impossible.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This is an objection and answer. The objector points out that opposition also occurs in propositions (qaḍāyā), such as contradiction (tanāquḍ) and contrariety (taḍādd). For example, 'Every animal is a man' is contradictory to 'Some animal is not a man', and contrary to 'No animal is a man'. The problem is that propositions seem not to have a single locus (maḥall) like terms do. The answer has two aspects. First, the use of terms like generality etc. is by homonymy: in terms, they apply by truth (ṣidq) to something, while in propositions they apply by existence in themselves. So the opposition between terms involves impossibility of coexistence in the locus, while between propositions it involves impossibility of coexistence in existence. Second, we can consider opposition of affirmation and negation more generally to include both terms and propositions. In this view, the subject of the proposition is the locus for the predicate's affirmation or negation. The verifying philosophers explain that such opposition can be simple (like horseness and non-horseness, where there is no truth value) or composite (like 'Zayd is a horse' and 'Zayd is not a horse', where truth and falsity are involved). Both cannot apply to one subject at one time.

Key Terms
تقابل الإيجاب والسلب
تناقض
تضاد
قضية
اشتراك الاسم
صدق
وجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Opposition in propositions is fundamentally different from opposition in terms and has no analogy.
Correction: It is analogous with a shift from truth to existence (first aspect) or unified under a more general concept of affirmation/negation (second aspect).

Checkpoint
In the answer to the objection about opposition in propositions, what is the distinction between the first and second aspect?
A) First aspect uses homonymy of terms; second considers general opposition of affirmation and negation.
B) First aspect considers the subject of the proposition as locus; second uses homonymy.
C) First aspect discusses simple vs composite; second discusses truth and falsity.
D) Both aspects are the same.

Key Takeaway
Propositional opposition is analogous to term opposition but considered in terms of existence rather than truth, or unified under a broader notion of affirmation/negation.

Affirmation and Negation: The Basic Logical Opposites
Ibn Sina defines the two basic logical opposites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's definitions of affirmation and negation as the most fundamental logical opposites.

Core Idea
Affirmation means existence; negation means non-existence in any sense.

In logic, the most basic opposition is between affirmation and negation. Ibn Sina, the great philosopher, provides a simple but precise definition that covers both essential and accidental existence. This lesson will clarify these fundamental terms.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Note that the passage is a short definition from Ibn Sina.
The passage begins with the heading 'Negation' (al-salb) which is part of the definition.
2
Understand affirmation
Affirmation (ījāb) is defined as existence in any sense, whether in itself or for another. Think about what 'existence in itself' (essential) and 'existence for another' (accidental) mean.
This covers both necessary and contingent existence.
3
Understand negation
Negation (salb) is defined as non-existence in any sense, whether in itself or for another. This is the opposite of affirmation.
Negation is the absence of existence, not just non-existence in the subject.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السلب
وقال ابن سينا : إن من التقابل الإيجاب والسلب ومعنى الإيجاب وجود أي كان، سواء كان باعتبار وجوده في نفسه أو وجوده لغيره، ومعنى لا وجود بأي معنى كان، سواء كان لا وجوده في ذاته، أو لا وجوده في غيره .
معنى
التضاد الحقيقي
(وقد يعتبر (۱) في التضاد (۲) غاية الخلاف (۳) ويسمى بالتضاد الحقيقي، والأول بالمشهوري () وفي الملكة والعدم استعداد المحل للوجود في ذلك الوقت، ويخص باسم المشهورى والأول بالحقيقي، وأول كل أعم (۷)، وباعتباره يدخل في أقسام التقابل تقابل مثل البياض مع الصفرة، والبصر مع عدمه من الشجر إلا أنهم صرحوا بأن أحد الضدين في المشهوري قد يكون عدماً للآخر، كالسكون للحركة والظلمة للنور والمرض للصحة والعجمة للنطق والأنوثة للذكورة والفردية للزوجية، وإن غاية الخلاف شرط في المشهوري أيضاً).
Negation
Ibn Sina said: Among opposition is affirmation and negation. The meaning of affirmation is existence, however it may be, whether it is by virtue of its existence in itself or its existence for another, and the meaning of negation is non-existence, in any sense, whether it is its non-existence in itself or its non-existence in another.
Meaning
Real Contrariety
(And in contrariety (1) extreme difference (2) may be considered (3) and it is called real contrariety, and the first (contrariety without that condition) is called popular contrariety (). And in possession and privation, the subject's preparedness for existence at that time is considered, and it is specifically called by the popular name, and the first by the real; and each first is more general (7). And by virtue of it, an opposition enters among the divisions of opposition like the opposition of whiteness and yellowness, and sight with its absence from a tree. However, they explicitly stated that in popular contrariety, one of the two contraries may be the non-existence of the other, like rest for motion, darkness for light, illness for health, stammering for speech, femaleness for maleness, and oddness for evenness. And extreme difference is also a condition in popular contrariety.)
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage begins with a heading 'Negation' (al-salb) and then quotes Ibn Sina's definition of affirmation and negation. Affirmation is existence in any sense, whether self-existent or existent for another. Negation is non-existence in any sense. Then the author turns to contrariety (taḍādd). He explains that sometimes extreme difference (ghāyat al-khilāf) is considered in contrariety, and this is called real contrariety (al-taḍādd al-ḥaqīqī), like black and white. The first kind (without that condition) is called popular contrariety (al-taḍādd al-mashhūrī). He also discusses possession and privation (al-malaka wa al-ʿadam), where the subject's preparedness for existence at that time is considered; this division is called by the popular name (i.e., popular contrariety) while the first is real. Each first is more general than its counterpart. Because of this, some oppositions enter that are not strictly contraries, e.g., whiteness and yellowness (not extreme difference), or sight and its absence in a tree (possession and privation). Yet they explicitly state that in popular contrariety one opposite may be the privation of the other, like rest for motion, darkness for light, etc. And extreme difference is also a condition in popular contrariety (i.e., both are contraries but differ in kind). So the classification is nuanced.

Key Terms
إيجاب
سلب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Negation only means non-existence in the subject itself.
Correction: Ibn Sina explicitly includes non-existence in another, as shown in the definition: 'whether it is its non-existence in itself or its non-existence in another.'

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Sina, what is the meaning of affirmation?
A) Existence in itself only
B) Non-existence in any sense
C) Existence in any sense
D) Possibility

Key Takeaway
Affirmation and negation are defined as existence and non-existence in any sense, covering all modes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you examine your own character, do you only affirm the existence of virtues in your private self, or do you also consider whether those virtues exist for others? In what relationships are you failing to manifest goodness that you claim to possess?
?
What non-existence in yourself are you avoiding acknowledging? Not just what you lack in essence, but what you fail to provide for others? Confront one concrete absence that you have been neglecting.
?
If affirmation and negation are opposites that cover every sense of existence, then your life is an ongoing interplay of what is and what is not. What affirmation of truth or justice are you withholding from the world right now? What negation of falsehood or injustice are you refusing to enact?

Real and Popular Contrariety
Author explains real and popular contrariety with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between real contrariety (based on extreme difference) and popular contrariety (including possession and privation).

Core Idea
Real contrariety requires extreme difference; popular contrariety does not.

After understanding the basic logical opposites, we now turn to contrariety, which can be of two types: real, where the two opposites are at extremes, and popular, which is broader. This lesson will explain this classification and its subtypes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on contrariety
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the discussion of real vs popular and the examples.
The passage has many parentheses and numbers; they are editorial footnotes.
2
Identify the two types of contrariety
Real contrariety is defined by considering extreme difference (e.g., black and white). Popular contrariety is without that condition (e.g., whiteness and yellowness).
The passage also mentions possession and privation as a subtype of popular contrariety.
3
Understand possession and privation
Possession and privation is a type of popular contrariety where one opposite is the non-existence of the other in a subject prepared to receive it. Examples: sight and blindness, motion and rest.
The passage clarifies that in popular contrariety, one opposite may be the privation of the other.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is an example of popular contrariety?
A) Black and white
B) Whiteness and yellowness
C) Motion and rest
D) Sight and blindness

Key Takeaway
Contrariety is divided into real (extreme difference) and popular (broader, including possession and privation).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time you considered two things as opposites but they actually had a middle ground. How does the distinction between real and popular contrariety help you think more precisely about conflicts in your life? What specific step can you take this week to avoid oversimplifying such oppositions?
?
The passage mentions that in popular contrariety, one opposite may be the non-existence of the other, like rest is the absence of motion. In what area of your life do you mistakenly treat an absence (e.g., lack of anger) as a positive opposite rather than a privation? How does recognizing this affect your spiritual growth? What will you change today?

Logic and Technical Terms
beginner
4 min
Ibn Sina's Definition of Ijab and Salab as Opposition
Defines Ijab and Salab as a type of opposition with Ibn Sina's wording.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's definition of affirmation (ījāb) and negation (salab) as a type of opposition.

Core Idea
Affirmation and negation are opposites defined by existence or non-existence in any sense.

This passage introduces a key logical concept from Ibn Sina: the opposition between affirmation and negation. It provides a concrete example and then defines each term precisely. Understanding this distinction is foundational for classical logic and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it, then read the literal translation. Notice the example and the definitions.
2
Identify the example
Find the example (horse, Zayd is not a horse) and understand why it illustrates opposition: the two meanings cannot apply to the same subject at the same time.
3
Compare the definitions of Ijab and Salab
Notice that both definitions use 'in any sense' and cover existence/non-existence in itself and for something else. Reflect on the comprehensiveness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فرس، وزيد ليس بفرس فإن إطلاق هذين المعنيين على موضوع واحد في زمان واحد محال . السلب وقال ابن سينا : إن من التقابل الإيجاب والسلب ومعنى الإيجاب وجود أي كان، سواء كان باعتبار وجوده في نفسه أو وجوده لغيره، ومعنى لا وجود بأي معنى كان، سواء كان لا وجوده في ذاته، أو لا وجوده في غيره .
Horse, and Zayd is not a horse, for applying these two meanings to one subject at one time is impossible. Negation (al-salab). And Ibn Sina said: Indeed, among the types of opposition is affirmation (al-ījāb) and negation (al-salab). The meaning of affirmation is existence in any sense, whether considered as existence in itself or existence for something else. The meaning of negation is non-existence in any sense, whether it is non-existence in its essence or non-existence in something else.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage defines the opposition of affirmation (ījāb) and negation (salab) as a type of opposition (taqābul). It first gives an example: "horse, and Zayd is not a horse" – applying these two meanings (being a horse and not being a horse) to one subject at one time is impossible, illustrating contradiction. The term "al-salab" appears as a heading or indicator. Then it quotes Ibn Sīnā: this is a type of opposition called ījāb and salab. Ījāb means existence (wujūd) in any sense, whether existence in itself or existence for something else. Salab means non-existence (lā wujūd) in any sense, whether non-existence in its essence or non-existence in something else. The definition covers both essential and relational existence.

إيجاب
سلب
تقابل

عدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Sina, what does 'ījāb' mean?
A) Non-existence in any sense
B) Existence in any sense
C) A type of contrariety
D) The impossibility of applying two meanings

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina defines affirmation as existence in any sense and negation as non-existence in any sense, forming a fundamental opposition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage teaches that affirmation and negation cannot be true of the same subject at the same time. Is there any area in your life where you verbally affirm faith in Allah yet your actions deny it? Today, identify one such contradiction and resolve to align your action with your affirmation.
?
Ibn Sina defines negation as non-existence in any sense. Have you ever mentally negated an attribute of Allah, such as His mercy or power, when facing hardship? Repent from that negation and affirm His attribute consciously.
?
The source uses the example 'Zayd is a horse' and 'Zayd is not a horse' to show impossibility. In your daily life, are there beliefs you hold that contradict each other, such as believing that Allah controls everything yet acting as if your efforts alone determine success? How will you replace that negation with pure affirmation of tawheed?
